Miracles
beginner
4 min
Definition of the Miracle (Mu'jiza) and Its Conditions
Defines mu‘jiza, its linguistic origin, and technical conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of a mu‘jiza, its linguistic roots, and the conditions that distinguish it from other extraordinary events.

Core Idea
A true miracle is an extraordinary event with challenge and no opposition.

Before we can understand prophethood or karamat, we must know what makes an event a miracle. The scholars defined it precisely to avoid confusion with sorcery or saintly acts. This passage presents the agreed-upon definition, its linguistic origins, and the key conditions that set it apart.

Guided Path
1
Read the main definition
Start with the first sentence: a miracle is an extraordinary event with two key conditions. Then read the alternative definitions that follow.
Notice the variation: some add concurrency with the time of obligation.
2
Understand the etymology
Examine how the word mu‘jiza derives from ‘ajz (inability) and how it acquired its technical meaning through metaphorical transfer.
Imam al-Haramayn’s Ash‘ari analysis adds a layer of nuance.
3
Identify the conditions
List the three conditions: extraordinary, accompanied by challenge, and no opposition. See how each condition excludes other types of events.
The condition of challenge excludes karāmāt and irhāṣāt.
4
See what the definition excludes
The passage explicitly rules out saintly miracles, sorcery, jugglery, and the prophet's own earlier signs.
This protects the distinctive nature of a prophetic miracle.
5
Review the concluding summary
Imam al-Razi’s summary confirms the definition and conditions. Ensure you can restate them from memory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : المبحث الثاني المعجزة: أمر خارق العادة مقرون بالتحدي وعدم المعارضة، وقيل: أمر قصد به إظهار صدق من ادعى النبوة والرسالة، وزاد بعضهم قيد موافقة الدعوى. و بعضهم مقارنة زمن التكليف إذ عند انقراضه تظهر الخوارق، لا لقصد التصديق). مأخوذ من العجز المقابل للقدرة، وحقيقة الإعجاز إثبات العجز استعير لإظهاره، ثم أسند مجازاً إلى ما هو سبب العجز ، وجعل اسماً له، فالتاء للنقل من الوصفية إلى الاسمية كما في الحقيقة وقيل للمبالغة كما في العلامة . وذكر إمام الحرمين بناء على رأي الأشعري (١) أن ههنا تجوزاً آخر، هو استعمال العجز في عدم القدرة كالجهل في عدم العلم، وهو في الحقيقة ضد للقدرة، وإنما يتعلق بالموجود، وبما يقدر عليه حتى إن عجز الزمن إنما هو عن القعود بمعنى وجد منه اضطراراً لا اختياراً . فلو تحقق العجز عن المعارضة لوجبت المعارضة الاضطرارية، والمعجزة في العرف أمر خارق للعادة، مقرون بالتحدي مع عدم المعارضة، وإنما قال: أمر ليتناول الفعل كانفجار الماء من بين الأصابع وعدمه، كعدم إحراق النار، ومن اقتصر على الفعل، جعل المعجز ههنا كون النار برداً وسلاماً (۲) و بقاء الجسم على ما كان عليه من غير احتراق، واحترز بقيد المقارنة للتحدي عن كرامات الأولياء والعلامات الإرهاصية التي تتقدم بعثة الأنبياء، وعن أن يتخذ الكاذب معجزة من مضى من الأنبياء حجة لنفسه، وبقيد عدم المعارضة عن السحر والشعبذة، كذا ذكره الإمام الرازي (٣) ،
He said: The Second Topic: The Miracle: An extraordinary event, accompanied by challenge (tahaddi) and absence of impediment. And it was said: An event intended to manifest the truthfulness of one who claims prophethood and messengership. And some added the condition of conformity to the claim. And some [added] the condition of being concurrent with the time of obligation, because at its expiration extraordinary events appear, not for the purpose of verification. It is derived from 'ajz (inability) which is opposite to qudra (power). The reality of i'jaz is the establishment of inability; it was borrowed for its manifestation, then it was assigned metaphorically to that which is the cause of inability, and it was made a name for it. So the tā' indicates transfer from adjective-hood to noun-hood as in 'al-ḥaqīqa', and it is said for hyperbole as in 'al-ʿalāma'. And Imām al-Ḥaramayn mentioned, based on the view of al-Ashʿarī (1), that here there is another metaphor: the use of 'ajz for absence of power, like jahl for absence of knowledge, while in reality it is the opposite of power, and it pertains only to the existent and to that over which one has power, such that the inability of the old man is only to sit, meaning it occurs from him compulsorily, not voluntarily. So if inability to oppose were realized, the compulsory opposition would be necessary. And the miracle in custom is an extraordinary event, accompanied by challenge with absence of impediment. And he said 'an event' to include the act, like the bursting forth of water from between the fingers, and its absence, like the fire not burning; and whoever restricts it to the act makes the miracle here the fact that the fire became cool and safe (2) and the body remaining as it was without burning. And by the condition of being concurrent with the challenge, he avoided the miracles (karāmāt) of the saints and the precursory signs (irhāṣāt) that precede the sending of prophets, and [avoided] that a liar should take as an argument for himself the miracle of a past prophet. And by the condition of absence of impediment, he avoided sorcery and sleight-of-hand. Thus mentioned it by Imām al-Rāzī (3).
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage defines the miracle (mu'jiza) and explains its conditions. It begins by stating that a miracle is an extraordinary event accompanied by challenge (tahaddi) and absence of impediment. Variant definitions are then given: some say it is an event intended to manifest the truthfulness of a claimant to prophethood; others add the condition of conformity with the claim, or that it be concurrent with the time of obligation. The passage then delves into the etymology of 'mu'jiza' from 'ajz (inability), explaining that i'jaz means establishing inability, and the word is used metaphorically. Imām al-Ḥaramayn's view is cited: a further metaphor where 'ajz is used for absence of power. The definition returns to the technical meaning: an extraordinary event with challenge and no opposition. The term 'event' includes both actions (like water flowing) and their absence (like fire not burning). The condition of being concurrent with the challenge excludes the saintly miracles (karāmāt) that precede prophethood, and prevents a liar from claiming a previous prophet's miracle. The condition of absence of impediment excludes sorcery and jugglery. This is the definition as given by al-Rāzī.

Key Terms
معجزة
إعجاز
عجز
تحدي
خارق العادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any extraordinary event is a miracle.
Correction: A miracle must be accompanied by a challenge (tahaddi) and have no opposition. This excludes saintly miracles, sorcery, and precursory signs.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a condition for something to be considered a miracle (mu'jiza)?
A) Being extraordinary (khāriq al-ʿāda)
B) Being accompanied by a challenge (tahaddī)
C) Being performed by a saint (walī)
D) Having no opposition (ʿadam al-muʿāraḍa)

Key Takeaway
A miracle is an extraordinary event, accompanied by a challenge, and with no opposition, which distinguishes it from other marvels.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Quran is presented as a miracle with a direct challenge to produce a chapter like it. Have you ever truly accepted that challenge in your heart? What prevents you from responding with humility and submission?
?
The passage distinguishes a prophet's miracle from sorcery. In your daily life, are there things you marvel at that might be from God, or do you attribute them to other sources without reflection? How can you train yourself to see God's hand more clearly?
?
If a miracle is a sign of truth, what does your own response to the Quran's challenge say about your faith? Are you content to admire it from a distance, or will you let it transform you?

Miracles
intermediate

First Objection to the Definition of Mu'jiza and Its Answer
Raises the objection that the definition lacks conditions of appearance by the claimant, and answers that challenge implies them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific objection that the definition of mu'jiza lacks conditions of appearance from the claimant and conformity, and how the condition of challenge (tahaddī) implicitly covers both.

Core Idea
Challenge (tahaddī) is the key that ties a miracle to its claimant.

The first objection to the standard definition of mu'jiza is that it fails to require that the miracle must appear at the hand of the claimant himself and must conform to his claim. Without these, a liar could misuse a prophet's miracle. The answer, however, is that the condition of challenge (tahaddī) already includes both requirements. This lesson unpacks that objection and its resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its two conditions
The objector argues that the definition must include two conditions: appearance from the claimant and conformity to the claim. Read this part carefully.
The objection is introduced with 'أما :أولاً'.
2
Understand the answer: challenge implies both
The answer says that the word 'tahaddī' (challenge) already indicates both conditions. Note how the author explains the meaning of tahaddī and how it connects the miracle to the claim.
The key phrase is 'ذكر التحدي مشعر بالقيدين'.
3
Examine the examples
The passage gives examples of a silent claimant and a liar claiming past events. See how the lack of challenge invalidates the miracle claim in these cases.
A sign without challenge is not a miracle.
4
Reflect on the implication
The outcome is that miracles are tied to the prophet who issues the challenge, not to others. This ensures the link between miracle and claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما :أولاً : فلأنه لا بد من قيد الظهور على يد المدعي، ومن جهته احترازاً عن أن يتخذ الكاذب معجزة من يعاصره من الأنبياء حجة لنفسه، وعن ان يقول: معجزتي ما ظهر مني في السنين الماضية، فقد صرحوا بأنه لا عبرة بذلك، ومن قيد الموافقة للدعوى احترازاً عما إذا قال : معجزتي نطق هذا الجماد. فنطق بأنه مفتر کذاب. ولهذا قال الشيخ أبو الحسن : هي فعل من الله تعالى، أو قائم مقام الفعل. يقصد بمثله التصديق، وقال بعض الأصحاب : هي أمر قصد به إظهار صدق من ادعى الرسالة . ويمكن الجواب عن الأول بأن ذكر التحدي مشعر بالقيدين. فإن معناه طلب المعارضة فيما جعله شاهداً لدعوته، وتعجيز الغير عن الإتيان بمثل ما أبداه . تقول : تحديت فلاناً إذا باريته الفعل، ونازعته الغلبة، وتحديته القراءة أينا أقرأ . وبالتحدي يحصل ربط الدعوى بالمعجزة حتى لو ظهرت آية من شخص وهو ساكت لم يكن معجزة وكذا لو ادعي الرسالة، فظهرت الآية من غير إشعار منه بالتحدي . قالوا : ويكفي في التحدي أن يقول : آية صدقي أن يكون كذا وكذا . ولا يحتاج إلى أن يقول : هذه آيتي ولا يأتي أحد بمثلها، فعلى هذا لا تكون معجزة نبي ماض ، ولا معاصر معجزة للغير.
As for the first [objection]: because there must be the condition of appearance at the hand of the claimant, and from his direction, as a precaution against a liar taking the miracle of a contemporary prophet as a proof for himself, and against him saying: 'My miracle is what appeared from me in past years,' for they stated that there is no consideration for that. And from the condition of conformity to the claim as a precaution against when he says: 'My miracle is the speech of this inanimate object,' and it speaks saying that he is a liar. And for this reason Shaykh Abū al-Ḥasan said: It is an act of God most high, or standing in place of an act, intended by it to produce belief. And some of the companions said: It is an event intended to manifest the truthfulness of one who claims the message. And the answer to the first is that the mention of challenge (tahaddī) indicates both conditions. For its meaning is demanding opposition regarding what he made a witness for his claim, and rendering others incapable of bringing the like of what he produced. You say: 'I challenged so-and-so' when you vie with him in action and contend for victory, and 'I challenged him in reading' as to which of us reads better. And by challenge, the connection of the claim to the miracle is established, such that even if a sign appears from a person while he is silent, it is not a miracle; similarly if he claims prophethood and the sign appears without his indicating challenge. They said: It suffices in challenge that he say: 'The proof of my truthfulness is that such-and-such occurs.' And he does not need to say: 'This is my miracle and no one can bring its like.' On this basis, the miracle of a past prophet is not a miracle for another, nor is a contemporary's miracle for someone else.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection to the definition of mu'jiza and its answer. The objector argues that the definition lacks two important conditions: (1) that the miracle be manifested at the hand of the claimant and from his direction (to prevent a liar from claiming the miracle of a contemporary prophet, or claiming past events as his own miracle), and (2) that it conform to the claim (to avoid a case where the claimant says the miracle is an inanimate object speaking, but the object speaks to denounce him). The objector cites Shaykh Abū al-Ḥasan (al-Ashʿarī) and some companions who emphasize that the miracle is intended to produce belief. The answer is that the condition of challenge (tahaddī) already implies both conditions. The word 'tahaddī' means demanding opposition and rendering others incapable. By issuing a challenge, the claimant links the miracle to his claim. Even if a sign appears without an explicit challenge, it is not a miracle. A simple statement like 'the proof of my truthfulness is that such-and-such happens' suffices as a challenge. Therefore, the miracle of a past prophet is not attributable to another, nor does a miracle from a contemporary belong to someone else.

Key Terms
تحدي
مدعي
ظهور
موافقة الدعوى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the condition of challenge is separate from the condition of appearance from the claimant.
Correction: The answer shows that challenge implicitly includes both appearance and conformity, so no separate conditions are needed.

Checkpoint
How does the author answer the objection that the definition of mu'jiza lacks the condition that the miracle must appear from the claimant?
A) By adding a separate condition of appearance
B) By stating that the condition of challenge (tahaddī) already implies both appearance and conformity
C) By rejecting the objection as irrelevant
D) By redefining the term 'mu'jiza'

Key Takeaway
The condition of challenge (tahaddī) in the definition of mu'jiza inherently requires that the miracle appear at the hand of the claimant and conform to his claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever expected a sign from Allah to confirm a belief or action you were attached to, without first checking whether that sign was linked to a clear prophetic claim? What does this lesson teach you about demanding evidence on your own terms?
?
When you witness something extraordinary in your life or in the world, do you automatically assume it is a divine stamp of approval—or do you pause to ask: 'Does this point directly to the truth of the message of Islam?' Where does your heart default, and what must change?
?
The condition of tahaddī forces a connection between a miracle and a specific claim of prophethood. In your own life, do you hold yourself accountable to the same standard—ensuring that your actions and words are consistently aligned with the truth you profess? If a sign were to appear through you, would it confirm or contradict your claim to be a follower of Muhammad?

Miracles
beginner
5 min
First Condition of Miracle: Appearance and Consistency with Claim
Explains the first condition of miracle and gives definitions from al-Ash'ari and others.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a miracle must appear from the claimant of prophethood and match his claim, and how the concept of challenge (tahaddi) encompasses both conditions.

Core Idea
A miracle is valid only when tied to a prophetic claim and a challenge.

To determine if an event is a true miracle, scholars require it to appear from the one claiming prophethood and to be consistent with his claim. This prevents misuse of miracles and ensures they serve as divine attestation. The passage below explains these conditions, presents definitions from major theologians, and clarifies how the notion of challenge (tahaddi) inherently covers both requirements.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage carefully, then its literal translation. Notice the flow from conditions to definitions to the answer involving challenge.
Pay attention to the terms 'ظهور على يد المدعي' and 'موافقة للدعوى'.
2
Identify the two conditions and their rationale
Locate where the author states the necessity of appearance on the claimant's hand and consistency with the claim. Read the reasons given for each condition.
The conditions prevent false claims using genuine miracles and past events.
3
Understand the role of challenge (tahaddi)
Examine how the author argues that mentioning challenge already implies both conditions. Consider the linguistic examples of 'tahaddi' and how it connects the miracle to the claim.
Challenge means demanding opposition and incapacitating others.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما :أولاً : فلأنه لا بد من قيد الظهور على يد المدعي، ومن جهته احترازاً عن أن يتخذ الكاذب معجزة من يعاصره من الأنبياء حجة لنفسه، وعن ان يقول: معجزتي ما ظهر مني في السنين الماضية، فقد صرحوا بأنه لا عبرة بذلك، ومن قيد الموافقة للدعوى احترازاً عما إذا قال : معجزتي نطق هذا الجماد. فنطق بأنه مفتر کذاب. ولهذا قال الشيخ أبو الحسن : هي فعل من الله تعالى، أو قائم مقام الفعل. يقصد بمثله التصديق، وقال بعض الأصحاب : هي أمر قصد به إظهار صدق من ادعى الرسالة . ويمكن الجواب عن الأول بأن ذكر التحدي مشعر بالقيدين. فإن معناه طلب المعارضة فيما جعله شاهداً لدعوته، وتعجيز الغير عن الإتيان بمثل ما أبداه . تقول : تحديت فلاناً إذا باريته الفعل، ونازعته الغلبة، وتحديته القراءة أينا أقرأ . وبالتحدي يحصل ربط الدعوى بالمعجزة حتى لو ظهرت آية من شخص وهو ساكت لم يكن معجزة وكذا لو ادعي الرسالة، فظهرت الآية من غير إشعار منه بالتحدي . قالوا : ويكفي في التحدي أن يقول : آية صدقي أن يكون كذا وكذا . ولا يحتاج إلى أن يقول : هذه آيتي ولا يأتي أحد بمثلها، فعلى هذا لا تكون معجزة نبي ماض ، ولا معاصر معجزة للغير.
As for first: because it is necessary to have the condition of appearing on the hand of the claimant, and on his side, to avoid that the liar takes the miracle of a contemporary prophet as a proof for himself, and to avoid saying: 'My miracle is what appeared from me in past years,' for they have stated that there is no consideration for that. And the condition of matching the claim is to avoid when he says: 'My miracle is that this inanimate object speaks.' And it speaks that he is a lying fabricator. For this reason, Shaykh Abu al-Hasan said: 'It is an act of God, or something that stands in place of an act, intended thereby to give attestation.' And some of the associates said: 'It is a thing intended to show the truthfulness of one who claims prophethood.' And it is possible to answer the first by saying that the mention of challenge implies both conditions. For its meaning is the demand for opposition in what he makes a witness to his claim, and incapacitating others from bringing the like of what he has shown. You say: 'I challenged so-and-so' when you vie with him in action and contend for victory, and 'I challenged him in reading: which of us reads?' And by challenge, the connection of the claim to the miracle is established, so much so that if a sign appears from a person while he is silent, it is not a miracle; likewise if he claims prophethood and the sign appears without notification of challenge from him. They said: It suffices for challenge that he says: 'The sign of my truthfulness is that such-and-such occurs.' And he does not need to say: 'This is my sign and no one can bring its like.' Thus, on this basis, the miracle of a past prophet is not a miracle for another, nor is a contemporary's miracle a miracle for another.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage presents the first condition for a miracle (mu'jiza): that it appear from the claimant of prophethood and that it match his claim. It explains the rationale behind these conditions: to prevent someone from falsely using a miracle performed by a genuine prophet, and to prevent claiming past events as miracles. Then it quotes two definitions: from Shaykh Abu al-Hasan (al-Ash'ari) who defines a miracle as an act of God or something in place of an act intended for attestation, and from some associates (as-hab) who define it as something intended to show the truthfulness of the claimant. The passage then provides an answer to a potential objection: the inclusion of the condition of challenge (tahaddi) already covers both conditions—appearance and consistency—because challenge inherently demands a connection to the claim and incapacitation of others. The answer illustrates the meaning of challenge with linguistic examples and concludes that a silent appearance or a claim without challenge does not constitute a miracle.

Key Terms

ظهور على يد المدعي
موافقة للدعوى
الشيخ أبو الحسن
فعل من الله تعالى
قائم مقام الفعل
تحدي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any extraordinary event is a miracle.
Correction: A miracle must be tied to a prophetic claim and accompanied by a challenge; otherwise, it is not considered a miracle.

Checkpoint
What is the purpose of the condition that the miracle appears 'on the hand of the claimant' (ظهور على يد المدعي)?
A) To ensure the miracle is performed by a righteous person
B) To prevent a liar from using a miracle from a true prophet as his own proof
C) To limit miracles to prophets only
D) To require the miracle to be witnessed by many people

Key Takeaway
A miracle is authenticated only when it appears from the claimant of prophethood, matches his claim, and is accompanied by a challenge (tahaddi).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you evaluate a miracle, do you personally verify that it meets the conditions of appearing from a claimant and being consistent with his claim, or do you accept it without scrutiny? How can you apply this rigor to your own faith?
?
The challenge (tahaddi) ties the miracle to a specific claim. Have you ever considered that every proof of prophethood, including the Qur'an, carries an implicit challenge? What does that demand from you as a believer?
?
If a miracle from a past prophet cannot be used as evidence for a later claimant, then what responsibility do you have to examine the signs of the final Prophet with fresh eyes, unclouded by tradition? Will you commit to studying the evidences of Islam as if they were presented to you today?

The Principle of Counting Irhāṣāt as Miracles
Sanusi presents an objection and its answer regarding counting irhāṣāt as miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why pre-miraculous events are considered miracles by way of predominance (taghlīb) and similitude (tashbīh), not literally.

Core Idea
Pre-miraculous events are called miracles by a figurative extension, not in the strict sense.

The passage begins by presenting an objection: how can events that occur before prophethood, without a challenge, be counted as miracles? Sanusi answers by explaining that this counting is by way of taghlīb (predominance) and tashbīh (similitude). This lesson clarifies that principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part of the passage where Sanusi presents the objection against counting irhāṣāt as miracles.
Notice the examples given: cloud providing shade, greeting of stones.
2
Understand the answer
Read Sanusi's answer: these events are included in miracles by way of taghlīb and tashbīh.
Taghlīb means predominance, tashbīh means similitude.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this distinction matters: irhāṣāt are not true miracles in the full sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ثانياً : فلأن القوم عدوا من المعجزات ما هو متقدم غير مقرون بالتحدي، ولا مقصود به إظهار الصدق لعدم الدعوى حينئذ، كإظلال الغمام، وتسليم الحجر والمدر، ونحو ذلك . وعن الثاني أن عد الإرهاصات من جملة المعجزات إنما هو على سبيل التغليب والتشبيه والمحققون على أن خوارق العادات المتعلقة ببعثة النبي إذا كانت متقدمة. فإن ظهرت منه. فإن ،شاعت وكان هو مظنة البعثة كما في حق نبينا ( عليه السلام حيث أخبر بذلك بعض أهل الكتاب والكهنة . فإرهاص (١) أي تأسيس لقاعدة البعثة وإلا فكرامة محضة، وإن ظهرت من غيره، فإن كان من الأخيار، فكذلك أي إرهاص أو كرامة وإلا فإرهاص محض كظهور النور في جبين عبد الله ، أو ابتلاء كما إذا ظهرت على يد من ادعى الألوهية. فإن الأدلة القطعية قائمة على كذبه بخلاف مدعي النبوة. فلهذا جوزوا إظهارها على يد المتآله دون المتنبي. الإرهاص ما يظهر من الخوارق عن النبي - قبل ظهوره كالنور الذي كان في جبين آباء نبينا . وقيل : إحداث أمر خارق للعادة دال على بعثة نبي قبل بعثته :وقيل: هو ما يصدر عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قبل النبوة من أمر خارق للعادة قيل إنها من قبيل الكرامات، فإن الأنبياء قبل النبوة لا يقصرون عن درجة الأولياء .
And as for the second: because the people counted as miracles that which is prior, not accompanied by challenge, and not intended to show truthfulness due to the absence of a claim at that time, such as the cloud providing shade, the greeting of stones and clods, and the like. And the answer to the second is: that counting the irhāṣāt (pre-miraculous events) as part of miracles is only by way of predominance (taghlīb) and similitude (tashbīh). The verifiers hold that extraordinary events related to the mission of a prophet, if they occur prior, and if they become widespread and he is the locus of prophethood (as in the case of our Prophet, peace be upon him, where some People of the Book and soothsayers foretold that), then it is irhāṣ, i.e., a foundation for the basis of prophethood; otherwise it is a pure miracle (karāma). And if it appears from someone other than him, then if it is from the righteous, it is likewise either irhāṣ or karāma; otherwise it is pure irhāṣ, like the appearance of a light on the forehead of ʿAbd Allāh, or a trial (ibtilāʾ) as when it appears on the hand of one who claims divinity. For decisive proofs establish his falsehood, unlike the claimant of prophethood. For this reason they permitted its appearance on the hand of the one who claims divinity (al-mutaʾallih) but not on the hand of the false prophet (al-mutanabbī). Irhāṣ: what appears of extraordinary events from a prophet before his advent, like the light that was on the forehead of the fathers of our Prophet. And it is said: the bringing about of an extraordinary event indicating the mission of a prophet before his mission. And it is said: it is what issues from the Prophet, peace be upon him, before prophethood of an extraordinary event; it is said that it is of the type of miracles (karāmāt), for the prophets before prophethood are not lower than the rank of saints.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage addresses the second issue: the counting of pre-miraculous events (irhāṣāt) as miracles even though they occur before the prophetic claim and lack a challenge. The answer explains that this counting is by way of predominance (taghlīb) and similitude (tashbīh), not literal. The verifiers (muḥaqqiqūn) classify these events based on the person from whom they appear and the context. If they appear from a future prophet and are widespread and he is expected to be a prophet (maẓinna al-biʿtha), they are irhāṣ, serving as a foundation for his prophethood. If from a prophet but without the expected context, it is a pure miracle (karāma). If from a righteous person, it is either irhāṣ or karāma. If from a false claimant of divinity, it is a trial (ibtilāʾ). The passage then provides a definition of irhāṣ, clarifying its lexical and technical meanings. It also mentions that such events are allowed on the hand of a claimant of divinity (because decisive proofs prove his falsehood) but not on the hand of a false prophet (mutanabbī).

Key Terms
إرهاص
تغليب
تشبيه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Pre-miraculous events are literal miracles like those after prophethood.
Correction: They are considered miracles only by way of predominance and similitude, not in the strict sense.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why are events like the cloud shading the Prophet before prophethood counted as miracles?
A) Because they are literally miracles
B) By way of predominance and similitude
C) Because they are accompanied by a challenge
D) Because they prove truthfulness

Key Takeaway
Irhāṣāt are included among miracles only figuratively, not as true miracles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever labeled an event in your life as a 'miracle' when it might not have been one in the strict sense? What would change if you called it something else?
?
When you speak about God's favors, are you tempted to exaggerate or use figurative language to make them sound more powerful? How does this lesson call you to be truthful even in your praise?
?
If a blessing comes before a period of faith, does it lose its value? Does your heart honor the Giver regardless of whether it fits a formal category?

Classification of Pre-Miraculous Events (Irhāṣāt)
Sanusi classifies extraordinary events related to prophethood and defines irhāṣ.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how verifiers classify extraordinary events that occur before prophethood based on the person and context.

Core Idea
Whether an extraordinary event is irhāṣ, karāma, or trial depends on who it appears from and the circumstances.

After establishing that irhāṣāt are counted as miracles figuratively, the verifiers (muḥaqqiqūn) provide a detailed classification. This lesson walks through the four scenarios and defines irhāṣ.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification framework
Read the passage starting from 'Wa al-muḥaqqiqūn' to understand the four cases.
Focus on how the type changes based on the person and context.
2
Identify the four scenarios
List the four possibilities: from a future prophet (widespread vs. not), from righteous person, from false divine claimant.
3
Understand the definitions of irhāṣ
Read the three definitions provided at the end of the passage.
Note how they relate to the classification.
4
Reflect on the ruling for false prophets
Why are extraordinary events not allowed on the hand of a false prophet but allowed on a false divine claimant?
Because decisive proofs establish the latter's falsehood.

?

?

In your own life, do you sometimes mistake general blessings or small favors as signs that you are on a special mission from Allah? How can you distinguish between a general gift from Allah and a specific sign pointing to a responsibility He wants you to undertake?
?

What hidden preparations are you neglecting because you seek immediate recognition or miraculous confirmation? Commit today to one act of sincere devotion that no one else will see, trusting that Allah is building your foundation.

Delayed Miracle After Challenge
States that a miracle can be delayed after a challenge and gives an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that a miracle can be delayed after a challenge and the example used to illustrate this.

Core Idea
A miracle may be delayed, but it remains valid if it occurs as promised.

This passage addresses the third issue regarding miracles: whether a miracle must occur immediately after the challenge. The author argues that a delay is possible, and he provides a clear example. This sets the stage for more nuanced discussions about time and concurrence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic sentence and its translation to understand the basic claim and example.
Notice that the author uses 'ثالثاً' to indicate this is the third point.
2
Identify the main claim
The claim is that a miracle can be delayed after a challenge. This is a key principle in the theology of miracles.
3
Examine the example
The example: a prophet says 'My miracle is what will appear from me on such a day,' and it appears. This shows a delayed miracle is valid.
Think about why this was considered a valid challenge-response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ثالثاً : فلأن المعجزة قد تتأخر عن التخدي، كما إذا قال : معجزتي ما يظهر مني يوم كذا، فظهرت. وعن الثالث أن المتأخر، إن كان بزمان يسير يعد مثله في العرف مقارناً، فلا إشكال، وإن كان بزمان متطاول فالمعجزة عند من شرط المقارنة هو ذلك القول المقارن، فإنه إخبار بالغيب، لكن العلم بإعجازه تراخي إلى وقت وقوع ذلك الأمر، ومن جعل المعجزة نفس ذلك الأمر، فهو لا يشترط المقارنة، وعلى التقديرين لا يصح من ذلك النبي تكليف الناس بالتزام الشرع نا جزاً لانتفاء المعجزة أو العلم بها. لكن لو بين الأحكام وعلق التزامها بوقوع ذلك الأمر، صح عند الإمام ، ولم يصح عند القاضي.
And as for the third: because the miracle may be delayed after the challenge, as when he says: 'My miracle is what will appear from me on such a day,' and it appears. And the answer to the third: that the delayed event, if it is by a short time, is considered in custom as concurrent, so there is no problem. But if it is by a protracted time, then the miracle according to those who stipulate concurrence is that concurrent statement, for it is a report of the unseen, but the knowledge of its miraculousness is delayed until the time of that event. And for those who make the miracle itself that event, they do not stipulate concurrence. On both views, it is not valid for that prophet to obligate people to adherence to the law immediately, due to the absence of the miracle or knowledge of it. However, if he explained the rulings and made their adherence conditional on the occurrence of that event, it is valid according to al-Imam, but not according to al-Qadi.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage deals with the third issue: a miracle may be delayed after the challenge. For example, a prophet says 'My miracle will appear on such a day' and then it appears. The answer distinguishes two cases: if the delay is short (by custom considered concurrent), there is no issue. If the delay is long, those who stipulate concurrence (muqārana) consider the miracle to be the statement itself (because it is a report of the unseen), though knowledge of its miraculousness is delayed until the event occurs. Those who consider the event itself to be the miracle do not require concurrence. In either case, the prophet cannot immediately obligate people to follow the law because the miracle (or knowledge of it) is not yet present. However, if he explains the rulings and makes adherence conditional on the occurrence of the event, it is valid according to al-Imam (possibly Imām al-Ḥaramayn?), but not according to al-Qadi (likely al-Qāḍī ʿAbd al-Jabbār or al-Qāḍī al-Bāqillānī?). The passage leaves the disagreement noted.

Key Terms
تخدي (تحدي)
مقارنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example given in the passage, how does the prophet specify his miracle?
A) He says 'My miracle is whatever appears today'
B) He says 'My miracle is what will appear from me on such a day'
C) He performs a miracle and then challenges
D) He remains silent and a miracle occurs

Key Takeaway
A miracle can be delayed after a challenge and still be valid as long as it occurs as promised.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever desperately prayed for a sign from God, only to feel that He was silent or delayed? In that waiting, did you hold onto the promise that He would answer in His time, or did doubt creep in? How does this lesson's principle—that a delayed miracle is still a miracle—challenge your impatience and strengthen your trust in God's timing?
?
Think of a time when you judged a person or a message based on how quickly results appeared. Did you dismiss something true because it didn't happen immediately? This lesson teaches that the truth of a sign is not diminished by delay. Where in your life do you need to apply this lesson—to wait for God's promised help instead of demanding instant proof?

Concurrence and Obligations After a Delayed Miracle
Explains the implications of a delayed miracle and scholarly disagreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the conditions for a delayed miracle's validity and the disagreement between scholars on whether people can be obligated immediately.

Core Idea
A delayed miracle does not obligate immediate adherence, but conditional obligation may be valid.

Having established that a miracle can be delayed, the author now examines the details. He distinguishes between short and long delays, explains two views on what constitutes the miracle, and discusses whether the prophet can obligate people to follow the law before the miracle occurs. This leads to a noted disagreement between two early scholars.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Read from 'وعن الثالث' to the end, along with its translation. This is the answer to the third objection.
The translation uses 'answer to the third'.
2
Identify the two cases of delay
Short delay (customarily considered concurrent) has no issue. Long delay leads to different views on what the miracle is.
Think about why short delay is not problematic.
3
Understand the two views on long delay
Those who stipulate concurrence consider the statement of challenge (report of unseen) as the miracle; others consider the event itself as the miracle.
Note that knowledge of miraculousness is delayed in the first view.
4
Analyze the obligation consequence
In both views, immediate obligation to follow the law is invalid. However, conditional obligation (making adherence contingent on the event) is valid according to al-Imam but not al-Qadi.
This is a real scholarly disagreement.

And as for the third: because the miracle may be delayed after the challenge, as when he says: 'My miracle is what will appear from me on such a day,' and it appears. And the answer to the third: that the delayed event, if it is by a short time, is considered in custom as concurrent, so there is no problem. But if it is by a protracted time, then the miracle according to those who stipulate concurrence is that concurrent statement, for it is a report of the unseen, but the knowledge of its miraculousness is delayed until the time of that event. Commentary
This passage deals with the third issue: a miracle may be delayed after the challenge. For example, a prophet says 'My miracle will appear on such a day' and then it appears. The answer distinguishes two cases: if the delay is short (by custom considered concurrent), there is no issue. If the delay is long, those who stipulate concurrence (muqārana) consider the miracle to be the statement itself (because it is a report of the unseen), though knowledge of its miraculousness is delayed until the event occurs. However, if he explains the rulings and makes adherence conditional on the occurrence of the event, it is valid according to al-Imam (possibly Imām al-Ḥaramayn?), but not according to al-Qadi (likely al-Qāḍī ʿAbd al-Jabbār or al-Qāḍī al-Bāqillānī?). The passage leaves the disagreement noted.

Key Terms
مقارنة
إخبار بالغيب
الإمام
القاضي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A delayed miracle cannot be a valid miracle because it does not occur immediately.
Correction: A delayed miracle can be considered valid either through the statement of challenge (as a report of the unseen) or through the event itself, depending on one's view.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the view of those who do not stipulate concurrence (muqārana) regarding a long-delayed miracle?
A) The miracle is the statement of challenge
B) The miracle is the event itself
C) The miracle is invalid
D) The prophet can obligate people immediately

Key Takeaway
A delayed miracle's validity depends on whether the delay is short (customarily concurrent) or long; immediate obligation is not possible, but conditional obligation is subject to scholarly disagreement.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray for a sign or answer from Allah and it is delayed, how do you respond? Do you still hold onto obedience conditionally, or do you hesitate because you haven't seen the miracle yet?
?
If you knew for certain that a promised sign would occur in the future, would you commit to following a command right now even without witnessing the sign? What holds you back?
?
Reflect on a time when Allah revealed a truth to you gradually. Did you require immediate proof to act, or did you trust and obey conditionally? Which scholar's position—al-Imam's or al-Qadi's—reflects your own heart's default stance?

Conditions of Miracles: No Opposition and Time of Obligation
Clarifies the condition of no opposition and adds the condition that the miracle occur during the time of religious obligations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two conditions that refine the definition of a miracle: the absence of opposition from non-prophets and the requirement that it occur during the time of religious obligation.

Core Idea
Miracles only count as proof if they appear from a prophet during the time of obligation.

In this passage, the author clarifies what it means for a miracle to have 'no opposition' and adds an important temporal condition. Understanding these nuances prevents confusion about what counts as a miracle and what does not.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the two distinct parts: first the clarification of 'no opposition', then the additional condition.
2
Understand the 'no opposition' condition
The passage says that 'no opposition' means that no one other than a prophet can produce a similar event. However, if another prophet produces a similar miracle, that is allowed.
This prevents the objection that two prophets might have the same miracle.
3
Consider the time condition
Some scholars added that the miracle must occur during the time of religious obligation (taklīf). Extraordinary events in the afterlife or at the end of the world do not count as miracles because they cannot attest to a claim of prophethood.
At the end of time, customs are broken, so extraordinary events are no longer unusual.
4
Reflect on the reasoning
Ask yourself why the time condition is necessary. The passage gives two reasons: afterlife events are not miracles, and end-time events do not attest to truthfulness because norms are already changing.
This connects to the purpose of miracles: to prove prophethood during the period when people are obligated to believe.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم المراد بعدم المعارضة أن لا يظهر مثله ممن ليس بنبي ، وأما من نبي آخر فلا امتناع وزاد بعضهم في تفسير المعجزة قيداً آخر، وهو أن يكون في زمان التكليف، لأن ما يقع في الآخرة من الخوارق ليست بمعجزة، ولأن ما يظهر عند ظهور أشراط الساعة وانتهاء التكاليف لا يشهد بصدق الدعوى (۲) لكونه زمان نقض العادات وتغير الرسوم.
Then what is meant by 'absence of opposition' is that no similar event appears from one who is not a prophet; as for from another prophet, there is no impossibility. And some added another condition in the interpretation of miracle, which is that it be in the time of obligation (taklīf), because the extraordinary events that occur in the afterlife are not miracles, and because what appears at the coming of the signs of the Hour and the end of obligations does not attest to the truthfulness of the claim, because it is a time of breaking of customs and change of norms.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage first clarifies the condition of 'no opposition' (ʿadam al-muʿāraḍa): it means that no similar event appears from someone who is not a prophet. However, it is not impossible for a similar miracle to appear from another prophet; that is allowed. Then it mentions an additional condition added by some scholars: the miracle must occur during the time of taklīf (the period of religious obligation). Extraordinary events in the afterlife are not miracles because they do not serve to attest to a prophetic claim. Similarly, events at the onset of the Last Hour, when customs are broken and norms change, cannot serve as evidence for a claim of prophethood because they are no longer unusual.

Key Terms
عدم المعارضة
زمان التكليف
أشراط الساعة
نقض العادات
تغير الرسوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that any extraordinary event can be a miracle, regardless of when or by whom it occurs.
Correction: Miracles must come from a prophet, not be matched by non-prophets, and occur during the time of religious obligation to serve as valid evidence.

Checkpoint
Why are extraordinary events in the afterlife not considered miracles according to the passage?
A) Because they are not caused by God
B) Because they do not occur in the time of obligation and cannot attest to a claim of prophethood
C) Because they are not witnessed by people
D) Because they are opposed by other prophets

Key Takeaway
Miracles are extraordinary events that appear from a prophet during the time of obligation, without being matched by non-prophets.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Are there times when you expect a miracle from God to confirm your own desires or beliefs, even though you are not in the time of obligation? How does this lesson correct that expectation and remind you that miracles are reserved for prophetic proof during the time of obligation?

Brief assertion of the necessary possibility of miracles and transmission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the possibility of miracles and their transmission to absentees is considered rationally necessary, and how miracles function as evidence similar to explicit statements.

Core Idea
Miracles are not just possible—they are rationally necessary.

In this passage, the author asserts a foundational claim about miracles: their possibility is not merely contingent but rationally necessary. He extends this necessity to the transmission of miracle reports to those who did not witness them, and begins to explain how miracles serve as signs. This lesson unpacks these concise but powerful statements.

2

3

The author compares miracles to explicit statements (ṣarīḥ), indicating they are clear proofs, but the text cuts off.
The passage is incomplete, so this point is preliminary.

Classification of Extraordinary Events: Irhas, Karamah, and Trial
Classification of extraordinary events into irhas, karamah, and trial

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different categories of extraordinary events (kharq al-‘adat) and how they are classified as irhas, karamah, or trial based on timing and the person involved.

Core Idea
Extraordinary events are classified by context: irhas founds a mission, karamah honors a saint, and trials test false claimants.

The study of miracles begins by distinguishing them from other extraordinary events. Not every wonder is a miracle; some are signs preparing for a prophet (irhas) or favors granted to saints (karamah). This passage from Sanusi's work lays out the classification criteria clearly, including the case of events that appear at the hand of a false claimant of divinity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on classification
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how the author uses conditional structures to categorize different types of extraordinary events.
Focus on the conditions: prior to mission, widespread, and person involved.
2
Identify the three main categories
The passage mentions irhas, karamah, and trial (ibtila'). Identify what each term means and under what circumstances an event falls into each category.
Irhas is foundation for prophethood, karamah is an honor for a saint, and trial is a test for a false claimant.
3
Understand the logic behind the trial case
Why does the author say a claimant to divinity can produce an extraordinary event as a trial, but a false prophet cannot? The answer lies in definitive proofs establishing falsehood.
This distinction shows the theological reasoning behind limiting miracles to true prophets.
4
Apply the classification to examples
Consider examples given: the light on Abdullah's forehead is pure irhas; an event from a righteous non-prophet could be either irhas or karamah. Try to categorize them.
This will help solidify the concepts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التغليب والتشبيه والمحققون على أن خوارق العادات المتعلقة ببعثة النبي إذا كانت متقدمة. فإن ظهرت منه، فإن شاعت وكان هو مظنة البعثة كما في حق نبينا عليه السلام حيث أخبر بذلك بعض أهل الكتاب والكهنة، فإرهاص أي تأسيس لقاعدة البعثة وإلا فكرامة محضة، وإن ظهرت من غيره، فإن كان من الأخيار، فكذلك أي إرهاص أو كرامة وإلا فإرهاص محض كظهور النور في جبين عبد الله، أو ابتلاء كما إذا ظهرت على يد من ادعى الألوهية. فإن الأدلة القطعية قائمة على كذبه بخلاف مدعي النبوة. فلهذا جوزوا إظهارها على يد المتآله دون المتنبي. وعن الثالث أن المتأخر، إن كان بزمان يسير يعد مثله في العرف مقارناً، فلا إشكال، وإن كان بزمان متطاول فالمعجزة عند من شرط المقارنة هو ذلك القول المقارن، فإنه إخبار بالغيب، لكن العلم بإعجازه تراخي إلى وقت وقوع ذلك الأمر، ومن جعل المعجزة نفس ذلك الأمر، فهو لا يشترط المقارنة، وعلى التقديرين لا يصح من ذلك النبي تكليف الناس بالتزام الشرع ناجزاً لانتفاء المعجزة أو العلم بها. لكن لو بين الأحكام وعلق التزامها بوقوع ذلك الأمر، صح عند الإمام، ولم يصح عند القاضي، ثم المراد بعدم المعارضة أن لا يظهر مثله ممن ليس بنبي، وأما من نبي آخر فلا امتناع وزاد بعضهم في تفسير المعجزة قيداً آخر، وهو أن يكون في زمان التكليف، لأن ما يقع في الآخرة من الخوارق ليست بمعجزة، ولأن ما يظهر عند ظهور أشراط الساعة وانتهاء التكاليف لا يشهد بصدق الدعوى لكونه زمان نقض العادات وتغير الرسوم.
Al-taghlib wa al-tashbih and the verifiers hold that the extraordinary events related to the mission of the prophet, if they are prior. If they appear from him, and they become widespread and he is the locus of the mission as in the case of our prophet, peace be upon him, when some of the People of the Book and soothsayers informed of that, then it is irhas, i.e., a foundation for the basis of the mission; otherwise it is a pure karamah. And if they appear from someone else, if he is from the righteous, then similarly it is either irhas or karamah; otherwise it is a pure irhas, like the appearance of light on the forehead of 'Abd Allah, or a trial as when it appears at the hand of one who claims divinity. For definitive proofs are established upon his falsehood, unlike the claimant of prophethood. For this reason, they permitted its manifestation at the hand of the one claiming divinity but not the claimant of prophethood. And regarding the third: that which is later, if it is by a short time such that it is considered in custom as concomitant, then there is no problem. But if it is by a long time, then the miracle, according to the one who stipulates concomitance, is that concomitant statement, for it is an announcement of the unseen, but knowledge of its miraculousness is deferred to the time of the occurrence of that matter. And the one who makes the miracle that very matter, he does not stipulate concomitance. On both estimates, it is not valid for that prophet to obligate people to adhere to the law immediately due to the absence of the miracle or knowledge of it. But if he explained the rulings and suspended their adherence upon the occurrence of that matter, it is valid according to al-Imam, and not valid according to al-Qadi. Then the meaning of non-contradiction is that its like does not appear from one who is not a prophet; but from another prophet, there is no impossibility. And some added another condition in the interpretation of miracle: that it be in the time of religious obligation, because what occurs in the afterlife of extraordinary events is not a miracle, and because what appears at the appearance of the signs of the Hour and the end of obligations does not testify to the truth of the claim, because it is a time of breaking of customs and changing of norms.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage classifies extraordinary events and defines the conditions of miracles. The author begins by discussing events that occur before the prophet's mission (irhas) and their role as a foundation for the mission. He then distinguishes between events that appear from the prophet (which are irhas or karamah) and from others. If from a righteous non-prophet, it can be irhas (if preceding a mission) or karamah; otherwise it is pure irhas or a trial (ibtila'). He then addresses the issue of timing: if a miracle occurs after the claim but not immediately, it can still count if the claim itself is a prophecy fulfilled later, but this raises issues about immediate obligation. The views of al-Imam (al-Ash'ari?) and al-Qadi are contrasted. The condition of non-contradiction ('adam al-mu'aradah) is explained: the miracle must not be matched by a non-prophet, though it can be matched by another prophet. Finally, an additional condition is added: the miracle must occur in the time of taklif (religious obligation), not in the afterlife or during the apocalyptic signs when customs are broken.

Key Terms
خوارق العادات
إرهاص
كرامة
معجزة
ابتلاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any extraordinary event is a miracle that confirms prophethood.
Correction: The passage shows that extraordinary events can be irhas (foundation for prophet), karamah (favor for saint), or even a trial for a false claimant of divinity. Only those meeting specific conditions are considered miracles confirming prophethood.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the term for an extraordinary event that appears from a righteous non-prophet?
A) Irhas only
B) Karamah only
C) Either irhas or karamah
D) Ibtila'

Key Takeaway
Extraordinary events are not all miracles; they are classified by timing and person into irhas, karamah, and trial, each serving a different theological purpose.

Are you too quick to accept charisma or wonders as proof of righteousness? What criteria from this lesson can you apply to guard your heart?

Miracles as Proof of Prophethood and Refutation of Objections
Author proves miracles indicate truthfulness and refutes four objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how miracles serve as proof of prophethood and how classical scholars responded to rational objections against their indicative value.

Core Idea
Miracles are divine affirmations; rational possibilities do not undermine certainty.

This passage from al-Maqasid tackles a central issue in proving prophethood: how do miracles actually indicate truthfulness? The author presents a clear analogy, then anticipates and refutes four objections that might weaken the argument. By studying this, you will see how kalam deals with probabilistic challenges.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the full translation. Notice the structure: analogy, objections, and answers.
The passage uses an analogy from royal courts to illustrate the indicative value of miracles.
2
Understand the analogy
The author likens a miracle to a king violating his custom to affirm a messenger. This analogy helps grasp how an extraordinary event can serve as explicit confirmation.
The analogy is based on qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid (analogical reasoning from the witnessed to the unseen).
3
Identify the four objections
The passage lists four rational possibilities that could undermine the indicative value of a miracle. Find each one and note its specific challenge.
Objections include natural causes, rare coincidences, contestation, and alternative purposes.
4
Review the overall and detailed answers
The author gives a summary answer that rational possibilities do not preclude certainty, then provides two detailed points: only God is the true agent, and the miracle is known to be extraordinary and unchallenged.
The detailed answer also explains why each prophet's miracle matches the expertise of his time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: وأما إمكانها فضروري (وكذا إمكان نقلها إلى الغائبين، وأما وجه دلالتها فهو أنها بمنزلة صريح التصديق، كما إذا ادعى أحد أنه رسول هذا الملك فطولب بالحجة، فقال: أن يخالف الملك عادته ويقوم عن سريره ثلاث مرات. ففعل. وهذا توضيح بالمثال لاستدلال بقياس الغائب على الشاهد فإن قيل ههنا أنواع احتمالات لا يثبت معها المقصود.

الأول أن يستند ذلك الأمر إلى المدعي لخاصية في نفسه، أو مزاج في بدنه أو اطلاع منه على بعض الخواص، أو الأوضاع الفلكية، أو إلى ملك أو جني، أو غير ذلك.

الثاني: أن يكون ابتداء عادة أو تكريراً بما لا يكون إلا بعد دهور.

الثالث: أن يكون مما يعارض، ولم يعارض لغرض، أو عورض ولم ينقل لمانع.

الرابع: أن لا يكون لغرض التصديق، إما لانتفاء الغرض، أو لثبوت غرض آخر، كلطف المكلف، أو إجابة لدعوة، أو معجزة لنبي آخر، أو ابتلاء للعباد، أو إضلال لهم، ويعد كونه بمنزلة صريح القول بأنك صادق، فإنما يفيد إذا استحال الكذب في إخباره، وما ذلك إلا بالسمع.

فالجواب إجمالاً أن الاحتمالات العقلية لا تنافي حصول العلم القطعي كما في سائر العاديات، وتفصيلاً:

أولاً بأنا بينا أن لا مؤثر سيما في مثل هذه الغرائب إلا الله تعالى على أن مجرد التمكين كاف في إفادة المطلوب.

وثانياً: بأن الكلام فيما علم قطعاً أنه خارق للعادة، وأن المتحدين عجزوا عن معارضته مع فرط الاهتمام، وكمال الاشتغال ولهذا كانت معجزة كل نبي من جنس ما غلب على أهل زمانه كالسحر في زمن موسى عليه السلام، والطب. قال تعالى مخاطباً موسى عليه السلام: {وألق ما في يمينك تلقف ما صنعوا إنما صنعوا كيد ساحر ولا يفلح الساحر حيث أتى}
He said: As for its possibility, it is necessary (and likewise the possibility of transmitting it to the absent). And as for the aspect of its indication, it is that it is in the position of explicit affirmation, just as when someone claims that he is the messenger of this king, and is asked for proof, and he says: 'Let the king violate his custom and rise from his throne three times.' And he does it. This is an illustration by example of inference by analogizing the absent to the witnessed. For it is said: here are types of possibilities that do not establish the intended purpose.

First: that that matter is attributable to the claimant due to a property in himself, or a temperament in his body, or his knowledge of some properties, or astronomical configurations, or to an angel or jinn, or other than that.

Second: that it be the beginning of a custom or a repetition that only occurs after ages.

Third: that it be such as to be contested, but it was not contested for some purpose, or it was contested but not transmitted due to an obstacle.

Fourth: that it is not for the purpose of affirmation, either due to the absence of purpose, or due to the existence of another purpose, such as kindness to the obligated, or answering a supplication, or a miracle for another prophet, or a trial for the servants, or misguidance for them. And considering it to be in the position of the explicit statement that you are truthful, it only benefits if lying in his report is impossible, and that is only by revelation.

So the answer in summary is that rational possibilities do not preclude the attainment of certain knowledge as in other customary matters. And in detail:

First: we have explained that there is no effective agent especially in such strange matters except Allah, may He be exalted, and that mere enabling is sufficient for producing the desired outcome.

Second: that the discussion concerns what is known with certainty to be extraordinary, and that the challengers were unable to contest it despite extreme interest and complete engagement. And for this reason, the miracle of every prophet was of the type that dominated the people of his time, like sorcery in the time of Moses, peace be upon him, and medicine. He said, addressing Moses, peace be upon him: {And throw what is in your right hand; it will swallow up what they have made. They have only made the trick of a sorcerer, and the sorcerer will not succeed wherever he comes.}
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage proves the indicative value of miracles and responds to objections. The author first asserts that the possibility of miracles is necessary (daruri) and that their indication is like explicit affirmation (sarih al-tasdiq). He gives a parable: a claimant to be a king's messenger asks the king to violate custom by rising three times; when the king does so, it confirms the claim. This is an inference by analogy (qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid). Then four objections are raised: (1) the event might have a natural or supernatural cause other than divine support; (2) it might be a coincidence or a rare occurrence; (3) it might be contested but the contestation was not transmitted; (4) it might have a purpose other than affirmation (e.g., kindness, answering prayer, miracle for another prophet, trial, or misguidance). The author notes that the miracle's value as explicit affirmation only holds if the prophet's truthfulness is already established by revelation. The answer is given in summary (ijmalan) and detail (tafsilan). In summary, rational possibilities do not negate certain knowledge (al-‘ilm al-qat‘i) in customary matters. In detail: first, only God is the true agent of such extraordinary events, and mere enabling (tamkin) by God suffices. Second, the miracle under discussion is known with certainty to be extraordinary and the challengers (mutahaddun) failed to match it despite full effort. This is why each prophet's miracle matches the dominant expertise of his time (e.g., sorcery for Moses, medicine for Jesus?). The Quranic verse is cited as evidence.

Key Terms
إمكان
دلالة
تصديق
احتمالات
قطع
تمكين
تحدي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Rational possibilities (like natural causes or alternative purposes) completely undermine the certainty of a miracle's indication.
Correction: The author argues that rational possibilities do not preclude certain knowledge, just as in other customary matters; additional considerations like the inability to contest it reinforce certainty.

Checkpoint
What is the author's general answer to the four objections?
A) Rational possibilities do not prevent certain knowledge, as in other customary matters.
B) The objections only apply to false prophets.
C) The miracle must be contemporaneous.
D) The miracle must not be contested.

Key Takeaway
Miracles, when properly understood as extraordinary events that cannot be matched, provide certain knowledge of the prophet's truthfulness, and rational possibilities do not negate that certainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you confront doubts about the truth of Islam, do you treat them as 'just rational possibilities' without seriously examining the actual evidence of miracles? The author argues that rational possibilities do not undermine certain knowledge. Are you willing to set aside abstract doubts and directly engage with the specific miracles of the Prophet (ﷺ) to build unshakeable conviction? What concrete step will you take this week to study one miracle in depth?
?
The author compares a miracle to a king rising from his throne at a claimant's request. Have you ever experienced a clear sign from Allah—an answered prayer or an unmistakable event—that should have strengthened your faith, but you rationalized it away as coincidence? What would it take for you to accept such an event as a divine affirmation of truth, and are you prepared to change your life based on that acceptance?
?
Consider the challenge (tahaddi) aspect: the Quran challenges humanity to produce something like it. Have you ever honestly confronted that challenge, or have you avoided it for fear of what it might demand? What is preventing you from making a sincere, humble effort to test the claim of prophethood? Will you commit to reading the Quran with the intention of verifying its divine origin, starting today?

Three Rational Proofs for the Indicative Value of Miracles
Sanusi enumerates three arguments for the probative value of miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational arguments for why miracles serve as definitive proof of prophethood, including the contextual fitting of miracles, the concept of ends without purposes, the certainty that miracles do not occur on liars, and the direct knowledge they provide.

Core Idea
Miracles are self-authenticating signs: they fit their time and prove truth directly.

This passage from Sanusi presents three rational proofs for why miracles are reliable indicators of a prophet's truthfulness. He begins with a striking observation that each prophet's miracle matched the dominant art of their era—music for David, eloquence for Muhammad. Then he delves into three distinct arguments: the presence of ends even without explicit purposes, the impossibility (or extreme unlikelihood) of miracles occurring on liars, and the direct knowledge they impart without needing a divine declaration. Let's examine each component carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the historical observation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Sanusi first mentions the matching of miracles to the arts of their time. This sets the stage for the rational arguments.
2
Identify the three arguments
The passage presents three numbered arguments: third, fourth, and fifth. For each, note the key claim and the reasoning supporting it.
3
Reflect on the cumulative force
Ask yourself how these three arguments work together to establish that miracles are proofs of prophethood. Consider whether they complement each other or are independent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في زمن عيسى(۱) (علیه السلام) والموسيقى في زمن داود عليه السلام) والفصاحة في زمن محمد (صلى الله عليه وسلم) .
وثالثاً : أنه لا خفاء في ترتب الغايات على أفعاله، وإن لم تكن أغراضاً على أنا لا ندعي سوى أنها تدل على تصديق قائم بذاته، سواء كان غرضاً أم لم يكن .
ورابعاً : أن ظهور المعجزة على يد الكاذب وإن جاز عقلاً، فمعلوم الانتفاء قطعاً. ومنا من قال باستحالته لإفضائه إلى التعجيز عن الأدلة على صدق دعوى الرسالة، أو لأن الصدق لازم لها بمنزلة العلم لإيقان الفعل، أو لأن التسوية بين الصادق والكاذب سفه .
وخامساً : أنها تفيد العلم بالصدق من غير افتقار إلى اعتبار إخبار من الله بمنزلة أن يقول : جعلتك رسولاً، وأنشأت الرسالة فيك .
In the time of Jesus, peace be upon him, and music in the time of David, peace be upon him, and eloquence in the time of Muhammad, peace be upon him. Thirdly: There is no obscurity in the ordering of ends to His actions, even if they are not purposes, for we do not claim anything except that they indicate an affirmation subsisting in His essence, whether it is a purpose or not. Fourthly: The appearance of a miracle at the hand of a liar, even if intellectually possible, is known to be non-existent with certainty. Among us are those who say it is impossible because it would lead to incapacity to provide evidence for the truth of the claim of prophethood, or because truthfulness is necessary for it like knowledge for certainty of action, or because equating the truthful and the liar is foolishness. Fifthly: It (the miracle) provides knowledge of truth without needing to consider a declaration from God, like His saying: 'I have made you a messenger and established the message in you.'
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage presents three numbered arguments (third, fourth, fifth) for how miracles indicate the truth of a prophet's claim, preceded by a brief observation that each prophet's miracle matched the dominant art of his time (music for David, eloquence for Muhammad, etc.). The third argument states that actions (including miracles) have ends even if not purposes, and they indicate an affirmation of truth. The fourth argument asserts that although a miracle occurring on a liar is theoretically possible, it is known with certainty not to happen; some scholars argue it is impossible because it would undermine evidence for prophethood or because truthfulness is inherent to the miracle. The fifth argument claims that miracles produce direct knowledge of the prophet's truth without needing an explicit divine declaration, as if God said 'I have made you a messenger.'

Key Terms
تصديق
غرض
إيقان
استحالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The third argument denies that miracles have any purpose (غرض).
Correction: Sanusi says that ends (غايات) exist even if purposes (أغراض) are not explicit; the argument does not deny purpose altogether.

Checkpoint
According to the fifth argument, how does a miracle provide knowledge of the prophet's truthfulness?
A) Through direct divine declaration
B) Without needing any explicit divine declaration
C) Through the miracle's impossibility on a liar
D) Through the miracle matching the art of the time

Key Takeaway
Sanusi provides three rational arguments—ends without purposes, impossibility of miracles on liars, and direct knowledge—that together establish miracles as authoritative proofs of prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear about miracles, do you ever doubt that they could truly prove someone's prophethood? How does Sanusi's argument that miracles provide direct knowledge challenge your own skepticism? Will you now seek to strengthen your certainty in the miracles of the Prophet (pbuh) by reflecting on these proofs?
?
Sanusi argues that equating the truthful and the liar is foolish. In your daily life, do you sometimes treat truth and falsehood as if they are equally valid? How does this lesson compel you to be more discerning in what you accept as truth?
?
Consider the fittingness of miracles to their time: music for David, eloquence for Muhammad. What does this teach you about how Allah communicates with people according to their circumstances? Are you listening to the 'miracle' that speaks to your own heart—the Quran—as the definitive sign for you?

Refuting the Sophistry Objection to Miracles
Sanusi refutes the objection that allowing miracles is sophistry.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's response to the claim that allowing miracles leads to logical absurdities.

Core Idea
Miracles are possible in themselves, but contrary to custom.

Some opponents of prophethood argue that if miracles were possible, anything could happen — even turning mountains to gold. Sanusi refutes this by clarifying what miracles actually are: events that are intrinsically possible but do not normally occur. This lesson explores that distinction and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the opening lines where the deniers claim that permitting extraordinary events (khawariq al-'adat) is sophistry because it would allow absurdities.
Notice the examples they give: mountain turning to gold, sea to oil, etc.
2
Identify Sanusi's definition
Find where Sanusi defines what is meant by khawariq al-'adat: things possible in themselves but impossible by custom.
The key phrase is 'أمور ممكنة في نفسها، ممتنعة في العادة'.
3
Understand the analogy
Sanusi compares the origination of miracles to the creation of the heavens and earth — both are within divine power, so the objection of absurdity collapses.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The denial of specific miraculous occurrences (like mountain->gold) does not invalidate miracles per se; it only shows they are not essential to prophethood.
Sanusi refers back to earlier discussions in the book.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قدح بعض المنكرين للنبوة في المعجزات بأن تجويز خوارق العادات سفسطة، إذ لو جازت لجاز أن ينقلب الجبل ذهباً والبحر دهناً، والمدعي للنبوة شخصاً آخر عليه ظهرت المعجزة إلى غير ذلك من المحالات، وبعضهم بأنها على تقدير ثبوتها لا تثبت على الغائبين لأن أقوى طرق نقلها التواتر، وهو لا يفيد اليقين، لأن جواز الكذب على كل أحد يوجب جوازه على الكل، لكونه نفس الأحاد، ولأنه لو أفاده الأفاده خبر الواحد، لأن كل طبقة يفرض عدد التواتر، فعند نقصان واحد منه إن بقيت مفيدة لليقين، وهكذا إلى الواحد، فظاهر. وإن لم تبق كان المفيد هو ذلك الواحد الزائد. ولأنه غير مضبوط بعدد، بل ضابطه حصول اليقين، فإثبات اليقين به يكون دوراً.
والجواب عن الأول أن المراد بخوارق العادات أمور ممكنة في نفسها، ممتنعة في .العادة. بمعنى أنها لم يجر العادة بوقوعها كانقلاب العصا حية، فإمكانها ضروري، وإبداعها ليس أبعد من إبداع خلق الأرض والسماء وما بينهما، والجزم بعدم وقوع بعضها كانقلاب الجبل والبحر ، وهذا الشخص، وأمثال ذلك لا ينافي إلا مكان الذاتي على ما سبق في صدر الكتاب.
وعن الثاني بأن المتواترات أحد أقسام الضروريات، فالقدح فيها بما ذكر مع أنه ظاهر الاندفاع لا يستحق الجواب.
Some deniers of prophethood impugned miracles by saying that allowing extraordinary events is sophistry, for if they were possible, then it would be possible for a mountain to turn into gold, the sea into oil, and the claimant of prophethood to be another person upon whom the miracle appeared, and other impossibilities. And some of them (said) that even if they (miracles) are established, they are not established for absent people because the strongest means of their transmission is tawatur (mass transmission), and it does not yield certainty, because the possibility of lying for each individual necessitates its possibility for the whole, since it is the same as single reports; and because if it (tawatur) provided certainty, then a single report would provide it, because each level is assumed to be the number of tawatur; when one is missing, if it (the remainder) remains beneficial for certainty, and so on down to one, it is obvious; and if it does not remain, then the beneficial thing is that extra one. And because it is not defined by a number, rather its definition is the attainment of certainty, so establishing certainty by it would be circular. The answer to the first: that the intended meaning of extraordinary events is things that are possible in themselves, impossible by custom, meaning that it is not customary for them to occur, like the staff turning into a snake. Their possibility is necessary, and their origination is not more remote than the origination of the creation of the heavens and the earth and what is between them; and asserting the non-occurrence of some of them, like the mountain turning into gold and the sea (into oil) and that person, and the like, does not contradict except the status of the essential as previously mentioned at the beginning of the book. And as for the second: mutawatirat (mass-transmitted reports) are one of the divisions of necessary things, so impugning them with what was mentioned, even though it is clearly refutable, does not deserve a response.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage presents two objections against miracles and their refutations. The first objection claims that allowing supernatural events (khawariq al-'adat) is sophistry because it would lead to absurd possibilities. The second objection argues that even if miracles exist, they cannot be proven to later generations because tawatur (mass transmission) does not yield certainty due to the possibility of lying and regress problems. Sanusi's answer to the first objection clarifies that miracles are possible in themselves but contrary to habit (mumtani' fi al-'ada), meaning they do not normally occur; their possibility is self-evident and no more impossible than creation itself. The assertion that certain miracles (like turning a mountain to gold) do not occur does not contradict the essence of miracles as defined. The answer to the second objection is brief: mutawatirat are a kind of necessary knowledge (dururiyyat), so the objection is baseless and does not merit a detailed response.

Key Terms
خوارق العادات
سفسطة
ممكن
ممتنع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think miracles violate natural laws absolutely and thus lead to logical chaos.
Correction: Sanusi explains miracles are only contrary to custom, not impossibility, and their occurrence is limited to what is intrinsically possible like the creation itself.

Checkpoint
How does Sanusi define khawariq al-'adat (extraordinary events) in his response to the first objection?
A) As impossible in themselves
B) As possible in themselves but contrary to custom
C) As necessarily false
D) As only possible through divine intervention

Key Takeaway
Miracles are not violations of logic but extraordinary events that are possible in themselves, contrary only to normal patterns.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you secretly doubt that miracles are possible because you have never seen one? How does Sanusi’s distinction between 'possible in itself' and 'contrary to custom' challenge that doubt? Will you now reconsider what you think is impossible?
?
When you hear a story of a miracle, do you dismiss it as impossible without considering that it might be a khariq al-'ada? What does that say about your reliance on custom as the sole measure of truth? Are you willing to open your heart to the reality of divine intervention?
?
The objection of sophistry suggests that believing in miracles opens the door to absurdity. But Sanusi argues that the creation of a miracle is no more improbable than the original creation of the heavens and earth. Does your mind truly believe that? If not, what is holding you back from accepting the power of the Creator over His creation?

Refuting the Tawatur Objection to Miracles
Sanusi refutes the objection that tawatur cannot prove miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi dismisses the objection that mass transmission (tawatur) cannot yield certainty about miracles.

Core Idea
Mutawatirat are a type of necessary knowledge; the objection is frivolous.

A second objection argues that even if miracles occurred, they cannot be reliably known by later generations because tawatur (mass transmission) does not guarantee certainty. Sanusi's answer is brief: mutawatirat are part of necessary knowledge (daruriyyat), so the objection is baseless. This lesson unpacks this response.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the passage where opponents claim that tawatur cannot yield certainty due to the possibility of lying and regress arguments.
Note the detailed reasoning: if each individual can lie, then the whole can; if tawatur gave certainty, singletons would too, etc.
2
Locate Sanusi's answer
Find Sanusi's response: mutawatirat are one of the divisions of necessary things (daruriyyat), so the objection is not worth a detailed answer.
The phrase 'المتواترات أحد أقسام الضروريات' is key.
3
Understand the dismissive tone
Sanusi says the objection is 'clearly refutable' (ظاهر الاندفاع) and does not merit a response, indicating its weakness from the perspective of established kalam principles.
