The Direct Indication of Miracles
Sanusi explains the mechanism of miracle indication and rebuts the analogy objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a miracle directly produces necessary knowledge of a prophet's truthfulness and why the objection that it is mere analogy fails.

Core Idea
A miracle is like an explicit affirmation, not an analogy.

This passage tackles a critical question: how exactly does a miracle prove that someone is a true prophet? Sanusi argues that the miracle itself, by divine custom, directly creates necessary knowledge (al-ilm al-daruri) in the observer. He illustrates this with a vivid example from a king's court and then defends his view against an objection that this is merely an analogy from the seen to the unseen.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once, then the translation. Notice the clear four-part structure: mechanism, example, objection, answer.
2
Identify the mechanism
Find the sentence that states how the miracle indicates truth. Sanusi says it is 'like an explicit affirmation' (bimanzilat sarih al-tasdiq) because God creates necessary knowledge after it.
3
Understand the king example
The example illustrates how an unusual act from the king directly confirms the messenger. It is not an analogy but a real-life demonstration that produces certainty.
4
Analyze the objection and answer
The objector claims this is an analogy (tamthil) that only gives conjecture. Sanusi responds that the example is only for clarification; the necessary knowledge comes from the miracle itself, not from analogical reasoning.
Notice that Sanusi strengthens his answer by considering two scenarios: absentees hearing via tawatur and presentees viewing a miracle in a controlled setting.
5
Synthesize the lesson
Summarize in your own words: Miracles are self-evident proofs that bypass human reasoning. They create immediate certainty, not probabilistic belief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما وجه دلالتها أي وجه دلالة المعجزة على صدق الرسالة أنها عند التحقيق بمنزلة صريح التصديق لما جرت العادة به من أن الله تعالى يخلق عقيبها العلم الضروري بصدقه، كما إذا قام رجل في مجلس ملك بحضور جماعة، وادعى أنه رسول هذا الملك إليهم، فطالبوه بالحجة ، فقال : هي أن يخالف هذا الملك عادته ويقوم عن سريره ثلاث مرات ويقعد ففعل، فإنه يكون تصديقاً له ومفيداً للعلم الضروري بصدقه من غير ارتياب .
فإن قيل : هذا تمثيل وقياس للغائب على الشاهد وهو على تقدير ظهور الجامع ، إنما يعتبر في العمليات لإفادة الظن، وقد اعتبرتموه بلا جامع لإفادة اليقين في العمليات التي هي أساس ثبوت الشرائع على أن حصول العلم فيما ذكرتم من المثال إنما هو بشواهد من قرائن الأحوال.
قلنا التمثيل إنما هو للتوضيح والتقريب دون الاستدلال، ولا مدخل لمشاهدة القرائن في إفادة العلم الضروري لحصوله للغائبين عن هذا المجلس عند تواتر القضية إليهم، وللحاضرين فيما إذا فرضنا الملك في بيت ليس فيه غيره ودونه حجب لا يقدر على تحريكها أحد سواه. وجعل مدعي الرسالة حجته أن الملك يحرك تلك الحجب من ساعته ففعل .
As for the aspect of its indication — that is, the aspect of the miracle's indication of the truthfulness of the message — it is, upon investigation, like an explicit affirmation, because of the custom that God, exalted, creates after it necessary knowledge of his truthfulness, just as when a man stands in the court of a king in the presence of a group and claims that he is the messenger of this king to them, and they ask him for proof, and he says: 'It is that this king should violate his custom and rise from his throne three times and sit down,' and he does it, then that is an affirmation for him and provides necessary knowledge of his truthfulness without doubt. If it is said: This is an analogy and comparison of the unseen to the witnessed, and assuming a common attribute, it is only considered in practical matters to provide conjecture, but you have applied it without a common attribute to provide certainty in practical matters which are the basis of establishing the laws, and the attainment of knowledge in the example you mentioned is only through indications from circumstantial evidence. We say: The analogy is only for clarification and approximation, not for proof; and the observation of circumstantial evidence has no part in providing necessary knowledge, because it is attained by those absent from this court when the matter is transmitted to them by tawatur, and for those present when we suppose the king in a house with no one else and behind him curtains that no one else can move, and the claimant of the message made his proof that the king would move those curtains at his command, and he did so.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage explains how a miracle indicates the truth of a prophet (the mechanism of indication) and responds to an objection. Sanusi states that the miracle is like an explicit affirmation (tasdiq sarih) because God creates necessary knowledge (al-ilm al-daruri) after it. He illustrates with the example of a messenger in a king's court: the king performs an unusual act, and that act directly confirms the messenger's truth. An objector then raises that this is an analogy (tamthil) from the witnessed to the unseen, which only yields conjecture, not certainty, and that the knowledge in the example actually comes from circumstantial evidence. The answer is that the analogy is only for clarification (tawdih) and approximation (taqrib), not for proof (istidlal); the necessary knowledge comes from the act itself, and it reaches absentees through tawatur. For presentees, even without witnesses, the same necessary knowledge is produced if the miracle occurs in a setting where no alternative explanation exists, as in the refined example of the king behind curtains.

Key Terms
وجه دلالة
علم ضروري
تمثيل
قياس
تواتر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The king example is an analogical argument (tamthil) that proves the miracle by comparing unseen to seen.
Correction: Sanusi explicitly states the example is only for illustration and approximation, not for proof (istidlal). The necessary knowledge arises from the miracle itself, not from an analogy.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why does the miracle produce necessary knowledge (al-ilm al-daruri) in the observer?
A) Because it is an analogy from the seen to the unseen
B) Because God creates it after the miracle according to His custom
C) Because observers see contextual clues
D) Because it is reported through tawatur

Key Takeaway
A miracle functions as an explicit affirmation from God, creating necessary knowledge in the observer without reliance on analogical reasoning or contextual evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear about a miracle like the splitting of the moon, do you genuinely feel necessary knowledge of the Prophet's truthfulness in your heart, or do you rationalize it away? What would change if you allowed that knowledge to settle in you?
?
Sanusi says the example of the king is just for clarification, not proof. Are there 'illustrations' in your own faith that you have mistaken for the actual proof? How can you strip away mental props and stand before the miracle itself?
?
The miracle produces necessary knowledge without need for circumstantial evidence. Are there areas of your life where you demand more 'evidence' from God before you trust Him, rather than accepting the clear signs He has already given? What would it look like to surrender to the certainty He offers?

6 min
How Miracles Produce Certain Knowledge of Prophethood
The author explains the evidential mechanism of miracles and defends it against an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how miracles provide necessary and certain knowledge of a prophet's truthfulness, based on the analogy of a king's unusual action.

Core Idea
Miracles are like a direct divine attestation, creating certain knowledge.

The author begins by explaining the evidential mechanism of miracles. He uses a vivid example: a man claims to be a king's messenger and the king violates his habit. This shows that miracles function as God's habit-breaking act that produces necessary knowledge of the prophet's truthfulness. An objection about the analogy being merely probabilistic is then addressed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the indication of miracles
Read the Arabic passage from 'وأما وجه دلالتها' to the end of the example. Notice how the author compares the miracle to a king's habit-breaking act.
The example is central to the argument.
2
Understand the objection about analogy
The objector claims the analogy is inductive and only yields probability, and that the example relies on circumstantial evidence.
3
Examine the author's response
The author replies that the analogy is merely illustrative, not inferential, and that necessary knowledge can arise without witnessing circumstances.
He gives the example of those hearing by tawatur.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعن الثاني بأن المتواترات أحد أقسام الضروريات، فالقدح فيها بما ذكر مع أنه ظاهر الاندفاع لا يستحق الجواب. وأما وجه دلالتها أي وجه دلالة المعجزة على صدق الرسالة أنها عند التحقيق بمنزلة صريح التصديق لما جرت العادة به من أن الله تعالى يخلق عقيبها العلم الضروري بصدقه، كما إذا قام رجل في مجلس ملك بحضور جماعة، وادعى أنه رسول هذا الملك إليهم، فطالبوه بالحجة، فقال: هي أن يخالف هذا الملك عادته ويقوم عن سريره ثلاث مرات ويقعد ففعل، فإنه يكون تصديقاً له ومفيداً للعلم الضروري بصدقه من غير ارتياب.
فإن قيل: هذا تمثيل وقياس للغائب على الشاهد وهو على تقدير ظهور الجامع، إنما يعتبر في العمليات لإفادة الظن، وقد اعتبرتموه بلا جامع لإفادة اليقين في العمليات التي هي أساس ثبوت الشرائع على أن حصول العلم فيما ذكرتم من المثال إنما هو بشواهد من قرائن الأحوال.
قلنا: التمثيل إنما هو للتوضيح والتقريب دون الاستدلال، ولا مدخل لمشاهدة القرائن في إفادة العلم الضروري لحصوله للغائبين عن هذا المجلس عند تواتر القضية إليهم، وللحاضرين فيما إذا فرضنا الملك في بيت ليس فيه غيره ودونه حجب لا يقدر على تحريكها أحد سواه. وجعل مدعي الرسالة حجته أن الملك يحرك تلك الحجب من ساعته ففعل.
فإن قيل: ههنا احتمالات تنفي الدلالة على الصدق والجزم به، وهي أنواع: الأول: احتمال أن لا يكون ذلك الأمر من الله تعالى، بل يستند إلى المدعي بخاصية في نفسه أو مزاج في بدنه، أو لاطلاع منه على خواص في بعض الأجسام يتخذها ذريعة (۱) إلى ذلك، أو يستند إلى بعض الملائكة أو الجن، أو إلى اتصالات كوكبية وأوضاع فلكية، لا يطلع عليها غيره، إلى غير ذلك من الأسباب. الثاني: احتمال أن لا يكون خارقاً للعادة، بل ابتداء عادة أراد الله إجراءها أو تكرير عادة لا تكون إلا في دهور متطاولة، كعود الثوابت إلى نقطة معينة. الثالث: احتمال أن يكون مما يعارض إلا أنه لم يعارض لعدم بلوغه إلى من يقدر المعارضة، أو لمواضعة من القول، وموافقة في إعلاء كلمته، أو لخوف، أو لاستهانة وقلة مبالاة، أو لاشتغال بما هو أهم، أو عورض ولم ينقل لمانع. الرابع: احتمال أن لا يكون لغرض التصديق، إما لانتفاء الغرض في فعله على ما هو المذهب، وإما لثبوت غرض آخر مثل أن يكون لطفاً بمكلف، أو إجابة لدعوته أو معجزة لنبي آخر أو ابتلاء للعبد لينال الثواب بالتوقف عن موجبه أو النظر والاجتهاد في دفعه كما في إنزال المتشابه أو إضلالاً للخلق على ما هو المذهب عندكم من أن الله يضل من يشاء من عباده.
وبعدم تسليم انتفاء الاحتمالات، وكون المعجزة بمنزلة صريح القول من الله تعالى بأن المدعي صادق فهو لا يوجب صدقه إلا بعد استحالة الكذب في إخبار الله تعالى، ولا سبيل إلى ذلك بدليل السمع للزوم الدور، ولا بدليل العقل، لأن غايته أن الكذب قبيح، وهو على الله تعالى مستحيل، وثبوت المقدمتين بغير دليل السمع في حيز المنع.
فالجواب إجمالاً أن الاحتمالات والتجويزات العقلية لا تنافي العلوم العادية الضرورية القطعية، فنحن نقطع بحصول العلم بالصدق عقيب ظهور المعجزة من غير التفات إلى ما ذكر من لا بالنفي ولا بالإثبات، كما يحصل في المثال المذكور، وإن كان الملك ظلوماً غشوماً كذوباً لا يبالي بإغواء رعيته والاستهزاء برسله، وتفصيلاً: أولاً: أنا بينا أن لا مؤثر في الوجود إلا الله وحده، سيما في مثل إحياء الموتى، وانقلاب العصا حية، وانشقاق القمر، وسلام الحجر والمدر، على أن مجرد التمكين، وترك الدفع من قبل الحكيم القادر المختار كاف في إفادة المطلوب، ولهذا ذهب المعتزلة إلى أن المعجزة (۱) تكون فعلاً لله تعالى، أو واقعاً بأمره أو بتمكينه.
(۱) المعجزة: أمر خارق للعادة داعية إلى الخير والسعادة مقرونة بدعوى النبوة قصد به إظهار صدق من ادعى أنه رسول من الله تعالى.
And regarding the second [objection]: that mutawatirat are one of the divisions of necessary [knowledge]; therefore impugning them by what was mentioned, while it is obviously refutable, does not deserve an answer. As for the aspect of their indication—i.e., the aspect of the indication of the miracle to the truthfulness of the message—it is, upon investigation, like explicit attestation due to the habitual practice that God, exalted, creates after it necessary knowledge of his truthfulness, just as if a man stands in the assembly of a king in the presence of a group and claims that he is a messenger of this king to them, and they demand evidence from him, and he says: 'It is that this king should violate his habit and rise from his throne three times and sit down,' and he does so, then that would be an attestation for him and productive of necessary knowledge of his truthfulness without doubt. If it is said: This is an analogy and a comparison of the unseen to the witnessed, and even if a commonality is apparent, it is only considered in practical matters for producing opinion, and you have used it without a commonality to produce certainty in practical matters that are the foundation of establishing the laws—while obtaining knowledge in the example you mentioned is only through indications of circumstantial evidence. We say: The analogy is only for clarification and approximation, not for inference. And there is no role for witnessing circumstantial evidence in producing necessary knowledge, because it occurs for those absent from this assembly when the report reaches them by tawatur, and for those present if we suppose the king is in a room where there is no one else and behind him curtains that no one else can move, and the claimant of the message makes his evidence that the king will move those curtains immediately, and he does so. If it is said: Here there are possibilities that negate the indication of truthfulness and certainty in it, and they are types: First: the possibility that that act is not from God, but rather originates from the claimant due to a special quality in himself or a temperament in his body, or from his knowledge of properties in some bodies that he uses as a means to that, or originates from some angels or jinn, or from planetary conjunctions and celestial configurations known only to him, and so on from other causes. Second: the possibility that it is not a violation of custom, but rather a beginning of a custom that God willed to enact, or a repetition of a custom that only occurs in long eras, like the return of fixed stars to a specific point. Third: the possibility that it is something that could be challenged, but it was not challenged either because it did not reach someone capable of challenging it, or due to collusion, and agreement to elevate his word, or due to fear, or due to disdain and lack of concern, or being occupied with what is more important, or it was challenged but not transmitted due to an obstacle. Fourth: the possibility that it is not for the purpose of attestation, either because of the absence of purpose in His action according to the [correct] doctrine, or because of the existence of another purpose, such as being a grace for a responsible individual, or an answer to his prayer, or a miracle for another prophet, or a trial for the servant so that he may gain reward by refraining from acting on its implication or by examining and striving to refute it, as in the revelation of ambiguous [verses], or as a misguidance for creatures according to your doctrine that God misguides whom He wills among His servants. And by not conceding the negation of the possibilities, and [the claim that] the miracle is like an explicit statement from God that the claimant is truthful—it does not entail his truthfulness except after [establishing] the impossibility of lying in God's report, and there is no way to that through a transmitted proof due to circularity, nor through reason, because its utmost is that lying is ugly, and it is impossible for God, and the establishment of the two premises without a transmitted proof is subject to denial. So the answer, in summary, is that rational possibilities and conjectures do not contradict necessary, decisive ordinary sciences. So we are certain about obtaining knowledge of truthfulness after the appearance of the miracle, without paying attention to what was mentioned, neither by negation nor affirmation, just as happens in the aforementioned example, even if the king were unjust, tyrannical, lying, unconcerned with misleading his subjects and mocking his messengers. And in detail: First: we have shown that there is no effecter in existence except God alone, especially in such acts as reviving the dead, changing the staff into a serpent, splitting the moon, and the speaking of stone and clay. Moreover, mere enabling and leaving prevention on the part of the Wise, Powerful, Free Chooser is sufficient to produce the desired result. For this reason, the Mu'tazila held that the miracle is an act of God, or occurs by His command or by His enabling. (1) The miracle: a matter breaking the custom, calling to good and happiness, coupled with a claim of prophethood, intended to manifest the truthfulness of one who claims to be a messenger from God.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage is a detailed refutation of objections against the evidential value of miracles for establishing the truthfulness of a prophet. The author first addresses an objection concerning mutawatirat (traditions reported by many), stating that they are a type of necessary knowledge, so impugning them is not worthy of response. He then explains the indication of miracles: God habitually creates necessary knowledge of the prophet's truthfulness after witnessing a miracle, similar to the example of a man claiming to be a king's messenger and the king violating his habit by standing and sitting. An objection is raised that this analogy is inductive and only yields probability, not certainty, and that knowledge in the example relies on circumstantial evidence. The author responds that the analogy is merely illustrative, not inferential, and that necessary knowledge arises even without witnessing circumstances, as for those who hear the report by tawatur or see the king move curtains. Then a set of four rational possibilities is presented that could undermine the certainty: (1) the act may be due to natural causes, jinn, or celestial influences; (2) it may not be a true violation of custom but a new custom or rare occurrence; (3) it may be open to challenge but not challenged for various reasons; (4) it may have a purpose other than attestation, such as trial or misguidance. The objector also argues that the miracle being like an explicit statement from God would require establishing the impossibility of lying in God's report, which cannot be proven without circularity or by reason alone. The author's summary answer is that rational possibilities do not contradict ordinary necessary knowledge—we are certain despite them. In detail, he reiterates the Ash'ari principle that God alone is the effecter, especially in clear miracles like reviving the dead, and that mere permission and lack of prevention suffice. He notes that even the Mu'tazila agree the miracle is God's act or enabled by Him. The passage concludes with a definition of miracle (mu'jiza) as a custom-breaking act coupled with a claim of prophethood, intended to prove truthfulness.

Key Terms
معجزة
علم ضروري
تمثيل
متواترات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what kind of knowledge does a miracle produce about the prophet's truthfulness?
A) Probable knowledge based on analogy.
B) Necessary, certain knowledge (علم ضروري).
C) Knowledge that requires additional circumstantial evidence.
D) Knowledge that is only valid for those who witness it directly.

Key Takeaway
Miracles produce necessary, certain knowledge of a prophet's truthfulness, akin to a direct divine testimony.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If miracles produce certain knowledge, why do you still find yourself hesitating or doubting the truth of prophethood? What is that hesitation rooted in, and what would it take for you to say 'I know without any doubt'?
?
The author says the proof of prophethood is not a matter of opinion but of necessary knowledge. If you truly believed this, would your daily life look different? What is one decision you make today that shows you are still living as if the message is uncertain?
?
The curtain example shows that even a single, extraordinary act can create certainty. Have you ever experienced a moment where Allah's sign was so clear you couldn't deny it? What did you do with that certainty? Did it change you, or did you let it fade? Why?

Miracles
intermediate
8 min
The Four Rational Doubts About Miracles
The author enumerates four rational possibilities that could challenge the evidential value of miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will identify and understand the four rational possibilities that skeptics raise to undermine the certainty of miracles.

Core Idea
Skeptics propose four possibilities, but they do not negate the necessary knowledge.

After establishing the basic indication, the author presents a detailed objection listing four rational possibilities that could cast doubt on the miracle's divine origin, its custom-breaking nature, its uniqueness, or its purpose. This lesson examines each possibility carefully, as they are the core challenges that must be refuted.

Guided Path
1
Read the enumeration of the four possibilities
Read the passage from 'فإن قيل: ههنا احتمالات' to the end of the fourth possibility. Notice the structure: each possibility is numbered.
The possibilities are comprehensive.
2
Understand each possibility in detail
Go through each type: 1) natural or non-divine cause, 2) not a true custom-break, 3) could be challenged but wasn't, 4) purpose other than attestation.
Consider why each might seem plausible.
3
Reflect on the cumulative effect
Ask yourself: If any of these were true, would the miracle still prove prophethood? The author later answers that they do not negate certainty.
This sets up the next lesson's refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعن الثاني بأن المتواترات أحد أقسام الضروريات، فالقدح فيها بما ذكر مع أنه ظاهر الاندفاع لا يستحق الجواب. وأما وجه دلالتها أي وجه دلالة المعجزة على صدق الرسالة أنها عند التحقيق بمنزلة صريح التصديق لما جرت العادة به من أن الله تعالى يخلق عقيبها العلم الضروري بصدقه، كما إذا قام رجل في مجلس ملك بحضور جماعة، وادعى أنه رسول هذا الملك إليهم، فطالبوه بالحجة، فقال: هي أن يخالف هذا الملك عادته ويقوم عن سريره ثلاث مرات ويقعد ففعل، فإنه يكون تصديقاً له ومفيداً للعلم الضروري بصدقه من غير ارتياب.
فإن قيل: هذا تمثيل وقياس للغائب على الشاهد وهو على تقدير ظهور الجامع، إنما يعتبر في العمليات لإفادة الظن، وقد اعتبرتموه بلا جامع لإفادة اليقين في العمليات التي هي أساس ثبوت الشرائع على أن حصول العلم فيما ذكرتم من المثال إنما هو بشواهد من قرائن الأحوال.
قلنا: التمثيل إنما هو للتوضيح والتقريب دون الاستدلال، ولا مدخل لمشاهدة القرائن في إفادة العلم الضروري لحصوله للغائبين عن هذا المجلس عند تواتر القضية إليهم، وللحاضرين فيما إذا فرضنا الملك في بيت ليس فيه غيره ودونه حجب لا يقدر على تحريكها أحد سواه. وجعل مدعي الرسالة حجته أن الملك يحرك تلك الحجب من ساعته ففعل.
فإن قيل: ههنا احتمالات تنفي الدلالة على الصدق والجزم به، وهي أنواع: الأول: احتمال أن لا يكون ذلك الأمر من الله تعالى، بل يستند إلى المدعي بخاصية في نفسه أو مزاج في بدنه، أو لاطلاع منه على خواص في بعض الأجسام يتخذها ذريعة (۱) إلى ذلك، أو يستند إلى بعض الملائكة أو الجن، أو إلى اتصالات كوكبية وأوضاع فلكية، لا يطلع عليها غيره، إلى غير ذلك من الأسباب. الثاني: احتمال أن لا يكون خارقاً للعادة، بل ابتداء عادة أراد الله إجراءها أو تكرير عادة لا تكون إلا في دهور متطاولة، كعود الثوابت إلى نقطة معينة. الثالث: احتمال أن يكون مما يعارض إلا أنه لم يعارض لعدم بلوغه إلى من يقدر المعارضة، أو لمواضعة من القول، وموافقة في إعلاء كلمته، أو لخوف، أو لاستهانة وقلة مبالاة، أو لاشتغال بما هو أهم، أو عورض ولم ينقل لمانع. الرابع: احتمال أن لا يكون لغرض التصديق، إما لانتفاء الغرض في فعله على ما هو المذهب، وإما لثبوت غرض آخر مثل أن يكون لطفاً بمكلف، أو إجابة لدعوته أو معجزة لنبي آخر أو ابتلاء للعبد لينال الثواب بالتوقف عن موجبه أو النظر والاجتهاد في دفعه كما في إنزال المتشابه أو إضلالاً للخلق على ما هو المذهب عندكم من أن الله يضل من يشاء من عباده.
وبعدم تسليم انتفاء الاحتمالات، وكون المعجزة بمنزلة صريح القول من الله تعالى بأن المدعي صادق فهو لا يوجب صدقه إلا بعد استحالة الكذب في إخبار الله تعالى، ولا سبيل إلى ذلك بدليل السمع للزوم الدور، ولا بدليل العقل، لأن غايته أن الكذب قبيح، وهو على الله تعالى مستحيل، وثبوت المقدمتين بغير دليل السمع في حيز المنع.
فالجواب إجمالاً أن الاحتمالات والتجويزات العقلية لا تنافي العلوم العادية الضرورية القطعية، فنحن نقطع بحصول العلم بالصدق عقيب ظهور المعجزة من غير التفات إلى ما ذكر من لا بالنفي ولا بالإثبات، كما يحصل في المثال المذكور، وإن كان الملك ظلوماً غشوماً كذوباً لا يبالي بإغواء رعيته والاستهزاء برسله، وتفصيلاً: أولاً: أنا بينا أن لا مؤثر في الوجود إلا الله وحده، سيما في مثل إحياء الموتى، وانقلاب العصا حية، وانشقاق القمر، وسلام الحجر والمدر، على أن مجرد التمكين، وترك الدفع من قبل الحكيم القادر المختار كاف في إفادة المطلوب، ولهذا ذهب المعتزلة إلى أن المعجزة (۱) تكون فعلاً لله تعالى، أو واقعاً بأمره أو بتمكينه.
(۱) المعجزة: أمر خارق للعادة داعية إلى الخير والسعادة مقرونة بدعوى النبوة قصد به إظهار صدق من ادعى أنه رسول من الله تعالى.
And regarding the second [objection]: that mutawatirat are one of the divisions of necessary [knowledge]; therefore impugning them by what was mentioned, while it is obviously refutable, does not deserve an answer. As for the aspect of their indication—i.e., the aspect of the indication of the miracle to the truthfulness of the message—it is, upon investigation, like explicit attestation due to the habitual practice that God, exalted, creates after it necessary knowledge of his truthfulness, just as if a man stands in the assembly of a king in the presence of a group and claims that he is a messenger of this king to them, and they demand evidence from him, and he says: 'It is that this king should violate his habit and rise from his throne three times and sit down,' and he does so, then that would be an attestation for him and productive of necessary knowledge of his truthfulness without doubt. If it is said: This is an analogy and a comparison of the unseen to the witnessed, and even if a commonality is apparent, it is only considered in practical matters for producing opinion, and you have used it without a commonality to produce certainty in practical matters that are the foundation of establishing the laws—while obtaining knowledge in the example you mentioned is only through indications of circumstantial evidence. We say: The analogy is only for clarification and approximation, not for inference. And there is no role for witnessing circumstantial evidence in producing necessary knowledge, because it occurs for those absent from this assembly when the report reaches them by tawatur, and for those present if we suppose the king is in a room where there is no one else and behind him curtains that no one else can move, and the claimant of the message makes his evidence that the king will move those curtains immediately, and he does so. If it is said: Here there are possibilities that negate the indication of truthfulness and certainty in it, and they are types: First: the possibility that that act is not from God, but rather originates from the claimant due to a special quality in himself or a temperament in his body, or from his knowledge of properties in some bodies that he uses as a means to that, or originates from some angels or jinn, or from planetary conjunctions and celestial configurations known only to him, and so on from other causes. Second: the possibility that it is not a violation of custom, but rather a beginning of a custom that God willed to enact, or a repetition of a custom that only occurs in long eras, like the return of fixed stars to a specific point. Third: the possibility that it is something that could be challenged, but it was not challenged either because it did not reach someone capable of challenging it, or due to collusion, and agreement to elevate his word, or due to fear, or due to disdain and lack of concern, or being occupied with what is more important, or it was challenged but not transmitted due to an obstacle. Fourth: the possibility that it is not for the purpose of attestation, either because of the absence of purpose in His action according to the [correct] doctrine, or because of the existence of another purpose, such as being a grace for a responsible individual, or an answer to his prayer, or a miracle for another prophet, or a trial for the servant so that he may gain reward by refraining from acting on its implication or by examining and striving to refute it, as in the revelation of ambiguous [verses], or as a misguidance for creatures according to your doctrine that God misguides whom He wills among His servants. And by not conceding the negation of the possibilities, and [the claim that] the miracle is like an explicit statement from God that the claimant is truthful—it does not entail his truthfulness except after [establishing] the impossibility of lying in God's report, and there is no way to that through a transmitted proof due to circularity, nor through reason, because its utmost is that lying is ugly, and it is impossible for God, and the establishment of the two premises without a transmitted proof is subject to denial. So the answer, in summary, is that rational possibilities and conjectures do not contradict necessary, decisive ordinary sciences. So we are certain about obtaining knowledge of truthfulness after the appearance of the miracle, without paying attention to what was mentioned, neither by negation nor affirmation, just as happens in the aforementioned example, even if the king were unjust, tyrannical, lying, unconcerned with misleading his subjects and mocking his messengers. And in detail: First: we have shown that there is no effecter in existence except God alone, especially in such acts as reviving the dead, changing the staff into a serpent, splitting the moon, and the speaking of stone and clay. Moreover, mere enabling and leaving prevention on the part of the Wise, Powerful, Free Chooser is sufficient to produce the desired result. For this reason, the Mu'tazila held that the miracle is an act of God, or occurs by His command or by His enabling. (1) The miracle: a matter breaking the custom, calling to good and happiness, coupled with a claim of prophethood, intended to manifest the truthfulness of one who claims to be a messenger from God.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed refutation of objections against the evidential value of miracles for establishing the truthfulness of a prophet. The author first addresses an objection concerning mutawatirat (traditions reported by many), stating that they are a type of necessary knowledge, so impugning them is not worthy of response. He then explains the indication of miracles: God habitually creates necessary knowledge of the prophet's truthfulness after witnessing a miracle, similar to the example of a man claiming to be a king's messenger and the king violating his habit by standing and sitting. An objection is raised that this analogy is inductive and only yields probability, not certainty, and that knowledge in the example relies on circumstantial evidence. The author responds that the analogy is merely illustrative, not inferential, and that necessary knowledge arises even without witnessing circumstances, as for those who hear the report by tawatur or see the king move curtains. Then a set of four rational possibilities is presented that could undermine the certainty: (1) the act may be due to natural causes, jinn, or celestial influences; (2) it may not be a true violation of custom but a new custom or rare occurrence; (3) it may be open to challenge but not challenged for various reasons; (4) it may have a purpose other than attestation, such as trial or misguidance. The objector also argues that the miracle being like an explicit statement from God would require establishing the impossibility of lying in God's report, which cannot be proven without circularity or by reason alone. The author's summary answer is that rational possibilities do not contradict ordinary necessary knowledge—we are certain despite them. In detail, he reiterates the Ash'ari principle that God alone is the effecter, especially in clear miracles like reviving the dead, and that mere permission and lack of prevention suffice. He notes that even the Mu'tazila agree the miracle is God's act or enabled by Him. The passage concludes with a definition of miracle (mu'jiza) as a custom-breaking act coupled with a claim of prophethood, intended to prove truthfulness.

Key Terms
احتمالات
خارق للعادة
معارضة
غرض التصديق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if a miracle could have a natural explanation, it loses all evidential value.
Correction: The author acknowledges this as a possibility but will later show that necessary knowledge is not negated by such possibilities.

Checkpoint
How many rational possibilities does the objector list that could negate the indication of a miracle?
A) Two

Key Takeaway
Skeptics raise four rational possibilities, but they are only possibilities, not certainties that negate the necessary knowledge from miracles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a reported miracle that challenges your comfort zone, do you instinctively list alternative explanations like those in this passage to avoid the demand for certainty? What does that reveal about your willingness to submit to the truth?
?
The fourth possibility suggests a miracle could be a trial. In your own life, have you ever dismissed a clear sign from Allah because it was too uncomfortable to accept its implication? What would it take for you to act on that sign instead of rationalizing it away?
?
If you witnessed an undeniable miracle today, would you still allow the 'possibilities' in this passage to create doubt? What does your answer tell you about the state of your faith and your attachment to worldly reasoning?

The Final Refutation: Rational Doubts Cannot Undermine Certain Knowledge
The author provides the summary and detailed answer, emphasizing God's sole agency and the definition of miracle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's summary and detailed refutation of the rational possibilities, establishing that God alone is the true effecter and that miracles produce unshakeable certainty.

Core Idea
Rational possibilities do not negate necessary sciences; God is the sole effecter of miracles.

Having presented the four powerful objections, the author now delivers the decisive answer. He first gives a concise summary: rational possibilities do not contradict necessary, ordinary sciences. Then he provides a detailed theological argument, emphasizing that God alone is the effecter, especially in clear miracles like reviving the dead. He even notes that the Mu'tazila agree. Finally, he defines a miracle (mu'jiza) to cement the concept.

Guided Path
1
Read the summary answer
Read the passage from 'فالجواب إجمالاً' to the end of the summary. Focus on the statement that rational possibilities do not negate necessary sciences.
This is the core insight.
2
Study the detailed response
Read the detailed answer beginning with 'وتفصيلاً: أولاً'. Note the argument that God alone is the effecter, and that mere permission suffices.
This builds on Ash'ari theology.
3
Reflect on the definition of miracle
Read the final definition of mu'jiza. Understand how it encapsulates the entire discussion.
The definition includes key elements: custom-breaking, coupled with claim of prophethood, for attestation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعن الثاني بأن المتواترات أحد أقسام الضروريات، فالقدح فيها بما ذكر مع أنه ظاهر الاندفاع لا يستحق الجواب. وأما وجه دلالتها أي وجه دلالة المعجزة على صدق الرسالة أنها عند التحقيق بمنزلة صريح التصديق لما جرت العادة به من أن الله تعالى يخلق عقيبها العلم الضروري بصدقه، كما إذا قام رجل في مجلس ملك بحضور جماعة، وادعى أنه رسول هذا الملك إليهم، فطالبوه بالحجة، فقال: هي أن يخالف هذا الملك عادته ويقوم عن سريره ثلاث مرات ويقعد ففعل، فإنه يكون تصديقاً له ومفيداً للعلم الضروري بصدقه من غير ارتياب.
فإن قيل: هذا تمثيل وقياس للغائب على الشاهد وهو على تقدير ظهور الجامع، إنما يعتبر في العمليات لإفادة الظن، وقد اعتبرتموه بلا جامع لإفادة اليقين في العمليات التي هي أساس ثبوت الشرائع على أن حصول العلم فيما ذكرتم من المثال إنما هو بشواهد من قرائن الأحوال.
قلنا: التمثيل إنما هو للتوضيح والتقريب دون الاستدلال، ولا مدخل لمشاهدة القرائن في إفادة العلم الضروري لحصوله للغائبين عن هذا المجلس عند تواتر القضية إليهم، وللحاضرين فيما إذا فرضنا الملك في بيت ليس فيه غيره ودونه حجب لا يقدر على تحريكها أحد سواه. وجعل مدعي الرسالة حجته أن الملك يحرك تلك الحجب من ساعته ففعل.
فإن قيل: ههنا احتمالات تنفي الدلالة على الصدق والجزم به، وهي أنواع: الأول: احتمال أن لا يكون ذلك الأمر من الله تعالى، بل يستند إلى المدعي بخاصية في نفسه أو مزاج في بدنه، أو لاطلاع منه على خواص في بعض الأجسام يتخذها ذريعة (۱) إلى ذلك، أو يستند إلى بعض الملائكة أو الجن، أو إلى اتصالات كوكبية وأوضاع فلكية، لا يطلع عليها غيره، إلى غير ذلك من الأسباب. الثاني: احتمال أن لا يكون خارقاً للعادة، بل ابتداء عادة أراد الله إجراءها أو تكرير عادة لا تكون إلا في دهور متطاولة، كعود الثوابت إلى نقطة معينة. الثالث: احتمال أن يكون مما يعارض إلا أنه لم يعارض لعدم بلوغه إلى من يقدر المعارضة، أو لمواضعة من القول، وموافقة في إعلاء كلمته، أو لخوف، أو لاستهانة وقلة مبالاة، أو لاشتغال بما هو أهم، أو عورض ولم ينقل لمانع. الرابع: احتمال أن لا يكون لغرض التصديق، إما لانتفاء الغرض في فعله على ما هو المذهب، وإما لثبوت غرض آخر مثل أن يكون لطفاً بمكلف، أو إجابة لدعوته أو معجزة لنبي آخر أو ابتلاء للعبد لينال الثواب بالتوقف عن موجبه أو النظر والاجتهاد في دفعه كما في إنزال المتشابه أو إضلالاً للخلق على ما هو المذهب عندكم من أن الله يضل من يشاء من عباده.
وبعدم تسليم انتفاء الاحتمالات، وكون المعجزة بمنزلة صريح القول من الله تعالى بأن المدعي صادق فهو لا يوجب صدقه إلا بعد استحالة الكذب في إخبار الله تعالى، ولا سبيل إلى ذلك بدليل السمع للزوم الدور، ولا بدليل العقل، لأن غايته أن الكذب قبيح، وهو على الله تعالى مستحيل، وثبوت المقدمتين بغير دليل السمع في حيز المنع.
فالجواب إجمالاً أن الاحتمالات والتجويزات العقلية لا تنافي العلوم العادية الضرورية القطعية، فنحن نقطع بحصول العلم بالصدق عقيب ظهور المعجزة من غير التفات إلى ما ذكر من لا بالنفي ولا بالإثبات، كما يحصل في المثال المذكور، وإن كان الملك ظلوماً غشوماً كذوباً لا يبالي بإغواء رعيته والاستهزاء برسله، وتفصيلاً: أولاً: أنا بينا أن لا مؤثر في الوجود إلا الله وحده، سيما في مثل إحياء الموتى، وانقلاب العصا حية، وانشقاق القمر، وسلام الحجر والمدر، على أن مجرد التمكين، وترك الدفع من قبل الحكيم القادر المختار كاف في إفادة المطلوب، ولهذا ذهب المعتزلة إلى أن المعجزة (۱) تكون فعلاً لله تعالى، أو واقعاً بأمره أو بتمكينه.
(۱) المعجزة: أمر خارق للعادة داعية إلى الخير والسعادة مقرونة بدعوى النبوة قصد به إظهار صدق من ادعى أنه رسول من الله تعالى.
And regarding the second [objection]: that mutawatirat are one of the divisions of necessary [knowledge]; therefore impugning them by what was mentioned, while it is obviously refutable, does not deserve an answer. As for the aspect of their indication—i.e., the aspect of the indication of the miracle to the truthfulness of the message—it is, upon investigation, like explicit attestation due to the habitual practice that God, exalted, creates after it necessary knowledge of his truthfulness, just as if a man stands in the assembly of a king in the presence of a group and claims that he is a messenger of this king to them, and they demand evidence from him, and he says: 'It is that this king should violate his habit and rise from his throne three times and sit down,' and he does so, then that would be an attestation for him and productive of necessary knowledge of his truthfulness without doubt. If it is said: This is an analogy and a comparison of the unseen to the witnessed, and even if a commonality is apparent, it is only considered in practical matters for producing opinion, and you have used it without a commonality to produce certainty in practical matters that are the foundation of establishing the laws—while obtaining knowledge in the example you mentioned is only through indications of circumstantial evidence. We say: The analogy is only for clarification and approximation, not for inference. And there is no role for witnessing circumstantial evidence in producing necessary knowledge, because it occurs for those absent from this assembly when the report reaches them by tawatur, and for those present if we suppose the king is in a room where there is no one else and behind him curtains that no one else can move, and the claimant of the message makes his evidence that the king will move those curtains immediately, and he does so. If it is said: Here there are possibilities that negate the indication of truthfulness and certainty in it, and they are types: First: the possibility that that act is not from God, but rather originates from the claimant due to a special quality in himself or a temperament in his body, or from his knowledge of properties in some bodies that he uses as a means to that, or originates from some angels or jinn, or from planetary conjunctions and celestial configurations known only to him, and so on from other causes. Second: the possibility that it is not a violation of custom, but rather a beginning of a custom that God willed to enact, or a repetition of a custom that only occurs in long eras, like the return of fixed stars to a specific point. Third: the possibility that it is something that could be challenged, but it was not challenged either because it did not reach someone capable of challenging it, or due to collusion, and agreement to elevate his word, or due to fear, or due to disdain and lack of concern, or being occupied with what is more important, or it was challenged but not transmitted due to an obstacle. Fourth: the possibility that it is not for the purpose of attestation, either because of the absence of purpose in His action according to the [correct] doctrine, or because of the existence of another purpose, such as being a grace for a responsible individual, or an answer to his prayer, or a miracle for another prophet, or a trial for the servant so that he may gain reward by refraining from acting on its implication or by examining and striving to refute it, as in the revelation of ambiguous [verses], or as a misguidance for creatures according to your doctrine that God misguides whom He wills among His servants. And by not conceding the negation of the possibilities, and [the claim that] the miracle is like an explicit statement from God that the claimant is truthful—it does not entail his truthfulness except after [establishing] the impossibility of lying in God's report, and there is no way to that through a transmitted proof due to circularity, nor through reason, because its utmost is that lying is ugly, and it is impossible for God, and the establishment of the two premises without a transmitted proof is subject to denial. So the answer, in summary, is that rational possibilities and conjectures do not contradict necessary, decisive ordinary sciences. So we are certain about obtaining knowledge of truthfulness after the appearance of the miracle, without paying attention to what was mentioned, neither by negation nor affirmation, just as happens in the aforementioned example, even if the king were unjust, tyrannical, lying, unconcerned with misleading his subjects and mocking his messengers. And in detail: First: we have shown that there is no effecter in existence except God alone, especially in such acts as reviving the dead, changing the staff into a serpent, splitting the moon, and the speaking of stone and clay. Moreover, mere enabling and leaving prevention on the part of the Wise, Powerful, Free Chooser is sufficient to produce the desired result. For this reason, the Mu'tazila held that the miracle is an act of God, or occurs by His command or by His enabling. (1) The miracle: a matter breaking the custom, calling to good and happiness, coupled with a claim of prophethood, intended to manifest the truthfulness of one who claims to be a messenger from God.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage is a detailed refutation of objections against the evidential value of miracles for establishing the truthfulness of a prophet. The author first addresses an objection concerning mutawatirat (traditions reported by many), stating that they are a type of necessary knowledge, so impugning them is not worthy of response. He then explains the indication of miracles: God habitually creates necessary knowledge of the prophet's truthfulness after witnessing a miracle, similar to the example of a man claiming to be a king's messenger and the king violating his habit by standing and sitting. An objection is raised that this analogy is inductive and only yields probability, not certainty, and that knowledge in the example relies on circumstantial evidence. The author responds that the analogy is merely illustrative, not inferential, and that necessary knowledge arises even without witnessing circumstances, as for those who hear the report by tawatur or see the king move curtains. Then a set of four rational possibilities is presented that could undermine the certainty: (1) the act may be due to natural causes, jinn, or celestial influences; (2) it may not be a true violation of custom but a new custom or rare occurrence; (3) it may be open to challenge but not challenged for various reasons; (4) it may have a purpose other than attestation, such as trial or misguidance. The objector also argues that the miracle being like an explicit statement from God would require establishing the impossibility of lying in God's report, which cannot be proven without circularity or by reason alone. The author's summary answer is that rational possibilities do not contradict ordinary necessary knowledge—we are certain despite them. In detail, he reiterates the Ash'ari principle that God alone is the effecter, especially in clear miracles like reviving the dead, and that mere permission and lack of prevention suffice. He notes that even the Mu'tazila agree the miracle is God's act or enabled by Him. The passage concludes with a definition of miracle (mu'jiza) as a custom-breaking act coupled with a claim of prophethood, intended to prove truthfulness.

Key Terms
فالجواب إجمالاً
مؤثر
تمكين
خارق للعادة
داعية إلى الخير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's ability to lie makes miracles uncertain.
Correction: The author's detailed answer establishes that God does not lie in such contexts because the miracle is His act of enabling, and the habitual practice of creating knowledge after it is sufficient.

Checkpoint
What is the author's summary answer to the rational possibilities?
A) They must be examined individually to see which apply.
B) They do not contradict necessary, decisive ordinary sciences.
C) They show that miracles only provide probable knowledge.
D) They are all valid and thus miracles cannot prove prophethood.

Key Takeaway
Rational possibilities do not negate the certain knowledge produced by miracles, because God alone is the effecter and His habit of creating knowledge is unshakable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a clear sign from Allah—like the miracle of the Qur'an or an answered prayer—do you ever let a 'what if' doubt creep in? How does the author's statement that rational possibilities do not contradict necessary sciences challenge you to silence that whisper right now?
?
You claim 'لا مؤثر في الوجود إلا الله' with your tongue, but in your heart, do you truly depend on Allah alone for every outcome? Think of one area—your health, your wealth, your relationships—where you secretly rely on causes other than Allah. What concrete step will you take today to shift that reliance to Him?
?
The author says certainty arises after a miracle even if the miracle worker is evil. Have you ever rejected truth because of the imperfections of those who conveyed it? What would it cost you to accept the truth despite the vessel? Can you commit to seeking truth for its own sake, not for the sake of the messenger?

Definition of a Miracle (Mu'jiza)
Sanusi defines a miracle with five key elements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of a miracle in Islamic theology and its five essential components.

Core Idea
A miracle is an extraordinary event that confirms a prophetic claim and calls to goodness.

In this lesson, Imam al-Sanusi provides a precise definition of the term 'miracle' (mu'jiza). This definition is foundational for distinguishing genuine miracles from mere marvels or sorcery. Let's examine each element carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the English translation. Let the author's own wording come through before you interpret.
Notice that the definition is a single sentence with multiple clauses.
2
Identify the five components
Break down the definition into its five essential elements. List each one and write down what it contributes to the overall meaning.
The elements are: khāriq lil-ʿāda, dāʿiya ilā al-khayr, maqrūna bi-daʿwā al-nubuwwa, qaṣd iẓhār al-ṣidq, and the claimant being a messenger from Allah.
3
Reflect on the purpose
Ask yourself why each component is necessary. What would be missing if one element were removed?
This helps you appreciate the precision of the definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المعجزة : أمر خارق للعادة داعية إلى الخير والسعادة مقرونة بدعوى النبوة قصد به إظهار صدق من ادعى أنه رسول من الله تعالى.
The miracle: a matter that breaks the ordinary course of nature, calling to good and happiness, coupled with a claim of prophethood, intended to manifest the truthfulness of one who claims to be a messenger from God Almighty.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'mu'jiza' (miracle). The definition comprises five elements: (1) it is a matter that breaks the customary course of nature (khāriq lil-ʿāda); (2) it calls to good and happiness (dāʿiya ilā al-khayr wa al-saʿāda); (3) it is linked to a claim of prophethood (daʿwā al-nubuwwa); (4) its purpose is to demonstrate the truthfulness of the claimant (iẓhār ṣidq man iddaʿā annahu rasūl); (5) the claimant must be claiming to be a messenger from God. This definition distinguishes a genuine miracle from mere magic or sorcery, which may also be extraordinary but lack the moral and prophetic context.

Key Terms
معجزة
خارق للعادة
دعوى النبوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any extraordinary event is a miracle.
Correction: A miracle must be coupled with a prophetic claim, promote good, and serve to confirm truthfulness.

Checkpoint
According to the definition, which of the following is NOT a required element of a miracle?
A) It breaks the ordinary course of nature.
B) It calls to good and happiness.
C) It must be performed in public.
D) It is coupled with a claim of prophethood.

Key Takeaway
A true miracle is defined by five inseparable elements: it is extraordinary, good, linked to a prophetic claim, intended to verify that claim, and the claimant must be a messenger from God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If a miracle must call to good, examine your own desires for supernatural signs. When you pray for a miracle, are you sincerely seeking confirmation of truth and goodness, or are you merely seeking relief from difficulty? How can you align your request with the purpose of a true miracle?
?
The definition requires that a miracle be coupled with a prophetic claim. Have you ever accepted an extraordinary event as divine without verifying its source? What criteria do you use to test whether a spiritual experience is from Allah or from deception?
?
The purpose of a miracle is to show the truthfulness of a messenger. How does this affect your relationship with the Qur'an, which is itself a miracle? Do you approach the Qur'an as a sign demanding your response, or as mere recitation? What action will you take today to respond to its call?

Objections to Miracles: The Second, Third, and Fourth Probabilities
The author enumerates three rational objections to miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three main rational objections raised against miracles as decisive proof of prophethood.

Core Idea
Rational doubts do not undermine the certain knowledge from miracles.

Before defending the conclusiveness of miracles, the author presents the strongest objections that skeptics might raise. These objections try to explain away the miracle as something other than divine verification. Understanding them is essential to appreciate the refutations that follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the objections carefully
Read the Arabic text of the second, third, and fourth objections. Notice how each one presents a different way to explain the miracle without accepting it as proof.
The first objection is not included here; the text begins with the second.
2
Identify the structure of each objection
Each objection begins with 'احتمال' (probability). Separate them in your mind and note the key phrase for each.
3
Reflect on the fourth objection's sub-points
The fourth objection is long and includes multiple alternative purposes. List them and see how they try to avoid the miracle's purpose of verification.
This objection also challenges the assumption that a miracle is like an explicit statement from God.
4
Ask yourself: why list these objections?
Consider why the author spends time on mere probabilities. How does this set up the need for a strong refutation?
This connects to the learning objective of understanding the challenges.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : احتمال أن لا يكون خارقاً للعادة، بل ابتداء عادة أراد الله إجراءها أو تكرير عادة لا تكون إلا في دهور متطاولة، كعود الثوابت إلى نقطة معينة . الثالث : احتمال أن يكون مما يعارض إلا أنه لم يعارض لعدم بلوغه إلى من يقدر المعارضة، أو لمواضعة من القول، وموافقة في إعلاء كلمته، أو لخوف، أو لاستهانة وقلة مبالاة، أو لاشتغال بما هو أهم ، أو عورض ولم ينقل لمانع . الرابع : احتمال أن لا يكون لغرض التصديق، إما لانتفاء الغرض في فعله على ما هو المذهب، وإما لثبوت غرض آخر مثل ان يكون لطفاً بمكلف، أو إجابة لدعوته أو معجزة لنبي آخر أو ابتلاء للعبد لينال الثواب بالتوقف عن موجبه أو النظر والاجتهاد في دفعه كما في إنزال المتشابه أو إضلالاً للخلق على ما هو المذهب عندكم من أن الله يضل من يشاء من عباده، وبعدم تسليم انتفاء الاحتمالات، وكون المعجزة بمنزلة صريح القول من الله تعالى بأن المدعي صادق فهو لا يوجب صدقه إلا بعد استحالة الكذب في إخبار الله تعالى، ولا سبيل إلى ذلك بدليل السمع للزوم الدور، ولا بدليل العقل، لأن غايته أن الكذب قبيح، وهو على الله تعالى مستحيل، وثبوت المقدمتين بغير دليل السمع في حيز المنع . فالجواب إجمالاً أن الاحتمالات والتجويزات العقلية لا تنافي العلوم العادية الضرورية القطعية، فنحن نقطع بحصول العلم بالصدق عقيب ظهور المعجزة من غير التفات إلى ما ذكر من ! لا بالنفي ولا بالإثبات، كما يحصل في المثال المذكور، وإن كان الملك ظلوماً غشوماً كذوباً لا يبالي بإغواء رعيته والاستهزاء برسله، وتفصيلاً أولاً : أنا بينا أن لا مؤثر في الوجود إلا الله وحده ، سيما في مثل إحياء الموتى ، وانقلاب العصا حية، وانشقاق القمر، وسلام الحجر والمدر، على أن مجرد التمكين، وترك الدفع من قبل الحكيم القادر المختار كاف في إفادة المطلوب، ولهذا ذهب المعتزلة إلى ان المعجزة تكون فعلاً لله تعالى، أو واقعاً بأمره أو بتمكينه . وثانياً : أن كلامنا فيما حصل الجزم بأنه خارق للعادة، وأن المتحدين عجزوا عن معارضته مع كونهم أحق بها إن أمكنت لكثرة اشتغالهم بما يناسب ذلك، وكمالهم فيه وفرط اهتمامهم بالمعارضة ، وتوفر دواعيهم . ولهذا كانت معجزة كل نبي من جنس ما غلب على أهل زمانه وتهالكوا عليه وتفاخروا به، كالسحر في زمن موسى عليه السلام والطب في زمن عيسى والموسيقى في زمن داود والفصاحة في زمن محمد (صلى الله عليه وسلم). وثالثاً : أنه لا خفاء ولا خلاف في ترتيب الغايات والآثار على بعض أفعاله وإن لم يجعلها أغراضاً له على أنا لا نقول إنه فعل المعجزة لغرض التصديق، بل إنها دلت على تصديق من الله تعالى قائم بذاته سواء جعل من جنس العلم أو كلام النفس، أو غيرهما . ورابعاً: أن ظهور المعجزة على يد الكاذب لأي غرض فرض وإن جاز عقلاً بناء على شمول قدرة الله ، فهو ممتنع عادة معلوم الانتفاء، قطعاً، كما هو حكم سائر العاديات، وهذا ما قال القاضي: إن اقتران ظهور المعجزة بالصدق أحد العاديات، فإذا جوزنا انحرافها عن مجراها جاز إخلاء المعجزة عن اعتقاد الصدق، وحينئذ يجوز إظهاره على يد الكاذب، وإما بدون ذلك فلا ، لاستحالة العلم بصدق الكاذب، ومنا من قال باستحالته عقلاً، فالشيخ لافضائه إلى التعجيز عن إقامة الدلالة على صدق دعوى الرسالة، والإمام وكثير من المتكلمين، لأن الصدق مدلول بها لازم بمنزلة العلم لإتقان الفعل، فلو ظهرت من الكاذب، لزم كونه صادقاً كاذباً، وهو محال، والماتريدية لإيجابه المسوية بين الصادق والكاذب، وعدم التفرقة بين النبي والمتنبي، وهو سفه لا يليق بالحكيم . وخامساً: أن مجرد إظهار المعجزة على يده يفيدنا العلم بصدقة، وبتصديق الله إياه من غير افتقار إلى اعتبار كلام وإخبار، ومن هنا يصح التمسك بخبر النبي في إثبات الكلام، وامتناع الكذب والنقص على ما مر وإلى هذا يشير ما قال إمام الحرمين، أنا نجعل إظهار المعجزة تصديقاً بمنزلة أن يقول: جعلته رسولاً، وأنشأت الرسالة فيه كقولك : جعلتك وكيلاً، واستنبتك بشأن من غير قصد إلى
Second: the probability that it is not extraordinary but rather an initial custom that God willed to execute or a repetition of a custom that only occurs in long epochs, like the return of fixed stars to a specific point. Third: the probability that it is something that could be opposed but was not opposed due to not reaching one who can oppose it, or due to collusion in speech, agreement in elevating his word, or fear, or disdain and lack of concern, or preoccupation with what is more important, or it was opposed but not transmitted due to an impediment. Fourth: the probability that it is not for the purpose of verification, either because of the absence of purpose in His action according to the [Ash'ari] doctrine, or because of the existence of another purpose such as being a grace for a morally responsible person, or an answer to his supplication, or a miracle for another prophet, or a trial for the servant to attain reward by refraining from acting upon its implication or by examining and striving to rebut it, as in the revelation of ambiguous verses, or leading people astray according to your doctrine that God leads astray whom He wills of His servants, and by not conceding the negation of the probabilities, and that the miracle is like an explicit statement from God that the claimant is truthful, so it does not necessitate his truthfulness except after the impossibility of lying in God's reporting, and there is no way to that through transmitted evidence due to circularity, nor through reason, because at most lying is evil, and it is impossible for God, and establishing both premises without transmitted evidence is in the realm of denial. So the answer in summary: that probabilities and rational possibilities do not contradict necessary decisive ordinary knowledge; so we are certain of attaining knowledge of truthfulness upon the appearance of the miracle without paying attention to what was mentioned... neither by negation nor affirmation, as occurs in the mentioned example, even if the king is unjust, oppressive, deceitful, unconcerned with misleading his subjects and mocking his messengers. And in detail: First: we have shown that there is no effect in existence except God alone, especially in such things as reviving the dead, turning the staff into a snake, splitting the moon, and greeting of stones and clods, and that mere enabling and leaving off prevention from the Wise, Powerful, Free Agent is sufficient for producing the desired result, and for this the Mu'tazila held that the miracle is an act of God, or occurs by His command or by His enabling. Second: our discussion concerns what is determined to be extraordinary and that the challengers are unable to oppose it while they are most entitled to it if it were possible, due to their frequent occupation with what suits that, their perfection in it, their extreme concern for opposition, and their abundant motives. For this reason, the miracle of every prophet was of the kind that dominated the people of his time and upon which they fiercely competed and boasted, like magic in the time of Moses (peace be upon him), medicine in the time of Jesus, music in the time of David, and eloquence in the time of Muhammad (may God bless him and grant him peace). Third: there is no concealment nor disagreement regarding the ordering of ends and effects upon some of His actions even if He does not make them purposes for Himself, yet we do not say that He performed the miracle for the purpose of verification, but rather that it indicates a verification from God inherent in His essence, whether it is considered as knowledge or inner speech or otherwise. Fourth: the appearance of a miracle at the hand of a liar for any assumed purpose, even if rationally possible based on the comprehensiveness of God's power, is customarily impossible and known to be non-existent, certainly, just as is the case for all customary matters. This is what al-Qadi said: that the coupling of the appearance of the miracle with truthfulness is one of the customary things; if we permit its deviation from its course, it becomes permissible for the miracle to be devoid of belief in truthfulness, and then it is permissible for it to be shown at the hand of a liar. Otherwise, no, because of the impossibility of knowing the truthfulness of a liar. Among us, some have said it is rationally impossible: al-Shaykh [al-Ash'ari] because it leads to the inability to establish evidence for the truth of the claim of prophethood; al-Imam [al-Juwayni] and many theologians because truthfulness is implied by it as a necessary concomitant akin to knowledge due to the perfection of the act, so if it appeared from a liar, it would necessitate him being both truthful and lying, which is impossible; and the Maturidis because it necessitates equality between the truthful and the liar, and the lack of distinction between the prophet and the claimant to prophethood, which is foolishness unbefitting the Wise. Fifth: the mere appearance of the miracle at his hand provides us with knowledge of his truthfulness and of God's verification of him, without needing to consider speech or report. Hence, it is valid to rely on the prophet's report to establish speech, the impossibility of lying, and deficiency, as has passed. And to this refers what Imam al-Haramayn said: we make the appearance of the miracle a verification in the manner of saying: 'I have made him a messenger,' and the establishment of prophethood in him is like your saying: 'I have made you a deputy,' and I appointed you for a matter without intending to...
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This lengthy passage presents a series of objections (probabilities) against the conclusiveness of miracles as proofs of prophethood, followed by a summary response and five detailed refutations. The author begins by listing four possible objections: (1) The miracle might not be truly extraordinary but merely a rare custom initiated by God; (2) It might be open to opposition but was not opposed due to various reasons; (3) Its purpose might not be verification but something else; (4) Denial of the probabilities and the claim that a miracle is like an explicit statement from God, which only proves truth after establishing the impossibility of lying in God's reports—this cannot be proven by transmitted evidence (circular) nor reason. Then the summary answer: rational probabilities do not undermine necessary, certain, ordinary knowledge. We are certain of truth after a miracle just as we are certain in ordinary cases. Then five detailed answers: First, only God is the true agent, and His enabling alone suffices. Second, our discussion is about miracles that are known to be extraordinary and that the challengers are unable to oppose, with their strong motives. Miracles match the expertise of the age. Third, the miracle indicates divine verification inherently, not necessarily as a purpose. Fourth, a miracle at the hand of a liar is customarily impossible, and various opinions on its rational impossibility are discussed (Ash'ari, Juwayni, Maturidi). Fifth, the mere appearance of the miracle gives certain knowledge of truth without need for a verbal report. The passage ends with a quote from Imam al-Haramayn comparing the miracle to an explicit appointment.

Key Terms
خارق للعادة
عاديات
تصديق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any rare event is automatically a miracle and proves prophethood.
Correction: The objections show that rational alternatives exist (e.g., rare custom, lack of opposition, alternative purpose) that prevent immediate certainty.

Checkpoint
What is the main focus of the third objection?
A) The miracle might be a rare custom.
B) The miracle might have an alternative purpose.
C) The miracle could have been opposed but wasn't.
D) The miracle cannot prove truth because God's lies are impossible.

Key Takeaway
Before any argument for miracles, one must address the rational probabilities that could explain away their demonstrative force.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear of a miracle, which of these probabilities immediately arises in your heart? Do you instinctively seek to rationalize it away? Ask yourself: is your hesitation rooted in genuine intellectual caution or in a fear of submitting to the truth?
?
The skeptics argued that a miracle might be a trial to test your response. If you were confronted with an undeniable sign, would you still find excuses to withhold belief? What is standing between you and full submission?
?
The fourth probability suggests that miracles might not be for verification but for other purposes. Are you willing to accept that God’s actions may not fit your expectations, or do you demand that every sign be exactly what you deem logical? Examine your heart: do you trust God's wisdom even when it defies your reasoning?

Miracles

Summary Answer and First Refutation: Certainty Beyond Doubts
The author gives a summary answer and first detailed refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's summary response that rational possibilities do not negate certain knowledge, and the first detailed refutation that only God is the true agent of miracles.

Core Idea
Theoretical possibilities do not undermine our certain, ordinary knowledge.

After listing the objections, the author gives a concise summary answer: rational possibilities do not shake our necessary, certain knowledge. Then he begins the detailed refutation by establishing that only God is the true agent, especially in extraordinary events. This first argument sets the foundation for the subsequent refutations.

Guided Path
1
Read the summary answer
Read the first part: 'فالجواب إجمالاً أن الاحتمالات والتجويزات العقلية لا تنافي العلوم العادية الضرورية القطعية'. Understand that the author claims we have certain knowledge that overrides mere possibilities.
Notice the analogy with ordinary cases like the example of the king.
2
Identify the first detailed refutation
Then read the first detailed point: 'أولاً: أنا بينا أن لا مؤثر في الوجود إلا الله وحده'. See how it asserts divine agency.
3
Connect to Mu'tazila view
Note the mention of Mu'tazila who also agree that miracles are acts of God. This shows common ground.
4
Reflect on the purpose
Ask yourself: how does establishing that only God is the true agent help refute the objections?
This is the key link to the learning objective.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : احتمال أن لا يكون خارقاً للعادة، بل ابتداء عادة أراد الله إجراءها أو تكرير عادة لا تكون إلا في دهور متطاولة، كعود الثوابت إلى نقطة معينة . الثالث : احتمال أن يكون مما يعارض إلا أنه لم يعارض لعدم بلوغه إلى من يقدر المعارضة، أو لمواضعة من القول، وموافقة في إعلاء كلمته، أو لخوف، أو لاستهانة وقلة مبالاة، أو لاشتغال بما هو أهم ، أو عورض ولم ينقل لمانع . الرابع : احتمال أن لا يكون لغرض التصديق، إما لانتفاء الغرض في فعله على ما هو المذهب، وإما لثبوت غرض آخر مثل ان يكون لطفاً بمكلف، أو إجابة لدعوته أو معجزة لنبي آخر أو ابتلاء للعبد لينال الثواب بالتوقف عن موجبه أو النظر والاجتهاد في دفعه كما في إنزال المتشابه أو إضلالاً للخلق على ما هو المذهب عندكم من أن الله يضل من يشاء من عباده، وبعدم تسليم انتفاء الاحتمالات، وكون المعجزة بمنزلة صريح القول من الله تعالى بأن المدعي صادق فهو لا يوجب صدقه إلا بعد استحالة الكذب في إخبار الله تعالى، ولا سبيل إلى ذلك بدليل السمع للزوم الدور، ولا بدليل العقل، لأن غايته أن الكذب قبيح، وهو على الله تعالى مستحيل، وثبوت المقدمتين بغير دليل السمع في حيز المنع . فالجواب إجمالاً أن الاحتمالات والتجويزات العقلية لا تنافي العلوم العادية الضرورية القطعية، فنحن نقطع بحصول العلم بالصدق عقيب ظهور المعجزة من غير التفات إلى ما ذكر من ! لا بالنفي ولا بالإثبات، كما يحصل في المثال المذكور، وإن كان الملك ظلوماً غشوماً كذوباً لا يبالي بإغواء رعيته والاستهزاء برسله، وتفصيلاً أولاً : أنا بينا أن لا مؤثر في الوجود إلا الله وحده ، سيما في مثل إحياء الموتى ، وانقلاب العصا حية، وانشقاق القمر، وسلام الحجر والمدر، على أن مجرد التمكين، وترك الدفع من قبل الحكيم القادر المختار كاف في إفادة المطلوب، ولهذا ذهب المعتزلة إلى ان المعجزة تكون فعلاً لله تعالى، أو واقعاً بأمره أو بتمكينه . وثانياً : أن كلامنا فيما حصل الجزم بأنه خارق للعادة، وأن المتحدين عجزوا عن معارضته مع كونهم أحق بها إن أمكنت لكثرة اشتغالهم بما يناسب ذلك، وكمالهم فيه وفرط اهتمامهم بالمعارضة ، وتوفر دواعيهم . ولهذا كانت معجزة كل نبي من جنس ما غلب على أهل زمانه وتهالكوا عليه وتفاخروا به، كالسحر في زمن موسى عليه السلام والطب في زمن عيسى والموسيقى في زمن داود والفصاحة في زمن محمد (صلى الله عليه وسلم). وثالثاً : أنه لا خفاء ولا خلاف في ترتيب الغايات والآثار على بعض أفعاله وإن لم يجعلها أغراضاً له على أنا لا نقول إنه فعل المعجزة لغرض التصديق، بل إنها دلت على تصديق من الله تعالى قائم بذاته سواء جعل من جنس العلم أو كلام النفس، أو غيرهما . ورابعاً: أن ظهور المعجزة على يد الكاذب لأي غرض فرض وإن جاز عقلاً بناء على شمول قدرة الله ، فهو ممتنع عادة معلوم الانتفاء، قطعاً، كما هو حكم سائر العاديات، وهذا ما قال القاضي: إن اقتران ظهور المعجزة بالصدق أحد العاديات، فإذا جوزنا انحرافها عن مجراها جاز إخلاء المعجزة عن اعتقاد الصدق، وحينئذ يجوز إظهاره على يد الكاذب، وإما بدون ذلك فلا ، لاستحالة العلم بصدق الكاذب، ومنا من قال باستحالته عقلاً، فالشيخ لافضائه إلى التعجيز عن إقامة الدلالة على صدق دعوى الرسالة، والإمام وكثير من المتكلمين، لأن الصدق مدلول بها لازم بمنزلة العلم لإتقان الفعل، فلو ظهرت من الكاذب، لزم كونه صادقاً كاذباً، وهو محال، والماتريدية لإيجابه المسوية بين الصادق والكاذب، وعدم التفرقة بين النبي والمتنبي، وهو سفه لا يليق بالحكيم . وخامساً: أن مجرد إظهار المعجزة على يده يفيدنا العلم بصدقة، وبتصديق الله إياه من غير افتقار إلى اعتبار كلام وإخبار، ومن هنا يصح التمسك بخبر النبي في إثبات الكلام، وامتناع الكذب والنقص على ما مر وإلى هذا يشير ما قال إمام الحرمين، أنا نجعل إظهار المعجزة تصديقاً بمنزلة أن يقول: جعلته رسولاً، وأنشأت الرسالة فيه كقولك : جعلتك وكيلاً، واستنبتك بشأن من غير قصد إلى
Second: the probability that it is not extraordinary but rather an initial custom that God willed to execute or a repetition of a custom that only occurs in long epochs, like the return of fixed stars to a specific point. Third: the probability that it is something that could be opposed but was not opposed due to not reaching one who can oppose it, or due to collusion in speech, agreement in elevating his word, or fear, or disdain and lack of concern, or preoccupation with what is more important, or it was opposed but not transmitted due to an impediment. Fourth: the probability that it is not for the purpose of verification, either because of the absence of purpose in His action according to the [Ash'ari] doctrine, or because of the existence of another purpose such as being a grace for a morally responsible person, or an answer to his supplication, or a miracle for another prophet, or a trial for the servant to attain reward by refraining from acting upon its implication or by examining and striving to rebut it, as in the revelation of ambiguous verses, or leading people astray according to your doctrine that God leads astray whom He wills of His servants, and by not conceding the negation of the probabilities, and that the miracle is like an explicit statement from God that the claimant is truthful, so it does not necessitate his truthfulness except after the impossibility of lying in God's reporting, and there is no way to that through transmitted evidence due to circularity, nor through reason, because at most lying is evil, and it is impossible for God, and establishing both premises without transmitted evidence is in the realm of denial. So the answer in summary: that probabilities and rational possibilities do not contradict necessary decisive ordinary knowledge; so we are certain of attaining knowledge of truthfulness upon the appearance of the miracle without paying attention to what was mentioned... neither by negation nor affirmation, as occurs in the mentioned example, even if the king is unjust, oppressive, deceitful, unconcerned with misleading his subjects and mocking his messengers. And in detail: First: we have shown that there is no effect in existence except God alone, especially in such things as reviving the dead, turning the staff into a snake, splitting the moon, and greeting of stones and clods, and that mere enabling and leaving off prevention from the Wise, Powerful, Free Agent is sufficient for producing the desired result, and for this the Mu'tazila held that the miracle is an act of God, or occurs by His command or by His enabling. Second: our discussion concerns what is determined to be extraordinary and that the challengers are unable to oppose it while they are most entitled to it if it were possible, due to their frequent occupation with what suits that, their perfection in it, their extreme concern for opposition, and their abundant motives. For this reason, the miracle of every prophet was of the kind that dominated the people of his time and upon which they fiercely competed and boasted, like magic in the time of Moses (peace be upon him), medicine in the time of Jesus, music in the time of David, and eloquence in the time of Muhammad (may God bless him and grant him peace). Third: there is no concealment nor disagreement regarding the ordering of ends and effects upon some of His actions even if He does not make them purposes for Himself, yet we do not say that He performed the miracle for the purpose of verification, but rather that it indicates a verification from God inherent in His essence, whether it is considered as knowledge or inner speech or otherwise. Fourth: the appearance of a miracle at the hand of a liar for any assumed purpose, even if rationally possible based on the comprehensiveness of God's power, is customarily impossible and known to be non-existent, certainly, just as is the case for all customary matters. This is what al-Qadi said: that the coupling of the appearance of the miracle with truthfulness is one of the customary things; if we permit its deviation from its course, it becomes permissible for the miracle to be devoid of belief in truthfulness, and then it is permissible for it to be shown at the hand of a liar. Otherwise, no, because of the impossibility of knowing the truthfulness of a liar. Among us, some have said it is rationally impossible: al-Shaykh [al-Ash'ari] because it leads to the inability to establish evidence for the truth of the claim of prophethood; al-Imam [al-Juwayni] and many theologians because truthfulness is implied by it as a necessary concomitant akin to knowledge due to the perfection of the act, so if it appeared from a liar, it would necessitate him being both truthful and lying, which is impossible; and the Maturidis because it necessitates equality between the truthful and the liar, and the lack of distinction between the prophet and the claimant to prophethood, which is foolishness unbefitting the Wise. Fifth: the mere appearance of the miracle at his hand provides us with knowledge of his truthfulness and of God's verification of him, without needing to consider speech or report. Hence, it is valid to rely on the prophet's report to establish speech, the impossibility of lying, and deficiency, as has passed. And to this refers what Imam al-Haramayn said: we make the appearance of the miracle a verification in the manner of saying: 'I have made him a messenger,' and the establishment of prophethood in him is like your saying: 'I have made you a deputy,' and I appointed you for a matter without intending to...

Misconception & Correction

Checkpoint
According to the summary answer, what type of knowledge does the miracle provide?
A) Probable knowledge
B) Necessary certain ordinary knowledge
C) Speculative knowledge
D) Knowledge from transmitted evidence

Key Takeaway
Certainty from miracles is based on ordinary necessary knowledge, not on eliminating all theoretical possibilities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face doubts about your faith, do you allow theoretical possibilities to shake your certainty, or do you hold fast to the necessary knowledge God has placed in your heart? What would it take for you to trust that certainty despite intellectual objections?
?
The author says that even if a king is unjust and deceitful, the miracle still gives certainty. Is there a person or authority in your life whose flaws you allow to undermine your trust in clear signs from God? How will you guard against that?
?
The first refutation reminds us that only God is the true agent. Do you live as if God alone controls outcomes, or do you secretly attribute effective power to created causes? What would change in your daily life if you truly believed 'la mu'aththira fi al-wujud illa Allah'?

Miracles
