Objection from Moses' Prayer and Its Refutation
Objection from Moses' prayer and its refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that the Quran's challenge is contradicted by the story of Moses is refuted through two key arguments.

Core Idea
The story of Moses does not fulfill the Quran's challenge because the wording is not identical.

This passage addresses a clever objection: if the Quran challenges humans and jinn to produce a surah of at least three verses, then doesn't Moses' prayer of eleven verses, which appears in the Quran, count as such a production? The author refutes this by distinguishing between the 'related speech' (al-mahki) and the Quran's own composition, and by noting that the challenge may be limited to long or medium surahs.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The opponent points to the Quran's story of Moses praying 'Rabbi ishrah li sadri...' and argues this is a surah-length passage that would meet the challenge.
The shortest surah is three verses, and Moses' prayer is about eleven verses.
2
Understand the first refutation
The author says the reported speech (al-mahki) does not have to be in this exact composition (nazm). That is, the Quran quotes Moses but not necessarily in the exact wording Moses used, so it doesn't serve as a counterexample.
The inimitability is in the unique nazm of the Quran, not in the content of quotations.
3
Consider the second refutation
Some scholars hold that the challenge applies only to a long surah (min al-tiwal) or ten medium verses (min al-awsat). A short prayer like Moses' would not fall under the challenge.
This limits the scope of the challenge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أن فيه مما يكذبه، حيث أخبر بأنه لا يتيسر للبشر والجن بل الانس والجن لا يأتيان بمثل سورة منه وأقل السور ثلاث آيات. ثم حكى عن موسى مع اعترافه بأن هارون منه أفصح مقدار إحدى عشرة آية منه وهي : رب اشرح لي صدري ويسر لي أمري إلى قوله : ( إنك كنت بنا بصيراً). ورد بأن المحكى لا يلزم أن يكون لهذا النظم بعينه، على أن المختار عند البعض في المتحدى به سورة من الطوال أو عشر من الأوساط
And among them is that there is in it [the Quran] what belies it, for it informs that it is not possible for humans and jinn—rather, humans and jinn—to bring the like of a surah from it, and the shortest surah is three verses. Then it related from Moses, while it acknowledges that Aaron was more eloquent than him, approximately eleven verses from it, namely: 'My Lord, expand for me my breast and ease for me my task' up to His saying: 'Indeed, You have ever been, of us, Seeing.' It is refuted by [saying] that the related [speech] does not necessarily have to be in this exact composition, and that the choice according to some in what is challenged with is a long surah or ten medium [verses].
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection: the Quran challenges mankind and jinn to produce even a single surah, but it itself contains a story where Moses prays a prayer that is about eleven verses long. The opponent argues that if the shortest surah is three verses, and Moses' prayer is longer, then the challenge is already fulfilled by Moses? The refutation explains that the speech attributed to Moses in the Quran is not necessarily in that exact verbal composition (nazm), so it does not serve as a counter-example. Moreover, some scholars hold that the challenge applies specifically to a long surah (min al-tiwal) or ten medium verses (min al-awsat), so a short prayer does not fall under the challenge.

Key Terms

سورة

محكى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any passage in the Quran, including quoted speech, could be imitated, thereby undermining the challenge.
Correction: The challenge targets the unique composition (nazm) of the Quran, not the content of reported speech. Quoted material is not required to be in that exact nazm, so it does not serve as a fulfillment.

Checkpoint
Why does the story of Moses' prayer not invalidate the Quran's challenge?
A) Because Moses was a prophet and his speech is also miraculous.
B) Because the exact wording of the prayer is not necessarily the same as the Quranic expression.
C) Because the prayer is longer than the shortest surah.
D) Because the challenge was only for disbelievers.

Key Takeaway
The story of Moses does not invalidate the Quran's challenge because the reported speech is not required to be in its original wording, and the challenge targets specific lengths of surahs.

Objection of Ambiguous Verses and Its Refutation
Sanusi presents the objection that the Quran has ambiguous verses and gives the refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the Quran contains ambiguous verses used by heretics and the scholarly refutation explaining their divine purposes.

Core Idea
Ambiguous verses are not flaws; they are opportunities for reward and deeper knowledge.

The fourth objection against the miraculous nature of the Quran is the presence of ambiguous verses (mutashabihat) that heretical groups, like the anthropomorphists (mujassima), misuse to support their claims. In this passage, Imam al-Sanusi presents the objection and offers a clear refutation, showing that such verses serve beneficial purposes: they reward those who exert intellectual effort (ijtihad) and encourage reliance on scholars firmly grounded in knowledge (al-rasikhun fi al-ilm).

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and example
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the objector cites the verse 'al-Rahmanu 'ala al-'arshi istawa' as an example of ambiguity used by anthropomorphists.
The verse is often taken literally by some groups, but scholars interpret it figuratively.
2
Identify the refutation's two points
The refutation provides two purposes for ambiguous verses: (1) to attain reward through nazar (examination) and ijtihad (effort), and (2) to have countless benefits by returning to al-rasikhun fi al-ilm.
Both points emphasize that ambiguity is intentional and beneficial.
3
Reflect on the role of scholars
Consider why al-rasikhun fi al-ilm are mentioned. Their role is crucial for correctly interpreting ambiguous verses and preventing misguidance.
This underscores the importance of scholarly authority in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أن فيه متشابهات يتمسك بها أهل الغواية كالمجسمة بمثل : الرَّحْمَنُ عَلَى الْعَرْشِ اسْتَوَى . ورد بأنها لنيل المثوبة بالنظر والاجتهاد في طلب المراد، أو لفوائد لا تحصى بالرجوع إلى الراسخين في العلم
And among them is that it contains ambiguous verses (mutashabihat) that the people of misguidance cling to, like the anthropomorphists (mujassima) with examples such as: 'The Most Merciful [is] above the Throne established' [Quran 20:5]. It is refuted by [saying] that they are for attaining reward through examination (nazar) and exertion (ijtihad) in seeking the intended meaning, or for countless benefits by returning to those firmly grounded in knowledge (al-rasikhun fi al-ilm).
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection: the Quran contains ambiguous verses (mutashabihat) that heretical groups, such as the anthropomorphists (mujassima), use to support their claims—for example, the verse 'al-Rahmanu 'ala al-'arshi istawa.' The objector implies this is a flaw. The refutation gives two purposes for such verses: (1) they are a means to attain divine reward (al-mathuba) by engaging in intellectual examination (nazar) and ijtihad to arrive at the intended figurative meaning, and (2) there are countless benefits in having such verses, especially by referring back to al-rasikhun fi al-ilm (those firmly grounded in knowledge), who interpret them correctly. Thus, ambiguity is not a defect but a feature for spiritual growth and reliance on scholars.

Correction: Islamic scholarship views them as opportunities for reward through ijtihad and for deeper understanding when consulting scholars.

Checkpoint

A) To mislead people deliberately.
B) To provide a reward for those who exert effort (ijtihad) in understanding them.
C) To support anthropomorphism.
D) To show that the Quran contains contradictions.

Key Takeaway
The Quran's ambiguous verses are not defects but divine wisdom to encourage reflection, reward effort, and emphasize reliance on knowledgeable scholars.

Objection of Repetition and Its Refutation
Objection and refutation regarding Quranic repetition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the Quran contains defective repetition and how scholars of rhetoric refute it by classifying such repetition as a beauty of speech.

Core Idea
Repetition in the Quran is rhetorical beauty, not defect.

Some critics claim that the Quran suffers from unnecessary repetition, citing examples like the many retellings of Pharaoh's story or the repeated refrains in Surat al-Rahman and al-Mursalat. In this passage, the author presents this objection and then refutes it by appealing to the established principles of Arabic eloquence, where repetition serves specific rhetorical purposes.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part of the passage where the defect of repetition is claimed. Note the specific examples given: the story of Pharaoh in multiple places, and the repeated verses in Surat al-Rahman and al-Mursalat.
These examples are chosen because they are clear instances of repetition.
2
Examine the refutation
Read the refutation which states that such repetition is actually among the 'beauties of speech' as established by scholars of eloquence. Think about why repetition can be considered beautiful.
The refutation is brief but relies on a whole field of Arabic rhetoric.
3
Understand the rhetorical perspective
Consider that in Arabic rhetoric (bayan), repetition can emphasize, reinforce, create rhythm, or deepen impact. The Quran's repetitions are deliberate and serve these goals.
This connects the refutation to a broader scholarly tradition.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
The objection is not simply denied; it is countered with a positive claim: repetition can be a strength. This is a common pattern in kalam: objections are met with reasoned rebuttals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أن فيه عيب التكرار كإعادة قصة فرعون في عدة مواضع، وكإعادة : فَبِأَيِّ آلَاءِ رَبِّكُمَا تُكَذِّبَانِ ووَيْلٌ يَوْمَئِذٍ لِلْمُكَذِّبِينَ في سورة الرحمن والمرسلات. ورد بأنه ربما يكون من محاسن الكلام على ما يقرره علماء البيان فيما وقع منه في القرآن
And among them is that it contains the defect of repetition (al-tikrar) like the retelling of the story of Pharaoh in several places, and like the repetition of 'So which of the favors of your Lord would you deny?' [Quran 55:13 etc.] and 'Woe, that Day, to the deniers' [Quran 77:15 etc.] in Surat al-Rahman and al-Mursalat. It is refuted by [saying] that it is perhaps among the beauties of speech (mahasin al-kalam) as established by the scholars of eloquence (ulama al-bayan) regarding what occurs of it in the Quran.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth objection: the Quran is marred by useless repetition (ayb al-tikrar), citing examples like the repeated story of Pharaoh in multiple chapters and the repeated refrains in Surat al-Rahman ('Fabi ayyi ala'') and Surat al-Mursalat ('Waylun yawma'idhin'). The refutation states that such repetition is actually one of the 'beauties of speech' (mahasin al-kalam) as confirmed by scholars of eloquence (bayan). In Arabic rhetoric, repetition can serve various purposes: emphasis, reinforcement, creating a rhythmic effect, or deepening the impact. Thus, it is a strength, not a flaw.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any repetition in the Quran is a sign of poor composition or redundancy.
Correction: In Arabic rhetoric, repetition can be a deliberate stylistic device for emphasis, rhythm, and impact, and is considered a beauty of speech (mahasin al-kalam).

Key Takeaway

Objection of Qira'at and Its Refutation
Sanusi presents and refutes the objection of internal contradiction via qira'at.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the existence of multiple canonical Quranic recitations (qira'at) does not constitute the type of 'contradiction' that would disprove the Quran's divine origin, according to the refutation presented by Sanusi.

Core Idea
Quranic variants are not contradictions; they are equally eloquent.

Sanusi addresses a powerful objection: the Quran itself claims to be free of contradiction (Quran 4:82), yet critics point to the thousands of variant readings (qira'at) among the canonical reciters. If the Quran is divine, how can it have so many differences? Sanusi responds by clarifying what the verse actually means.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the passage and its translation carefully. Notice how the opponent cites Quran 4:82 to claim that the Quran contradicts its own claim of consistency.
The verse is the foundation of the objection.
2
Identify the factual basis
The opponent mentions over twelve thousand differences in qira'at. Understand that this is the empirical challenge to the Quran's divine origin.
This number refers to canonical variant readings, not textual corruption.
3
Examine the refutation
Sanusi explains that the 'contradiction' (ikhtilaf) denied in the verse refers specifically to disparity in levels of eloquence (tafawut fi maratib al-balagha), not any difference in wording.
The key is to distinguish between types of difference.
4
Understand the implication
All qira'at are equally eloquent, so none is inferior. Thus the Quran's claim that it contains no disparity in eloquence remains true, even with multiple recitations.
The refutation preserves the Quran's inimitability.
5
Reflect on the principle
Consider how this argument shows that textual variants can coexist without undermining divine origin, as long as they maintain rhetorical excellence.
This is a sophisticated theological response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أن فيه قوله : وَلَوْ كَانْ مِنْ عِنْدِ غَيْرِ اللَّهِ لَوَجَدُوا فِيهِ اخْتِلافَاً كثيراً . وأنت تجد فيه من الاختلاف المسموع من أصحاب القراءة ما يربى على اثني عشر ألفاً . ورد بأن المراد من الاختلاف المنفي هو التفاوت في مراتب البلاغة
And among them is that it contains His saying: 'And if it had been from other than Allah, they would have found within it much contradiction' [Quran 4:82]. Yet you find within it from the heard discrepancy coming from the people of qira'at that exceeds twelve thousand. It is refuted by [saying] that what is meant by the denied contradiction is disparity in levels of eloquence.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth and final objection: the Quran itself claims there is no contradiction within it (Quran 4:82), but the various qira'at (canonical recitation variants) number over twelve thousand differences. How can this be reconciled? The refutation explains that the 'contradiction' (ikhtilaf) denied in the verse refers specifically to discrepancy in eloquence (tafawut fi maratib al-balagha), meaning that despite the existence of multiple recitations, none is inferior to another in rhetorical quality. All are equally eloquent and divinely revealed. The differing readings are not contradictions in meaning or deficiency in eloquence; rather, they enrich the Quran's meaning.

Key Terms
اختلاف
قراءات
بلاغة
تفاوت
النساء 82

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think that variant readings (qira'at) imply textual corruption or contradiction, which would disprove the Quran's divine origin.
Correction: The refutation shows that the 'contradiction' denied in Quran 4:82 refers specifically to disparity in eloquence, not mere differences in wording. The qira'at are all equally eloquent, so they do not undermine the Quran's divine origin.

Checkpoint
What is the meaning of 'contradiction' (ikhtilaf) that the Quran denies in verse 4:82, according to the refutation?
A) Any difference in wording.
B) Disparity in levels of eloquence.
C) Differences in interpretation.
D) Historical inconsistencies.

Key Takeaway
The Quran's claim of being free from contradiction refers to disparity in eloquence, not to the existence of multiple canonical readings.

Refuting Contradictions in the Quran: Apparent vs. Real Conflict
The author lists alleged contradictory verses and gives the refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how apparent contradictions in the Quran are refuted by denying the conditions of true logical contradiction.

Core Idea
Apparent Quranic contradictions disappear when the conditions for contradiction are not met.

The Quran's inimitability includes its freedom from contradiction. Critics have pointed to verses that seem to conflict, but careful examination shows that the conditions for logical contradiction are absent. This lesson examines two such examples and the principle of refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once without analysis. Let the author's presentation come through.
The passage lists two pairs of verses that critics claim contradict each other.
2
Identify the two pairs of verses
The first pair concerns questioning on Judgment Day (55:39 vs 15:92-93). The second pair concerns the food of Hell's inhabitants (88:6 vs 69:36).
Note the specific ayah numbers mentioned.
3
Understand the refutation
The author refutes by stating that the conditions for true contradiction are not present. True contradiction requires unity of subject, predicate, time, place, and relation.
The author refers to tafsir works for detailed explanations.
4
Apply the principle
Reflect on why these verses are not contradictory: they address different contexts or aspects of the same reality.
This reinforces the Quran's consistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أن فيه التناقض كقوله تعالى : فيَوْمَئِدٍ لا يُسْأَلُ عَنْ ذَنْبِهِ إِنْسٌ وَلَا جَانٌ) (۲) مع قوله: ﴿فَوَرَبِّكَ لَنَسْأَلَنَّهُمْ أَجْمَعِينَ عَمَّا كَانُوا يَعْمَلُونَ ) (۳) لَيْسَ لَهُمْ طَعَامٌ إِلا مِنْ ضَريع (4) مع قوله : وَلَا طَعَامُ إِلا مِنْ غِسْلِين (٥) . إلى غير ذلك من مواضع فيها تنافي الكلامين . ورد بمنع وجود شرائط التناقض . وقد بين ذلك على التفصيل في كتب التفسير.
And among them is that there is contradiction in it, like His saying: 'So that Day no soul will be asked about its sin, neither human nor jinn' (55:39) with His saying: 'So by your Lord, We will surely question them all about what they used to do' (15:92-93); and His saying: 'They will have no food except from thorny plants' (88:6) with His saying: 'And no food except from pus' (69:36). And other such places where there is apparent conflict between the two statements. And it was refuted by denying the existence of the conditions of contradiction. And that has been explained in detail in books of exegesis.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage presents the objection that the Quran contains contradictory verses. The objector cites two pairs of verses: one about being questioned on Judgment Day and another about being questioned; and two verses about the food of the inhabitants of Hell. The author states that the refutation is by denying that the conditions for true contradiction are present. He refers to detailed explanations in tafsir works. In other words, these verses can be reconciled without logical contradiction, as the contexts differ or the apparent conflict is resolved by interpretation.

شرائط التناقض

Correction: Apparent contradictions lack the conditions for true logical contradiction and are reconciled through contextual understanding.

The Claim of Falsehood in Quran 7:11 and Its Refutation
Objection and refutation concerning Surah al-A‘raf 7:11

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how an apparent chronological discrepancy in Surah al-A‘raf 7:11 is resolved through proper interpretation of the pronoun 'you' as referring to Adam.

Core Idea
The verse is not false; 'you' refers to Adam, not all humans.

This passage from a work on miracles addresses a specific objection: that the Quran contains a false statement because the command to the angels to prostrate to Adam appears after the creation of humans, though Adam was created before. The refutation clarifies that the pronoun 'you' in 'We created you' refers to Adam himself, preserving the truthful sequence. This illustrates the importance of precise exegesis in defending the Quran.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the critic's claim that the verse contains pure falsehood (kadhib mahd) due to a chronological error.
The objection hinges on the sequence of events in the verse.
2
Understand the refutation
The refutation states that the pronoun 'you' refers to Adam, the father of humanity. Thus, the creation and formation mentioned are Adam's, and the command to the angels occurred after that.
This is a common interpretive move in tafsir to resolve apparent contradictions.
3
Reflect on the broader lesson
Consider how a single pronoun shift resolves an apparent contradiction. This highlights the need for careful reading of the Quran and demonstrates the apologetic method of addressing objections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage presents the objection that a Quranic verse contains a false historical sequence: the verse seems to say that after creating and forming 'you' (the addressees), the angels were commanded to prostrate to Adam, but Adam was created before them. The author states the refutation: the 'you' refers to our father Adam, not to the present audience. Thus the verse is truthful and the objection fails.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Correction: The pronoun 'you' in 'We created you' refers to Adam, not to all humans, so the chronology is correct.

Key Takeaway
The apparent contradiction in Surah al-A‘raf 7:11 is resolved by interpreting the pronoun 'you' as referring to Adam, preserving the truthfulness of the Quran.

The Quran Is Not Poetry: Refuting the Meter Allegation
Listing Quranic verses in poetic meters and refuting the allegation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the presence of metrical patterns in the Quran does not make it poetry, and the key conditions that distinguish the Quran from poetry.

Core Idea
Metrical wording is not poetry without intent and design.

The objection that the Quran contains poetry has been raised by those who find its verses fitting into Arabic poetic meters. In this passage, the author lists fifteen such examples and then refutes the claim by clarifying the essential conditions of poetry. This lesson will guide you through the refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the list of examples
Start by reading the passage where the objector claims the Quran contains verses in every poetic meter. The author then provides fifteen examples from different meters.
Notice the variety of meters cited.
2
Identify the key condition: intentionality
The author states that mere occurrence in a meter is not enough; the composition must be intentional (ta‘ammud al-wazn). This is the primary refutation.
Intentionality distinguishes human poetry from divine speech.
3
Consider the additional requirement of rhyme
According to some scholars, rhyme (taqfiyah) is also necessary for something to be considered poetry. The Quran does not consistently have rhyme.
4
Examine the counterarguments: alteration and predominance
The author points out that many cited verses have slight alterations from the exact meter, and even if some fit, the principle of taghlīb (predominance) allows occasional metrical phrasing without it being poetry.
Taghlīb means that occasional similarity does not change the essential nature.
5
Reflect on the significance for the Quran's inimitability
This refutation defends the Quran's unique nature as divine speech, not subject to human poetic conventions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أن فيه الشعر من كل بحر، وقد قال: ﴿وَمَا عَلَّمْنَاهُ الشِّعْرَ (۷) فمن الطويل : فَمَنْ شَاءَ فَلْيُؤْمِنْ وَمَنْ شَاءَ فَلْيَكْفُرْ (۸) ومن المديد : واصْنَع الفُلْكَ بأعيننا (۱) ومن البسيط : لِيَقْضِيَ اللهُ أمْراً كَانَ مَفْعُولاً ) (۲) ومن الوافر : وَيُخْزِهِمْ وَيَنْصُرْكُمْ عَلَيْهِم وَيَشْفِ صُدُورَ قَوْمٍ مُؤْمِنين (۳) ومن الكامل : واللهُ يُهْدِي مَنْ يَشَاءُ إِلَى صِرَاطٍ مُستقيم (4) ومن الهزج : نا للهِ لَقَدْ أَثَرَكَ اللهُ عَلَيْنَا (٥) ومن الرجز وَدَانِيَةٌ عَلَيْهِمْ ظِلَالُهَا وَذُلِّلَتْ قُطُوفهَا تَدْلِيلاً) (۱) ومن الرمل: ﴿وَجِفَانٍ كالجَوَاب وَقُدُورٍ رَاسِيَاتٍ () ومن السريع : ﴿قَالَ فَمَا خَطْبُكَ يَا با سامري (٨) ومن المنسرح : إنا خَلَقْنَا الإِنْسَانَ مِنْ نُطْفَة ) (۱) ومن الخفيف: ﴿أَرَأَيْتَ الَّذِي يُكَذِّبُ بالدِّينِ فَذَلِكَ الذِي يَدُعُ اليتيم ) (١٠) ومن المضارع: ﴿وَيَوْمَ التَّنَادِ يَوْمَ يُوَلُّونَ مُدْبِرِينَ ) (۱۱) ومن المقتضب : في قُلُوبِهِم مَرَضٌ (۱۲) ومن المجتث : المُطَّوعِينَ مِنَ الْمُؤْمِنِينَ فِي الصَّدَقَاتِ) (۱۳) ومن المتقارب : وأمْلِي لَهُمْ إِنَّ كَيْدِي مَتِينُ ﴾ (١٤) . ورد بأن مجرد كون اللفظ على هذه الأوزان لا يكفي، بل لا بد من تعمد الوزن، وعند البعض من التقفية، على أن في كثير مما ذكر نوع تغيير، ولو سلم فالتغليب باب واسع .
And among them is that there is poetry in it from every meter, and He has said: 'And We did not teach him poetry' (36:69). So from the ṭawīl meter: 'So whoever wills, let him believe, and whoever wills, let him disbelieve' (18:29). From the madīd: 'And construct the ship under Our eyes' (11:37). From the basīṭ: 'That Allah might accomplish a matter already destined' (8:42). From the wāfir: 'And disgrace them and give you victory over them and heal the breasts of a believing people' (9:14). From the kāmil: 'And Allah guides whom He wills to a straight path' (2:213). From the hazaj: 'By Allah, you have certainly preferred you over us' (12:91). From the sarī‘: 'He said, Then what is your business, O Samaritan?' (20:95). From the munsariḥ: 'Indeed, We created man from a drop' (76:2). Commentary
This passage is a detailed refutation of the objection that the Quran contains poetry, contradicting the verse that denies teaching poetry to the Prophet. The objector claims to find verses that conform to various Arabic poetic meters. The author lists fifteen meters and gives examples. The refutation consists of several points: (1) mere presence of the wording in a metrical pattern does not make it poetry; (2) intentional composition (ta‘ammud al-wazn) is required, and according to some, rhyme (taqfiyah) as well; (3) many of the cited examples contain slight alterations from the exact meter; (4) even if some fit, the principle of taghlīb (predominance) allows occasional metrical phrasing without it being considered poetry. Thus the objection fails.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any text that fits a poetic meter is poetry.
Correction: Poetry requires intentional composition of meter and often rhyme; mere occurrence of metrical patterns does not suffice.

Checkpoint

B) They were not intentionally composed in meter

D) They are not in proper Arabic

Key Takeaway
The Quran's use of metrical patterns does not make it poetry, because poetry requires intentionality and the Quran is divine speech beyond human composition.

Poetic Meter Alone Does Not Prove Inimitability
The author lists Quranic verses in various meters then rebuts the claim of miracle based on meter alone.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why mere poetic meter in Quranic verses does not constitute a miracle, and what additional conditions (intentionality and rhyme) are required.

Core Idea
Poetic meter without intentionality does not make a miracle.

This lesson addresses a common objection: that the Quran contains verses in known poetic meters, so it must be poetry or miraculous because of that. The author first lists examples of Quranic verses that scan in various meters, then rebuts the claim that this alone proves inimitability. We will examine the rebuttal and understand the criteria for true miraculousness.

Guided Path
1
Review the list of examples
Read through the Quranic verses cited in various meters. Notice that the author is presenting the objection's evidence.
These examples are from different meters like al-basīt, al-wāfir, etc.
2
Understand the rebuttal
The author argues that mere meter is insufficient. Pay attention to the conditions of intentional weight and rhyme.
Intentionality (ta‘ammud) is key; accidental meter is not miraculous.
3
Consider the principle of taghlib
The author mentions that even if accepted, the principle of predominance (taghlīb) allows these verses to be considered in those meters despite alterations.
Taghlib is a broad principle that accommodates variations.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: Why does intentionality matter for miracles? How does this affect the claim of the Quran's inimitability?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بأعيننا ومن البسيط : لِيَقْضِيَ اللهُ أمْراً كَانَ مَفْعُولاً ومن الوافر : وَيُخْزِهِمْ وَيَنْصُرْكُمْ عَلَيْهِم وَيَشْفِ صُدُورَ قَوْمٍ مُؤْمِنين ومن الكامل : واللهُ يُهْدِي مَنْ يَشَاءُ إِلَى صِرَاطٍ مُستقيم ومن الهزج : نا للهِ لَقَدْ أَثَرَكَ اللهُ عَلَيْنَا ومن الرجز : وَدَانِيَةٌ عَلَيْهِمْ ظِلَالُهَا وَذُلِّلَتْ قُطُوفهَا تَدْلِيلاً ومن الرمل: ﴿وَجِفَانٍ كالجَوَاب وَقُدُورٍ رَاسِيَاتٍ ومن السريع : ﴿قَالَ فَمَا خَطْبُكَ يَا با سامري ومن المنسرح : إنا خَلَقْنَا الإِنْسَانَ مِنْ نُطْفَة ومن الخفيف: ﴿أَرَأَيْتَ الَّذِي يُكَذِّبُ بالدِّينِ فَذَلِكَ الذِي يَدُعُ اليتيم ومن المضارع: ﴿وَيَوْمَ التَّنَادِ يَوْمَ يُوَلُّونَ مُدْبِرِينَ ومن المقتضب : في قُلُوبِهِم مَرَضٌ ومن المجتث : المُطَّوعِينَ مِنَ الْمُؤْمِنِينَ فِي الصَّدَقَاتِ ومن المتقارب : وأمْلِي لَهُمْ إِنَّ كَيْدِي مَتِينُ ورد بأن مجرد كون اللفظ على هذه الأوزان لا يكفي، بل لا بد من تعمد الوزن، وعند البعض من التقفية، على أن في كثير مما ذكر نوع تغيير، ولو سلم فالتغليب باب واسع.
From al-basīt: 'Liyaqḍiya llāhu amran kāna maf‘ūlan'; from al-wāfir: 'Wayakhzihim wayanṣurukum ‘alayhim wayashfi ṣudūra qawmin mu’minīn'; from al-kāmil: 'Wallāhu yahdī man yashā’u ilā ṣirāṭin mustaqīm'; from al-hazaj: 'Nā lillāhi laqad ātharaka llāhu ‘alaynā'; from al-rajaz: 'Wadāniyatun ‘alayhim ẓilāluhā wadhullilat quṭūfuhā tadlīlan'; from al-ramal: 'Wajifānin kāljawābi waqudūrin rāsiyāt'; from al-sarī‘: 'Qāla famā khaṭbuka yā bā sāmirī'; from al-munsariḥ: 'Innā khalaqnā l-insāna min nuṭfatin'; from al-khafīf: 'Ara’ayta alladhī yukadhdhibu bi-d-dīni fadhālika alladhī yadu‘u l-yatīma'; from al-muḍāri‘: 'Wayawma t-tanādi yawma yuwallūna mudbirīn'; from al-muqtaḍab: 'Fī qulūbihim maraḍun'; from al-mujtathth: 'Al-muṭṭawwi‘īna mina l-mu’minīna fī ṣ-ṣadaqāt'; from al-mutaqārib: 'Wa’amlī lahum inna kaydī matīn'. Then it was replied that mere existence of the wording according to these meters is not sufficient; rather, intentional weight (meter) is necessary, and according to some, rhyme (taqfiya). Moreover, in many of what was mentioned there is a type of alteration, and even if conceded, the principle of predominance (taghlīb) is broad.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage is a rebuttal to an objection. The author first lists several Quranic verses that conform to various Arabic poetic meters (al-basīt, al-wāfir, al-kāmil, etc.), demonstrating that verses can be scanned according to these meters. Then he addresses an implied claim that this poetic quality itself constitutes a miracle of the Quran. The response (ward) argues that mere existence of these meters is not sufficient for inimitability. What is required is that the Prophet intended (ta‘ammud) the meter; accidentally having verses fit a meter is not miraculous. Additionally, some scholars require rhyme (taqfiya). Furthermore, many of the cited examples involve some alteration or change (taghyīr) from standard meter, and even if one accepts them, the principle of taghlīb (predominance) allows such cases to be considered as belonging to those meters broadly. Thus, the argument that the Quran's poetic form is the sole miracle is refuted.

Key Terms
إعجاز
وزن
تقفية
تغليب
تعمد الوزن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Quran is miraculous because its verses fit poetic meters.
Correction: The author explains that intentional weight and rhyme are necessary, and mere meter is insufficient.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the author's rebuttal regarding Quranic verses in poetic meters?
A) The Quran is actual poetry.
B) The poetic meters alone prove the miracle.
C) Intentional weight (meter) and rhyme are required for the miracle.
D) The verses do not actually fit any meter.

Key Takeaway
Mere poetic meter does not prove the Quran's inimitability; intentionality and rhyme are required.

The author argues that intentionality is key to the miracle. In your own life, where have you mistaken a coincidence for a sign from God, or dismissed a genuine sign as mere chance? How can you cultivate more discernment?
?
The principle of taghlīb allows for exceptions while recognizing the overall pattern. In your relationship with God, do you focus on isolated hardships or the overarching mercy? What would it take for you to see His predominance even amidst trials?

Miracle of Informing of Past and Future Unseen Events
The author enumerates examples of prophetic knowledge of the unseen as a miracle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Prophet's knowledge of past and future unseen events serves as a miracle, and how it is distinguished from sorcery, soothsaying, and other claims.

Core Idea
Prophetic knowledge of the unseen, untaught and pure, is a divine miracle.

This lesson examines one specific type of miracle: the Prophet's accurate reports of unseen past events and future occurrences. The author provides examples from the Quran and hadith, and then explains how these signs are uniquely tied to prophethood, setting them apart from saintly miracles, sorcery, and astrology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two categories
Notice that the passage first mentions 'informing of past and future unseen matters' and then separately discusses past and future examples. This is a clear two-part structure.
2
Examine the past events examples
The author lists stories of Moses, Joseph, Abraham, Noah, Lot, and others, emphasizing that the Prophet knew them without learning from anyone or reading a book. Look for the Quranic verse cited as evidence.
3
Examine the future events examples
The passage gives both Quranic predictions (e.g., defeat of Romans, conquest of Mecca) and hadith predictions (e.g., about Ali, Ammar, the caliphate). Note how these are presented as fulfilled prophecies.
4
Understand the distinguishing factors
The author explains that these miracles are accompanied by the claim of prophethood, purity of soul, righteous deeds, and avoidance of astrology. This distinguishes them from saintly miracles, sorcery, soothsaying, and astrology.
This is crucial for defending prophethood against false claims.
5
Reflect on the overall significance
Consider how this type of miracle authenticates the Prophet's message. The fact that he accurately narrated history and predicted future events without worldly means points to divine revelation.

فمن أنواع المعجزات إخباره عن الغيوب الماضية المستقبلة، أما الماضية فقصة موسى وفرعون، وقصة يوسف وقصة إبراهيم، ونوح، ولوط، وغيرهم على تفاصيلها وطولها من غير سماع من أحد ولا تلفن من كتاب على ما أشير إليه بقوله: وتِلْكَ مِنْ أنباء الغَيْبِ نُوحِيهَا إِلَيْكَ مَا كُنْتَ تَعْلَمُهَا أَنْتَ وَلَا قَوْمُكَ مِنْ قَبْلِ هَذَا . وأما المستقبلة، فمنها في القرآن كقوله تعالى: وعدكم الله مغانم كثيرة تأخذونها، آلم غلبت الروم... إلى قوله: وعد الله لا يخلف الله وعده، سنلقي في قلوب الذين كفروا الرعب، سيهزم الجمع ويولون الدبر ستدعون إلى قوم أولي بأس شديد، ولنستخلفنهم في الأرض لتدخلن المسجد الحرام، ليظهره على الدين كله لا يأتون بمثله فإن لم تفعلوا ولن تفعلوا، وإن الذي فرض عليك القرآن لرادك إلى معاد. ومنها ما ليس فيه كقوله عليه السلام لعلي رضي الله عنه: تقاتل بعدي الناكثين، والقاسطين والمارقين. ولعمار: تقتلك الفئة الباغية، وقوله عليه السلام زويت لي الأرض فأريت مشارقها ومغاربها، وسيبلغ ملك أمتي ما زوي لي منها. وقوله: الخلافة بعدي ثلاثون سنة. وكإخباره بهلاك كسرى وقيصر وزوال ملكهما، وإنفاق كنوزهما في سبيل الله، وباستيلاء الأتراك. إلى غير ذلك مما ورد في صحاح الأحاديث. وقد اقترنت بدعوى النبوة، فيتميز عن الكرامات، وبطهارة النفس وصوالح الأعمال، وترك المراجعة إلى أحوال الكواكب، والنظر في آلاتها، فيتميز عن السحر والكهانة والنجوم وأمثال ذلك.
Among the types of miracles is his informing of past and future unseen matters: as for the past, the story of Moses and Pharaoh, and the story of Joseph and the story of Abraham, Noah, Lot, and others, in their details and length, without hearing from anyone nor reading from a book, as indicated by His saying: 'That is from the news of the unseen which We reveal to you; you did not know it, nor did your people, before this.' As for the future, among them in the Quran are His saying: 'Allah has promised you much booty that you will take...' Alif Lam Meem, the Romans have been defeated... to His saying: 'The promise of Allah does not fail; We will cast terror into the hearts of those who disbelieve...' 'The assembly will be defeated and they will turn their backs...' 'You will be called to a people of great might...' 'And We will surely grant them replacement in the land...' 'You will surely enter the Sacred Mosque...' 'That He may make it prevail over all religion...' 'They do not produce the like of it, and if they do not, and they will never...' 'Indeed, He who imposed upon you the Quran will return you to a place of return.' And among them are those not in the Quran, like his saying to Ali: 'You will fight after me the breakers of covenant, the unjust, and the deviators.' And to Ammar: 'The rebellious group will kill you.' And his saying: 'The earth was rolled up for me, so I was shown its easts and wests, and the dominion of my nation will reach what was rolled up for me of it.' And his saying: 'The caliphate after me is thirty years.' And his informing of the destruction of Chosroes and Caesar and the removal of their kingdom, and the spending of their treasures in the path of Allah, and the domination of the Turks, etc. This, along with what is reported in authentic hadiths. This is accompanied by the claim of prophethood, thus it is distinguished from saintly miracles; and by purity of soul and righteous deeds, and refraining from consulting stars and observing their instruments, thus it is distinguished from sorcery, soothsaying, astrology, and the like.

Commentary
This passage defines and illustrates one type of prophetic miracle: knowledge of the unseen. The author begins by stating that among the types of miracles is the Prophet's information about past and future unseen matters. He then gives examples: past events such as the stories of Moses, Joseph, Abraham, Noah, and Lot, which the Prophet narrated in detail without having learned them from anyone or from a book. The author references the Quranic verse 'That is from the news of the unseen which We reveal to you.' Then he gives future predictions from the Quran, such as the promise of booty, the defeat of the Romans, and others. He also gives examples from hadith, like the Prophet's predictions about Ali's battles, Ammar's death, the extent of his nation's dominion, and the downfall of Persian and Roman empires. The author then explains how these miracles are distinguished from other claims: they are accompanied by the claim of prophethood (unlike karamat which are not), and by the prophet's purity and righteous deeds, and by refraining from astrology, thus distinguishing them from sorcery, soothsaying, and astrology.

Third Type of Miracles: Personal Signs and Characteristics
The author lists physical and moral miracles of the Prophet as the third type.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third category of miracles, which consists of the Prophet's personal physical and moral signs that testify to his prophethood.

Core Idea
The Prophet's personal attributes and miraculous events are direct evidence of his prophethood.

The author continues his classification of miracles by presenting the third type: personal signs and characteristics of the Prophet. This includes a long list of physical traits, moral excellences, and supernatural events that occurred around him. These are all considered miracles because they are extraordinary and serve as proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the English translation. Let the author's enumeration come through before you interpret.
Notice the phrase 'As for the third type' which sets the context.
2

The passage is a long list of items. Note the different kinds: physical signs (light, birth, seal), moral attributes, and external events. Try to group them mentally.
The list ends with 'and the like, which can hardly be enumerated' indicating its extensiveness.
3
Reflect on why personal attributes are miracles

4

The sheer number and variety of signs provide cumulative evidence. Think about how each item individually and collectively points to prophethood.

Classification of Miracles: Preparatory and Confirmatory
Classification of miracles into timing and three categories with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classification of miracles according to timing (preparatory vs confirmatory) and their further division into three categories.

Core Idea
Miracles are classified by timing and by relation to essence, attributes, or external matters.

This passage introduces a systematic classification of the Prophet's miracles. It distinguishes between preparatory miracles that occurred before his prophethood and confirmatory ones that followed, then further divides them into those related to his essence, his attributes, and matters external to both. Understanding this classification helps appreciate the comprehensive nature of prophetic evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the two main categories: irhasiat and tasdiquiat.
The passage also mentions that details are found in works on proofs of prophethood.
2
Identify the three subcategories
Look for the phrase 'تنقسم إلى أمور ثابتة في ذاته، وأمور متعلقة لصفاته، وأمور خارجة عنهما'. That is the division into essence, attributes, and external matters.
The author then gives examples for the first two categories.
3
Reflect on the examples
Consider how each example relates to the category: essence examples are physical or intrinsic, attribute examples are moral or behavioral.
The third category is mentioned but not exemplified here.
4
Answer the checkpoint question
Test your understanding by answering the multiple-choice question about the division after irhasiat and tasdiquiat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من أنواع المعجزات أفعال ظهرت منه عليه السلام على خلاف العادة تربى على ألف قد فصلت في دلائل النبوة، بعضها إرهاصية ظهرت قبل دعوى النبوة، وبضعها تصديقية ظهرت بعدها وتنقسم إلى أمور ثابتة في ذاته، وأمور متعلقة لصفاته، وأمور خارجة عنهما. فالأول: كالنور الذي كان ينقلب في آبائه، إلى أن ولد . وكولادته مختوناً مسروراً وضعاً إحدى يديه على عينيه، والأخرى على سوأته، وما كان من خاتم النبوة بين كتفيه، وطول قامته عند الطويل، ووساطته عند الوسيط ورؤية من خلفه كأن يرى من قدامه. والثاني: كاستجماعه الغاية القصوى من الصدق والأمانة، والعفاف والشجاعة، والفصاحة، والسماحة، والزهد والتواضع لأهل المسكنة، والشفقة على الأمة، والمصابرة على متاعب النبوة، والمواظبة على مكارم الأخلاق.
Among the types of miracles are acts that appeared from him, upon him peace, contrary to the usual course of things, amounting to more than a thousand, which have been detailed in proofs of prophethood. Some of them are preparatory (irhasiat) that appeared before the claim of prophethood, and some are confirmatory (tasdiquiat) that appeared after it. They are divided into matters fixed in his essence, matters related to his attributes, and matters external to both. The first: like the light that was transmitted in his forefathers until he was born; and his being born circumcised and with the umbilical cord cut, placing one of his hands on his eyes and the other on his private parts; and what was from the seal of prophethood between his shoulder blades; and his height being tall among the tall, average among the average; and his seeing from behind as he sees from front. The second: like his possessing the utmost degree of truthfulness, trustworthiness, chastity, bravery, eloquence, generosity, asceticism, humility towards the poor, compassion for the nation, endurance of the hardships of prophethood, and adherence to noble character.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage provides a classification of the miracles that appeared from the Prophet. The author states that the acts that appeared from him against the usual course of things amount to more than a thousand, detailed in works on proofs of prophethood. He divides them into two categories based on timing: preparatory miracles (irhasiat) that occurred before his claim of prophethood, and confirmatory miracles (tasdiquiat) that occurred after. Then he further divides them into three categories: matters fixed in his essence (such as the light in his lineage, his birth state, the seal, his vision, his stature), matters related to his attributes (his moral excellence), and matters external to both (implied but not detailed here). He gives examples for the first two categories: for the first, the light transmitted through his forefathers, being born circumcised with umbilical cord cut, placing his hands on his eyes and private part, the seal of prophethood, his balanced height, and seeing from behind; for the second, his possessing utmost truthfulness, trustworthiness, chastity, bravery, eloquence, generosity, asceticism, humility, compassion, patience, and adherence to noble character.

تصديقية (tasdiquiat)
ذات (essence)
صفات (attributes)
دلائل النبوة (proofs of prophethood)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: Only miracles that occurred after prophethood are considered evidence for prophethood.
Correction: Preparatory miracles (irhasiat) that occurred before the claim are also part of the proof.

Distinguishing Prophetic Miracles from Karamat and Sorcery
Explains criteria distinguishing prophetic miracles from karamat and sorcery.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two key criteria that distinguish prophetic miracles from saintly wonders and from sorcery, soothsaying, and astrology.

Core Idea
Prophetic miracles are authenticated by the claim of prophethood and spiritual purity.

In this lesson, we learn from the author how to tell apart true prophetic miracles from other extraordinary events. The passage explains that miracles come with a claim of prophethood, while saintly wonders do not. Additionally, the purity and righteousness of the prophet, and his avoidance of astrological practices, set miracles apart from sorcery and similar arts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and examples
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the specific examples given: the thirty-year caliphate, the fall of empires, etc.
These examples illustrate the type of predictions that are considered prophetic miracles.
2
Identify the two distinguishing criteria
Identify the two ways prophetic miracles are distinguished: first from karamat by the claim of prophethood, and second from sorcery by purity and avoidance of astrology.
3
Understand the significance of the claim of prophethood
Reflect on why claiming prophethood is a decisive factor. Miracles are public and support the truth of the prophet's message, unlike saintly wonders which are private.
This distinction is fundamental in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مازوى لي منها . وقوله : الخلافة بعدي ثلاثون سنة . وكإخباره بهلاك كسرى وقيصر وزوال ملكهما ، وإنفاق كنوزهما في سبيل الله ، وباستيلاء الأتراك . . إلى غير ذلك مما ورد في صحاح الأحاديث، وقد اقترنت بدعوى النبوة، فيتميز عن الكرامات، وبطهارة النفس وصوالح الأعمال، وترك المراجعة إلى أحوال الكواكب، والنظر في آلاتها، فيتميز عن السحر والكهانة والنجوم وأمثال ذلك .
What is beyond that. And his saying: The caliphate after me is thirty years. And his informing of the destruction of Kisra and Caesar and the removal of their kingdom, and the spending of their treasures in the way of Allah, and the domination of the Turks... and other such things that are reported in authentic hadiths. And these are accompanied by the claim of prophethood, so they are distinguished from the wonders of saints (karamat). And by the purity of the soul and righteous deeds, and abandoning recourse to the states of the planets and looking at their instruments, they are distinguished from sorcery (sihr), soothsaying (kahanah), astrology (nujum), and the like.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage explains the criteria that distinguish prophetic miracles from other supernatural phenomena. The author begins by listing examples of miraculous predictions made by the Prophet, such as the thirty-year caliphate, the fall of empires, and the future conquests. These are called miracles (mu'jizat) because they are accompanied by the claim of prophethood. This distinguishes them from the karamat of saints, which are not linked to such a claim. Additionally, these miracles are distinguished from sorcery, soothsaying, and astrology by the purity of the Prophet's soul, his righteous deeds, and his abstinence from astrological practices. The phrase 'what is beyond that' refers to additional examples that follow the same pattern.

النجوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prophetic miracles are distinguished only by being extraordinary or by making predictions.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that the claim of prophethood (اقترنت بدعوى النبوة) is what distinguishes them from karamat, and purity and avoidance of astrology distinguish them from sorcery.

Key Takeaway
Prophetic miracles are uniquely identified by the claim of prophethood and the prophet's moral purity and avoidance of sorcery and astrology.

Three Categories of the Prophet's Miracles
Classifies the Prophet's miracles into three types with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the threefold classification of the Prophet's miracles: miracles inherent in his person, miracles related to his attributes, and external events.

Core Idea
Miracles include not only external events but also personal and moral wonders.

The author organizes the vast array of the Prophet's miracles into three clear categories. This systematic classification helps us see that his prophetic signs encompassed his very being, his perfect character, and the world around him. Understanding this framework deepens our appreciation of the comprehensive nature of his prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of the three categories and the numerous examples provided.
Notice the author's playful use of lengthy enumeration before summarizing the categories.
2
Identify the three categories
Locate where the author explicitly divides the miracles: (1) matters established in his self, (2) matters related to his attributes, (3) matters external to both.
The categories are marked by 'فالأول', 'والثاني', 'والثالث'.
3
Analyze examples for each category
For each category, list the examples given and see how they fit the definition. For instance, the 'seal of prophethood' belongs to the first category, while 'truthfulness' belongs to the second.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why the author emphasizes that miracles are not just external wonders but also include personal virtues and physical traits. How does this strengthen the case for prophethood?
This connects to the broader proof of prophethood through comprehensive signs.

قال : وأما النوع الثالث فكان النور الذي كان ينتقل في آبائه، وولادته مختوناً مسروراً، وخاتم النبوة، ورؤيته من خلفه، وكاتصافه بغاية الصدق والأمانة والعفة والشجاعة والفصاحة، والسماحة والزهد والتواضع، النبوة، بعضها إرهاصية ظهرت قبل دعوى النبوة، وبضعها تصديقية ظهرت بعدها وتنقسم إلى أمور ثابتة في ذاته، وأمور متعلقة لصفاته، وأمور خارجة عنهما . فالأول: كالنور الذي كان ينقلب في آبائه ، إلى أن ولد . وكولادته مختوناً مسروراً وضعاً إحدى يديه على عينيه، والأخرى على سوأته، وما كان من خاتم النبوة بين كتفيه، وطول قامته عند الطويل، ووساطته عند الوسيط ورؤية من خلفه كأن يرى من قدامه . والثاني: كاستجماعه الغاية القصوى من الصدق والأمانة، والعفاف والشجاعة، والفصاحة ، والسماحة ، والزهد والتواضع لأهل المسكنة، والشفقة على الأمة، والمصابرة على متاعب النبوة، والمواظبة على مكارم الأخلاق وكبلوغه النهاية في العلوم والمعارف الإلهية، وتمهيد المصالح الدينية والدنيوية، وككونه مجاب الدعوة على ما دعى لابن عباس رضي الله تعالى عنه بقوله : «اللهم فَقَهْهُ في الدين» فصار إمام المفسرين، ودعــا عـلى عتبة بن أبي لهب بقوله : ( اللهم سلط عليه كلباً من كلابك فافترسه الاسد. وعلى مضر بقوله : « اللهم اشدد وطأتك على مضر واجعل عليهم سنين كسني يوسف فمنع الله القطر عنهم سنين . وعلى من لحقه من الكفار حين خرج من الغار بقوله : «يا أرض خذيه» فساخت قوائم فرسه . والثالث : كخرور الأوثان سجداً ليلة ولادته، وسقوط شرف قصور الأكاسرة، وإظلال السحاب عليه، وكانشقاق القمر، وانقلاع الشجر، وتسليم الحجر، ونبوع الماء من بين أصابعه إلى أن رويت الجنود ودوابهم، وشبع الخلق الكثير من اليسير، وحنين الجذع في مسجد المدينة حين انتقل منه إلى المنبر، وشكاية النوق عن أصحابها ، وشهادة الشاة المشوية يوم خيبر بأنها مسمومة، ودرور الضرع من الشاة اليابسة الجرباء لأم معبد حين مسح يده عليها، وخطاب الذئب وهب بن أوس بقوله : أتعجب من أخذى شاة، وهذا محمد يدعو إلى الحق فلا تجيبونه، وتسبيح الحصى، وغير ذلك مما لا يعد ولا يحصى.
He said: As for the third type, it was the light that used to be transmitted in his forefathers, and his birth circumcised and with his navel cut, and the seal of prophethood, and seeing him from behind, and his being described with the utmost truthfulness, trustworthiness, chastity, courage, eloquence, generosity, asceticism, humility, compassion, patience, knowledge, noble traits, and social benefits, and his being answered in supplication, and the falling of idols and the collapse of the balconies of the palaces of the Chosroes on the night of his birth, and the cloud shading him, and the splitting of the moon, and the uprooting of trees, and the stone greeting him, and water gushing from between his fingers, and the moaning of the palm trunk, and the complaint of the she-camel, and the testimony of the poisoned sheep, and the glorification of pebbles, and the like of that which can hardly be counted. Among the types of miracles are actions that appeared from him, peace be upon him, contrary to custom, reaching [?] a thousand that have been detailed in the proofs of prophethood, some of them preparatory (irhas) that appeared before the claim of prophethood, and some of them confirmatory that appeared after it, and they are divided into matters established in his self, matters related to his attributes, and matters external to both. As for the first: like the light that used to move in his forefathers until he was born. And his birth circumcised and with his navel cut, placing one of his hands on his eyes and the other on his private parts, and what was of the seal of prophethood between his shoulders, and his height being tall among the tall, and medium among the medium, and seeing from behind as if he sees from in front. As for the second: like his possessing the utmost extreme of truthfulness, trustworthiness, chastity, courage, eloquence, generosity, asceticism, humility towards the poor, compassion for the community, endurance of the hardships of prophethood, adherence to noble ethics, and his reaching the ultimate in divine sciences and knowledge, and establishing religious and worldly benefits, and his being answered in supplication, as he supplicated for Ibn Abbas, may Allah be pleased with him, with his saying: 'O Allah, give him deep understanding in religion,' so he became the imam of the exegetes; and he supplicated against Utbah ibn Abi Lahab with the words: 'O Allah, set upon him a dog from Your dogs,' so a lion devoured him; and against Mudar with his saying: 'O Allah, make Your punishment severe upon Mudar and make upon them years like the years of Joseph,' so Allah withheld rain from them for years; and against one of the disbelievers who pursued him when he came out of the cave, with his saying: 'O earth, take him,' so the legs of his horse sank. As for the third: like the falling of idols prostrating on the night of his birth, and the collapse of the balconies of the palaces of the Chosroes, and the cloud shading him, and the splitting of the moon, and the uprooting of trees, and the stone greeting him, and water gushing from between his fingers until the troops and their animals drank, and a great multitude being satisfied from little food, and the moaning of the palm trunk in the mosque of Medina when he moved from it to the pulpit, and the complaint of the she-camel about its owners, and the testimony of the roasted sheep on the day of Khaybar that it was poisoned, and the flow of milk from the udder of the dry mangy ewe for Umm Ma'bad when he wiped his hand over it, and the wolf addressing Wahb ibn Aws with the words: 'Do you wonder at my taking a sheep, while this Muhammad calls to the truth and you do not respond?', and the glorification of pebbles, and other things that cannot be counted or enumerated.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the Prophet Muhammad's miracles (mu'jizat) into three categories. It begins by listing a wide range of miracles including the light in his forefathers, his birth state, the seal of prophethood, and many others, noting that they are too numerous to count. Then the author states that these miracles are divided into three types: (1) matters established in his self (i.e., physical or inherent miracles in his person), (2) matters related to his attributes (i.e., moral and intellectual excellences), and (3) matters external to both (i.e., external events). For the first type, examples include the transmitted light, his circumcised birth, the seal of prophethood, his balanced stature, and his ability to see from behind. For the second type, examples include his perfect truthfulness, trustworthiness, chastity, courage, eloquence, generosity, asceticism, humility, compassion, patience, knowledge, answered supplications, and specific instances of his supplications being fulfilled, such as for Ibn Abbas and against his enemies. For the third type, examples include cosmic events at his birth, cloud shading, moon splitting, water from fingers, feeding multitudes, the moaning palm trunk, the complaining she-camel, the speaking wolf, and glorifying pebbles. The passage emphasizes that these miracles are either preparatory (irhas) before his prophethood or confirmatory after it. The classification systematically organizes the diverse miraculous events into a coherent theological framework.

Key Terms
الإرهاص (irhas)
التصديق (tasdiq)
النور المنتقل (al-nur al-muntaqil)
خاتم النبوة (khatam al-nubuwwa)
رجوع الشمس (ruju' al-shams)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many people think miracles are only extraordinary external events like splitting the moon or water flowing, overlooking the personal and moral wonders of the Prophet.
Correction: This lesson shows that miracles include his very being (e.g., seal of prophethood) and his perfect attributes (e.g., truthfulness), which are equally miraculous.

Checkpoint
According to the threefold classification, which of the following would belong to the category of miracles related to the Prophet's attributes?
A) The splitting of the moon
B) His truthfulness and trustworthiness
C) The light in his forefathers
D) The speaking wolf

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's miracles are comprehensively classified into three types: those inherent in his person, those related to his attributes, and external events, demonstrating the multi-faceted proof of his prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The source describes the Prophet as possessing 'the utmost extreme of truthfulness, trustworthiness, chastity, courage...' Which of these attributes do you find most lacking in your own character, and what specific action will you take today to begin cultivating it as a proof of your faith?
?
When the wolf addressed Wahb ibn Aws, it said: 'This Muhammad calls to the truth and you do not respond.' Are your daily choices a response to the Prophet's call, or are you like the one being questioned? What will you change starting this moment?
?
The second category of miracles includes the Prophet's answered supplications. If you could ask the Prophet to make one du'a for you, which area of your life would you most need him to pray for? Now, how can you live in a way that makes you worthy of that prayer?

The Prophet's Answered Prayers (Second Miracle)
The author presents the second miracle: the Prophet's answered prayers with specific examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second miracle of the Prophet: his answered prayers and their manifestations.

Core Idea
The Prophet's prayers were answered immediately, confirming his prophethood.

The author details the second miracle, which includes the Prophet's unmatched knowledge and his arranged benefits for religion and world, but focuses on his answered supplications as clear proofs. These prayers show direct divine intervention in response to his words.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the examples of answered supplications.
Notice how each supplication is followed by its outcome.
2
Identify the four supplications
List the four specific prayers mentioned: for Ibn Abbas, against Utbah, against Mudar, and against the pursuer.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why these answered prayers are considered miracles and how they affirm prophethood.
They show that Allah responds directly to the Prophet's words.

And his reaching the utmost in divine sciences and cognitions, and the arranging of religious and worldly benefits, and his being answered in supplication as he prayed for Ibn Abbas, may Allah be pleased with him, saying: 'O Allah, give him deep understanding of the religion,' so he became the Imam of the exegetes. And he prayed against Utbah ibn Abi Lahab saying: 'O Allah, let loose upon him a dog from Your dogs,' and it was torn by the lion. And against Mudar saying: 'O Allah, intensify Your punishment upon Mudar and make upon them years like the years of Joseph,' so Allah withheld rain from them for years. And against those who pursued him from the disbelievers when he came out of the cave saying: 'O earth, take him,' so the legs of his horse sank. Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage lists specific miracles (معجزات) of the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) under two headings: the second and third miracles. The second miracle comprises three aspects: his attainment of ultimate knowledge in divine sciences, his arrangement of religious and worldly benefits, and his answered prayers—exemplified by his supplication for Ibn Abbas (who became a great scholar), against Utbah ibn Abi Lahab (who was killed by a lion), against the tribe of Mudar (who suffered drought), and against a pursuer (whose horse sank into the earth). The third miracle gathers numerous extraordinary occurrences surrounding his life: the prostration of idols at his birth, the fall of Persian palace balconies, clouds shading him, the splitting of the moon, trees uprooting, stones greeting him, water flowing from his fingers, multiplication of food, the palm trunk yearning, she-camels complaining, a roasted ewe testifying to its poison, milk flowing from a dry ewe, a wolf speaking, and pebbles glorifying God. The author concludes that such phenomena are too numerous to count, all confirming his prophethood.

معجزة

Misconception & Correction

Correction: They are direct divine acts confirming prophethood, as the text shows.

?

?

?
If you knew that a single dua from you could change someone's life or bring a famine, how would that change your responsibility in making dua? What one prayer will you make today with full conviction?

Extraordinary Events Surrounding the Prophet (Third Miracle)
The author lists the third miracle: numerous extraordinary events.

Core Idea
Countless supernatural events affirmed the Prophet's prophethood.

Refuting the Objection that Karamat Contradict I'jaz
The author refutes the claim that karamat contradict i'jaz.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the claim that saintly miracles (karamat) contradict prophetic miracles (i'jaz).

Core Idea
The prophet's challenge is only against opponents, not against saints.

In this passage, the author addresses a key objection against the permissibility of saintly miracles. The objection argues that if saints can perform miracles similar to those of prophets, then prophetic miracles lose their evidential force. The author responds by clarifying the precise scope of the prophet's challenge. This distinction is crucial for understanding the relationship between prophetic and saintly miracles.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first sentence beginning with 'فإن قيل' to understand the objection: permitting karamat contradicts the condition of i'jaz because others can produce the like.
The objection is a standard one in kalam.
2
Identify the author's distinction
In the answer, notice the key term 'mu'araḍa' (opposition). The author says the contradiction is only when someone brings the like on purpose to oppose, not as a saintly miracle.
The prophet's claim is directed at 'al-mutahaddin' (those who challenge).
3
Note the exception
The author acknowledges that for some miracles, like the Quran, there is a decisive text that no one can bring its like at all.
This exception does not negate the general principle.
4
Understand the general principle
Despite the exception, the general rule remains: every miracle that occurs for a prophet may also occur as a karamat for a saint, unless a specific text prohibits it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : هذا الجواز مناف للإعجاز ، إذ من شرطه عدم تمكن الغير من الإتيان بالمثل، بل مفض إلى تكذيب النبي، حيث يدعي عند التحدي أنه لا يأتي أحد بمثل ما أتيت به . قلنا : المنافي هو الإتيان بالمثل على سبيل المعارضة . ودعوى النبي أنه لا يأتي بمثل ما أتيت به أحد من المتحدين ، لا أنه لا يظهر مثله كرامة لولي، أو معجزة لنبي آخر . نعم قد يرد في بعض المعجزات نص قاطع على أن احداً لا يأتي بمثله اصلاً كالقرآن ، وهو لا ينافي الحكم بأن كل ما وقع معجزة لنبي يجوز أن يقع كرامة لولي.
For if it is said: This permissibility (jawāz) contradicts the miracle (i‘jāz), since one of its conditions is that others cannot produce the like, rather it leads to accusing the prophet of lying, when he claims in the challenge (taḥaddī) that no one will bring the like of what I have brought. We say: The contradiction is [only] bringing the like by way of opposition (mu‘āraḍa). And the claim of the prophet that no one among those who oppose will bring the like of what I have brought, not that its like does not appear as a saintly miracle (karāma) for a saint (walī), or as a miracle (mu‘jiza) for another prophet. Yes, in some miracles a decisive text may occur that no one can bring its like at all, like the Qur’an, and that does not contradict the ruling that every miracle that occurs for a prophet may occur as a saintly miracle for a saint.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objection states that permitting saintly miracles (karāmāt) contradicts the condition of prophetic miracles (i‘jāz), because one requirement of a prophetic miracle is that no one else can produce its like. If a saint could produce something similar, it would invalidate the miracle and cause the prophet to be accused of lying when he issues a challenge (taḥaddī). The author answers by distinguishing between opposition (mu‘āraḍa) and saintly miracles. The prophet’s claim that no one can produce its like refers specifically to those who challenge and oppose, not to saints who receive honor (karāma) or to other prophets. The prophet says "no one from the opponents (al-mutaḥaddīn) will bring the like." However, the author acknowledges that for some miracles, like the Qur’an, there is a definitive text stating that absolutely no one can produce its like. But this does not negate the general principle that every miracle occurring for a prophet may also occur as a saintly miracle for a saint, except where a specific text prohibits it.

Key Terms
جواز
إعجاز
كرامة
معجزة
معارضة
تحدي
ولي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that a saintly miracle undermines a prophetic miracle, because if a saint can do something similar, the prophet's claim is false.
Correction: The author explains that the prophet's challenge is only against opponents (al-mutahaddin) who intend to disprove his prophethood. Saintly miracles are acts of honor, not opposition, so they do not contradict the miracle.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why does allowing karamat not contradict prophetic miracles (mu'jizat)?
A) Because karamat are not like mu'jizat
B) Because the prophet only claimed that no one from the opponents (al-mutahaddin) could bring its like
C) Because mu'jizat are only for prophets, not saints
D) Because the Qur'an is the only exception

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of saintly miracles does not contradict prophetic miracles because the prophet's challenge is specifically against opponents, not against saints.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness something extraordinary in a believing person's life, do you immediately doubt the uniqueness of the Prophet's miracles, or do you recognize it as a possible karama? How does your heart respond to the idea that God can honor saints without diminishing the Prophet's status?
?
The source teaches that the Prophet's challenge is only for opponents. Have you ever felt the need to 'prove' Islam to someone in a way that resembles opposing the Prophet rather than inviting? Are you more concerned with being right or with drawing people closer to the truth?
?
If you truly believe that no one can produce anything like the Qur'an, how does that shape your relationship with it? Do you treat the Qur'an as an inimitable miracle that demands your submission, or as just another book?

Proof for the Permissibility of Karamat from Divine Power
Sanusi proves the permissibility of karamat via intrinsic possibility and a king analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the permissibility of saintly miracles (karamat) is derived from the intrinsic possibility of extraordinary acts and the comprehensiveness of divine power, illustrated by a royal analogy.

Core Idea
The same extraordinary act can honor a saint as it confirmed a prophet.

This short passage from al-Sanusi's Sharh al-Maqasid presents a concise proof for the permissibility of karamat. Building on the earlier discussion of prophetic miracles, the author argues that extraordinary acts are intrinsically possible and fall within Allah's power. He then uses a powerful analogy to show that the same kind of act can serve different purposes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the use of the king analogy.
2
Identify the two premises
The passage builds on two premises from the earlier discussion of prophetic miracles: intrinsic possibility (imkān) and the comprehensiveness of Allah's power (qudra). Find these in the Arabic.
3
Analyze the analogy
The king analogy illustrates how the same extraordinary act can serve different purposes: authenticating the messenger and honoring a friend. Relate this to Allah granting karamat to saints.
The analogy is not about the essence of God but about logical permissibility in human customs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لنا على الجواز ما مر في المعجزة من إمكان الأمر في نفسه، وشمول قدرة الله تعالى. وذلك كالملك يصدق رسوله ببعض ما ليس من عاداته، ثم يفعل مثل ذلك إكراماً لبعض أوليائه،
We have for the permissibility (jawāz) what has passed in the [discussion] of the miracle (mu‘jiza) regarding the possibility (imkān) of the matter in itself, and the comprehensiveness of the power (qudra) of Allah Most High. That is like a king who authenticates his messenger by some [acts] not from his customs, then does the like of that as an honor (ikrām) for some of his friends (awliyā’).
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the permissibility of saintly miracles (karāmāt). The author summarizes a previous argument from the discussion of prophetic miracles (mu‘jizāt): that such acts are intrinsically possible (imkān fī nafsihi) and fall within Allah’s omnipotence (shumūl qudrat Allāh). He then provides an analogy: a king might support his messenger with extraordinary acts that break normal customs; later, the same king may perform similar acts to honor his close friends (awliyā’). Similarly, Allah may grant extraordinary acts to His saints (awliyā’) as honor, without contradicting the miracles given to His prophets. The point is that the same act can serve different purposes: confirming prophethood for a prophet, and honoring sainthood for a saint.

Key Terms
جواز
كرامة
معجزة
إمكان
قدرة
ولي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The analogy implies God is like a king in nature.
Correction: The analogy is only to show logical permissibility, not a comparison of essence.

Checkpoint
What analogy does the author use to illustrate the permissibility of karamat?
A) A king sending a messenger with unusual acts and later honoring his friends similarly
B) A doctor curing a patient and later treating others
C) A teacher giving a test to a student and then to others
D) A father giving gifts to his children

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of karamat is established by the same intrinsic possibility and divine power that allow prophetic miracles, and the analogy with a king shows that the same kind of act can serve different purposes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you recognize any 'extraordinary' experiences in your life as honors from Allah (karamat), or are you quick to dismiss them as coincidences? How would you know the difference based on this lesson's analogy?
?
The king honors his friends with acts similar to those confirming his messenger. If you claim to be a friend of Allah (wali), what extraordinary acts in your life are you willing to accept as divine honors? What response do they demand from you?
?
The lesson shows that the same act can serve different purposes. In your daily life, how can you transform ordinary actions into acts of honor to Allah, making them a source of spiritual elevation? What specific action will you commit to today to turn a mundane task into an act of worship?
