Evidence from Quranic Narratives for the Occurrence of Karamat
The author cites three Quranic stories as evidence for karamat and replies to the objection that they were prophetic miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses three Quranic stories as evidence for saintly miracles (karamat) and how he refutes the objection that these were prophetic miracles.

Core Idea
Quranic stories show extraordinary acts from righteous individuals not claiming prophethood.

In this lesson, we examine the first evidence for the occurrence of karamat: Quranic narratives. The author cites three well-known stories—Maryam's provision, the Companions of the Cave, and Asif's bringing the throne of Bilqis—and then addresses a key objection that these were actually prophetic miracles. Understanding his response is crucial for distinguishing karamat from miracles of prophets.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Notice the three stories and the objection.
Pay attention to the flow: evidence, objection, reply.
2
Identify the three examples
List the three Quranic stories: Maryam, Companions of the Cave, Asif. For each, consider why it could be seen as a karama.
The author uses these as scriptural proof.
3
Analyze the objection
The objector says these events were irhās or miracles for prophets. Understand why that might seem plausible.
4
Understand the author's reply
The author argues that context (siyāq) shows they were not for confirming prophethood; Zachariah was unaware. Also, the claim is only permissibility, not that they are definitely karamat.
The reply is based on the purpose and lack of prophetic claim.
5
Appreciate the broader defense
The author notes that the objection would apply to many prophetic miracles, so it does not undermine the argument.
This strengthens the case for karamat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى الوقوع وجهان : الأول ـ ما ثبت بالنص من قصة مريم عند ولادة عيسى(عليه السلام)، وأنه كلما دخل عليها زكريا المحراب وجد عندها رزقاً قال : يا مريم أنى لك هذا؟ قالت: هو من عند الله . وقصة أصحاب الكهف، ولبثهم في الكهف سنين بلا طعام وشراب. وقصة آصف وإتيانه بعرش بلقيس (۱) قبل ارتداد الطرف. فإن قيل : كان الأول إرهاصاً لنبوة عيسى، أو معجزة لزكريا، والثاني لمن كان نبياً في زمن أصحاب الكهف، والثالث لسليمان ، قلنا : سياق القصص يدل على أن ذلك لم يكن لقصد تصديقهم في دعوى النبوة، بل لم يكن لزكريا علم بذلك، ولذا سأل ونحن لا ندعي إلا جواز ظهور الخوارق من بعض الصالحين، غير مقرونة بدعوى النبوة، ولا مسوقة لقصد تصديق نبي، ولا يضرنا تسميته إرهاصاً أو معجزة لنبي هو من أمته على أن ما ذكرتم يرد على كثير من معجزات الأنبياء لجواز أن يكون معجزة لنبي آخر.
And regarding occurrence (wuqū‘), there are two aspects: First — what is established by the text from the story of Mary at the birth of Jesus (peace be upon him), and that whenever Zachariah entered the sanctuary (miḥrāb) he found with her provision. He said: O Mary, from where is this to you? She said: It is from Allah. And the story of the Companions of the Cave (Aṣḥāb al-Kahf), and their staying in the cave for years without food and drink. And the story of Aṣif and his bringing the throne of Bilqis before the twinkling of an eye. For if it is said: The first was a preparation (irhāṣ) for the prophethood of Jesus, or a miracle for Zachariah, and the second for a prophet in the time of the Companions of the Cave, and the third for Solomon. We say: The context of the stories indicates that that was not for the purpose of confirming their claim to prophethood; rather Zachariah did not know about it, and therefore he asked. And we only claim the permissibility of the appearance of extraordinary acts (kharq al-‘āda) from some righteous people (ṣāliḥīn), not coupled with a claim of prophethood, and not directed for the purpose of confirming a prophet. And it does not harm us if it is called irhāṣ or a miracle for a prophet from his community. Moreover, what you have mentioned applies to many miracles of the prophets, for it is possible that it be a miracle for another prophet.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage provides the first piece of evidence for the actual occurrence (wuqū‘) of saintly miracles: the Quranic stories. The author lists three examples: (1) Maryam receiving provision in the sanctuary, which was a marvel; (2) the Companions of the Cave surviving for years without food or drink; (3) Asif bringing the throne of Bilqis in an instant. The objection arises that these events were actually prophetic miracles or preparatory signs (irhāṣ) for prophets. For example, Mary's provision was either preparation for Jesus or a miracle for Zachariah; the cave story was a miracle for a prophet of that time; and Asif's act was a miracle for Solomon. The author refutes this: the context shows that these events were not intended to confirm prophethood; Zachariah was unaware and asked about it. The author's claim is only that extraordinary acts can occur from righteous individuals without claiming prophethood. Even if one labels them irhāṣ or a miracle for a prophet from his community, it does not harm the argument. Moreover, the objection would also apply to many prophetic miracles, as they could be seen as miracles for other prophets. Thus, the evidence stands.

Key Terms
كرامة
إرهاص
معجزة
خوارق
آصف
بلقيس
أصحاب الكهف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All extraordinary acts must be prophetic miracles; if a saint performs a marvel, it must actually be a miracle of a prophet.
Correction: The author argues that the context and purpose determine whether it is a karama or a miracle; many Quranic events were not intended to confirm prophethood and thus can be considered karamat.

Checkpoint

A) He argues that those events did not involve prophets
B) He states that the context shows they were not meant to confirm prophethood, and Zachariah was unaware
C) He says that the Quran explicitly calls them karamat
D) He ignores the objection

Key Takeaway
Quranic narratives provide evidence for the occurrence of karamat by showing extraordinary acts from righteous individuals that were not tied to a claim of prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Zachariah saw provision from Allah and asked in wonder. Do you recognize the provision in your life as coming directly from Allah, or do you attribute it to your own efforts? How can you cultivate a heart that sees every blessing as a karama and responds with gratitude, not entitlement?
?
The Companions of the Cave endured years without food and drink for their faith. What is one comfort or convenience you cling to that you would struggle to give up for Allah? Are you willing to ask Him to strengthen your trust so that you could endure hardship without complaint?
?
Asif brought the throne of Bilqis in the blink of an eye by Allah's permission. If Allah can grant such extraordinary ability to a righteous servant, what might He grant you if you were fully sincere? What is the one sin or attachment that you know holds you back from that level of devotion, and what will you do to remove it today?

Mass-Transmitted Reports of Saintly Miracles (Karamat)
The author enumerates mass-transmitted reports of saintly miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument for the occurrence of saintly miracles: the mass transmission of reports from the righteous predecessors.

Core Idea
The saintly miracles of the Companions and saints are as well-attested as prophetic miracles.

The author presents the second evidence for karamat: mass-transmitted reports from the Companions, Followers, and later saints. These accounts are so numerous and widely known that they reach the level of tawatur, even if some details are narrated by single chains. This lesson examines the examples given and the strength of this evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the literal translation. Focus on the examples and the author's claim of tawatur.
2
Identify the examples of karamat
Note the three examples: 'Umar's vision, Khalid's safety from poison, and 'Ali's numerous karamat. Recognize how each demonstrates breaking of natural patterns.
These are well-known accounts in Islamic tradition.
3
Understand the tawatur claim
The author asserts that these reports are mass-transmitted, meaning they are known with certainty. Reflect on why he considers them mutawatir despite some details being ahad.
Tawatur does not require every detail to be mutawatir; the core event is widely reported.
4
Compare with prophetic miracles
Notice the author's statement that karamat almost reach the level of prophetic miracles in their prevalence. This elevates the status of saintly miracles.
5
Prepare for the next lesson
This passage sets up the refutation of deniers. Think about why someone might reject these accounts.
The next lesson will cover the author's response to deniers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني ـ ما تواثر معنا وإن كانت التفاصيل أحاداً من كرامات الصحابة، والتابعين، ومن بعدهم من الصالحين، كرؤية عمر (رضي الله عنه) على المنبر جيشه بنهاوند حتى قال : يا سارية الجبل الجبل . وسمع سارية ذلك) . وكشرب خالد رضي الله تعالى عنه السم من غير أن يضر به. وأما من علي (رضي الله تعالى عنه فأكثر من أن تحصى. وبالجملة وظهور كرامات الأولياء يكاد يلحق بظهور معجزات الأنبياء، وإنكارها ليس بعجيب من أهل البدع والأهواء، إذ لم يشاهدوا ذلك من أنفسهم قط، ولم يسمعوا به من رؤسائهم الذين يزعمون أنهم على شيء مع اجتهادهم في أمور العبادات، واجتناب السيئات، فوقعوا في أولياء الله تعالى أصحاب الكرامات، يمزقون أديمهم، ويمضغون لحومهم، لا يسمونهم إلا باسم الجهلة المتصوفة (۲) ، ولا يعدونهم إلا في عداد آحاد المبتدعة، قاعدين تحت المثل السائر أوسعتهم سباً وأودوا بالإبل، ولم يعرفوا أن مبنى هذا الأمر على صفـاء العقيدة، ونقاء السريرة، واقتفاء الطريقة، واصطفاء الحقيقة، وإنما العجب من بعض فقهاء أهل السنة حيث قال فيما روي عن إبراهيم بن أدهم انهم رأواه بالبصرة يوم التروية، وفي ذلك اليوم بمكة. أن من اعتقد جواز ذلك يكفر . والإنصاف ما ذكره الإمام النسفي (۳) حين سئل عما يحكى أن الكعبة كانت تزور واحداً من الأولياء، هل يجوز القول بـه ؟ فقال : نقض العادة على سبيل الكرامة لأهل الولاية جائز عند أهل السنة
And the second — what has been mass-transmitted (tawātara) among us, even if the details are singular (āḥād), from the saintly miracles of the Companions (ṣaḥāba), the Followers (tābi‘īn), and those after them from the righteous (ṣāliḥīn), like the vision of ‘Umar (radiyallahu ‘anhu) on the pulpit of his army in Nahāwand, until he said: O Sāriya, the mountain, the mountain! And Sāriya heard that. And like the drinking of poison by Khālid (radiyallahu ta‘ālā ‘anhu) without it harming him. And as for ‘Alī (radiyallahu ta‘ālā ‘anhu), more than can be counted. And in summary, the appearance of saintly miracles of the saints (awliyā’) almost reaches the appearance of the miracles of the prophets. And denying them is not surprising from the people of innovation (bid‘a) and desires (ahwā’), since they have never witnessed that from themselves, nor have they heard it from their leaders who claim to be upon something despite their diligence in acts of worship and avoidance of sins. So they fall upon the saints of Allah Most High, the possessors of saintly miracles, tearing their skins and chewing their flesh, not naming them except by the name of the ignorant Sufis (mutasawwifa), and not counting them except among the individuals of the innovators. They sit under the proverbial saying 'I have filled them with insults and destroyed the camels.' They do not know that the foundation of this matter is upon purity of creed (ṣafā’ al-‘aqīda), sincerity of conscience (naqā’ al-sarīra), following the path (iqtifā’ al-ṭarīqa), and choosing the truth (iṣṭifā’ al-ḥaqīqa). And the wonder is from some jurists (fuqahā’) of Ahl al-Sunna who said, regarding what was narrated about Ibrāhīm ibn Adham that they saw him in Basra on the Day of Tarwiya and on that same day in Mecca, that whoever believes in the permissibility of that is a disbeliever (kāfir). The fair judgement (al-inṣāf) is what Imam al-Nasafī (3) [said] when he was asked about what is narrated that the Ka‘ba used to visit one of the saints: is it permissible to say that? He said: Breaking the habit (naqḍ al-‘āda) by way of saintly miracle (karāma) for the people of sainthood (ahl al-wilāya) is permissible (jā’iz) according to Ahl al-Sunna.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage presents the second evidence for the occurrence of karamat: mass-transmitted reports (tawātur) from the Companions, the Followers, and later righteous individuals. The author gives examples: ‘Umar’s vision of his army at Nahāwand and his call to Sāriya, which was heard remotely; Khālid ibn al-Walīd drinking poison without harm; and numerous accounts from ‘Alī. The author asserts that the appearance of saintly miracles is nearly as well-established as prophetic miracles. He then criticizes the deniers (ahl al-bid‘a wa al-ahwā’) who have not experienced or heard of such things from their own leaders. They slander the saints, calling them ignorant Sufis and innovators. The author notes the irony that some of Ahl al-Sunna themselves, like some jurists, have declared such karamat as disbelief—referring to the story of Ibrāhīm ibn Adham being seen in two distant places on the same day. The author then presents the correct view, quoting Imam al-Nasafī: breaking customary patterns as a saintly miracle for the people of sainthood is permissible according to Ahl al-Sunna. This establishes the Sunni position on the acceptance of karamat.

Key Terms
تواثر
كرامة
ولي
الصحابة
التابعون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) 'Umar calling to Sariya
B) Khalid drinking poison
C) 'Ali's many karamat
D) Prophet Muhammad's split of the moon

Key Takeaway
The second argument for saintly miracles is the mass transmission of reports from the Companions and early saints, which establishes their credibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read about 'Umar's call to Sariya being heard over a long distance, do you believe it happened exactly as reported? If your answer is 'no' or 'maybe', what does that reveal about your trust in the mass-transmitted reports of Islamic history? How can you strengthen that trust?
?
The author claims that saintly miracles are nearly as certain as prophetic miracles. Can you think of a time when you doubted a story about a saint's miracle? What would it take for you to accept it with the same certainty you have for the Quran? Commit today to studying one such report deeply to resolve any doubts.
?
If the miracles of the Companions and saints are real and well-attested, how should that affect your daily relationship with God? Are you living as if such miracles are possible in your own time? What would change if you truly believed that God can manifest extraordinary help to His friends?

Refuting Deniers of Saintly Miracles and the Sunni Position
The author refutes deniers of karamat and presents Imam al-Nasafi's ruling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's critique of those who deny karamat and the correct Sunni position as articulated by Imam al-Nasafi.

Core Idea
Denying saintly miracles stems from ignorance; the Sunni position accepts them as permissible.

After presenting the evidence for karamat, the author now turns to a scathing critique of their deniers. He identifies them as people of innovation (bid'a) and desires (ahwa), who have never experienced such wonders themselves. He also addresses an internal controversy within Ahl al-Sunna, where some jurists declared certain karamat as disbelief. The correct view is provided by Imam al-Nasafi, who affirms the permissibility of karamat as a breaking of the natural habit.

Guided Path
1
Read the second half of the passage
Read from 'وإنكارها ليس بعجيب' to the end. Focus on the criticism of deniers and the story of Ibrahim ibn Adham.
2
Analyze the criticism of deniers
Notice the strong language: 'tearing their skins and chewing their flesh.' Understand why the author says they are not surprising—they lack personal experience and follow their leaders.
The deniers are described as people of bid'a and ahwa.
3
Examine the internal Ahl al-Sunna controversy
Some jurists of Ahl al-Sunna rejected the story of Ibrahim ibn Adham being in two places at once. They declared belief in it as disbelief. This is striking because they are from the same school.
This shows that even within Ahl al-Sunna, there can be disagreements on details of karamat.
4
Learn Imam al-Nasafi's ruling
When asked about the Ka'ba visiting a saint, Nasafi ruled that breaking the habit (naqd al-'ada) as a saintly miracle is permissible. This is the balanced Sunni position.
The key term is 'naqd al-'ada' and it is specifically for ahl al-wilaya.
5
Reflect on the foundation of karamat
The author ends by stating that karamat are built on purity of creed, sincerity, following the path, and choosing the truth. This emphasizes that karamat are not random but result from spiritual excellence.

And the second — what has been mass-transmitted (tawātara) among us, even if the details are singular (āḥād), from the saintly miracles of the Companions (ṣaḥāba), the Followers (tābi‘īn), and those after them from the righteous (ṣāliḥīn), like the vision of ‘Umar (radiyallahu ‘anhu) on the pulpit of his army in Nahāwand, until he said: O Sāriya, the mountain, the mountain! And in summary, the appearance of saintly miracles of the saints (awliyā’) almost reaches the appearance of the miracles of the prophets. And denying them is not surprising from the people of innovation (bid‘a) and desires (ahwā’), since they have never witnessed that from themselves, nor have they heard it from their leaders who claim to be upon something despite their diligence in acts of worship and avoidance of sins. So they fall upon the saints of Allah Most High, the possessors of saintly miracles, tearing their skins and chewing their flesh, not naming them except by the name of the ignorant Sufis (mutasawwifa), and not counting them except among the individuals of the innovators. They sit under the proverbial saying 'I have filled them with insults and destroyed the camels.' They do not know that the foundation of this matter is upon purity of creed (ṣafā’ al-‘aqīda), sincerity of conscience (naqā’ al-sarīra), following the path (iqtifā’ al-ṭarīqa), and choosing the truth (iṣṭifā’ al-ḥaqīqa). And the wonder is from some jurists (fuqahā’) of Ahl al-Sunna who said, regarding what was narrated about Ibrāhīm ibn Adham that they saw him in Basra on the Day of Tarwiya and on that same day in Mecca, that whoever believes in the permissibility of that is a disbeliever (kāfir). The fair judgement (al-inṣāf) is what Imam al-Nasafī (3) [said] when he was asked about what is narrated that the Ka‘ba used to visit one of the saints: is it permissible to say that? He said: Breaking the habit (naqḍ al-‘āda) by way of saintly miracle (karāma) for the people of sainthood (ahl al-wilāya) is permissible (jā’iz) according to Ahl al-Sunna.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage presents the second evidence for the occurrence of karamat: mass-transmitted reports (tawātur) from the Companions, the Followers, and later righteous individuals. The author gives examples: ‘Umar’s vision of his army at Nahāwand and his call to Sāriya, which was heard remotely; Khālid ibn al-Walīd drinking poison without harm; and numerous accounts from ‘Alī. The author asserts that the appearance of saintly miracles is nearly as well-established as prophetic miracles. He then criticizes the deniers (ahl al-bid‘a wa al-ahwā’) who have not experienced or heard of such things from their own leaders. They slander the saints, calling them ignorant Sufis and innovators. The author notes the irony that some of Ahl al-Sunna themselves, like some jurists, have declared such karamat as disbelief—referring to the story of Ibrāhīm ibn Adham being seen in two distant places on the same day. The author then presents the correct view, quoting Imam al-Nasafī: breaking customary patterns as a saintly miracle for the people of sainthood is permissible according to Ahl al-Sunna. This establishes the Sunni position on the acceptance of karamat.

The Sunni position, as articulated by Imam al-Nasafi, affirms the permissibility of saintly miracles as a breaking of natural habits.

Karamat al-Awliya: Evidence, Denial, and al-Nasafi's Ruling
Explains the reality of karamat, denial by innovators, and al-Nasafi's ruling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the reality of karamat (miracles of saints) as affirmed by Ahl al-Sunna, the reasons why innovators deny them, and the correct ruling regarding extreme karamat according to Imam al-Nasafi.

Core Idea
Karamat are real, almost like prophetic miracles, but their denial stems from ignorance, not scholarship.

This lesson opens the discussion on karamat al-awliya with concrete examples from the Companions. The author then explains why innovators reject these wonders and criticizes their behavior. Finally, he addresses a surprising takfir ruling by some jurists and presents the balanced view of Imam al-Nasafi, which affirms the possibility of karamat without breaking fundamental beliefs.

Guided Path
1
Read the examples of karamat
Identify each karamah mentioned: ‘Umar’s distant vision, Khalid’s immunity to poison, and the numerous karamat of ‘Ali. Notice that these are from the early generations.
These examples establish a precedent for the type of events considered karamat.
2
Analyze the denial of innovators
The author explains that denial is not surprising because innovators lack personal experience and rely on leaders who reject karamat. Note the harsh language used against awliya.
The author compares them to those who 'tear skin and chew flesh'.
3
Examine the surprising ruling of some jurists
Read about the takfir ruling on the story of Ibrahim ibn Adham. Understand why the author finds it contrary to the spirit of Ahl al-Sunna.
Then learn al-Nasafi’s corrective ruling.
4
Reflect on al-Nasafi’s principle
Al-Nasafi states that breaking custom by way of karamah is permissible. Consider how this fits into the broader Aqeedah framework.
The lesson ends with the beginning of the opponent's objections.

Commentary

The author presents al-Nasafi’s ruling that breaking custom by way of karamah for the awliya’ is permissible for Ahl al-Sunna, and then introduces that the opponent has several objections, which will be listed next.

First Objection: Confusion with Prophethood
Presents first objection that karamat blur prophet distinction and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection against karamat of the awliya' and how it is refuted by distinguishing miracles from karamat.

Core Idea
The distinction between mu'jiza and karamah protects the uniqueness of prophethood.

The opponents of karamat raise a serious concern: if saints can perform extraordinary acts, how can we distinguish true prophets? This lesson examines that objection and its refutation, which relies on the key difference between a miracle (mu'jiza) and a karamah. As you read, notice how the refutation is concise yet decisive.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the concise style—objection and refutation are packed into two sentences.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the objection and its premise
The objection states that if extraordinary events appear from a wali, a prophet could be confused with others. The premise is that the differentiator is the miracle.
The objection is called 'al-‘umdah' (the main one).
3
Understand the refutation
The refutation refers back to an earlier distinction between mu'jiza (miracle) and karamah. Mu'jizat are exclusive to prophets and linked to the claim of prophethood; karamat are not. Thus no confusion arises.
The distinction is not repeated here, so recall the prior lesson.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this objection is considered 'the main one'. Then appreciate how a clear distinction resolves it without needing extensive argument.
This shows the power of precise definitions in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول - وهو العمدة، أنه لو ظهرت الخوارق من الولي لالتبس النبي بغيره، إذ الفارق هو المعجزة . ورد بما مر من الفرق بين المعجزة والكرامة .
The first - and it is the main one - that if extraordinary events appeared from the wali, the prophet would be confused with others, because the differentiator is the miracle. It is refuted by what has passed regarding the difference between the miracle (mu'jiza) and the karamah.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection of the opponents of karamat. The objection is that if awliya’ can perform extraordinary events (khawariq), then prophets would not be distinguishable, since the differentiator is the miracle. The refutation is brief: it refers back to the earlier distinction between mu'jizat (miracles) and karamat (extraordinary acts of saints). That distinction, not repeated here, explains that mu'jizat are accompanied by the claim of prophethood and are for proving that claim, while karamat are not accompanied by such a claim and thus do not cause confusion.

Key Terms
الخوارق
الولي
المعجزة
الكرامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any extraordinary event from a saint would make them indistinguishable from a prophet.
Correction: Only miracles (mu'jizat) are accompanied by the claim of prophethood and divine challenge; karamat are not, so no confusion.

Checkpoint
What is the first objection against karamat of the awliya'?
A) They are impossible by nature.
B) They would make it hard to distinguish prophets from saints.
C) They are not mentioned in the Quran.
D) They only occurred among the Companions.

Key Takeaway
Karamat do not undermine prophethood because they lack the essential sign of a divine mission: the claim of prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Second Objection: Multiplicity Would Diminish Extraordinariness
Presents the second objection and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection against karamat and its refutation, which shows that frequency does not negate the extraordinary nature of God's intervention.

Core Idea
Miracles remain extraordinary even if they happen frequently.

The second objection claims that if karamat occur often due to the many awliya', they would lose their extraordinary character. However, this overlooks the fact that 'extraordinary' means a break from natural custom, not rarity. Let's read the passage.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic Passage
Read the Arabic text once to get the rhythm. Notice it has two parts: the objection and the refutation.
The Arabic is concise; each phrase carries weight.
2
Identify the Objection
The objector argues that many karamat would cease to be khariqah li’l-‘adah (extraordinary). Ask: what is the implied definition of extraordinary here?
The objector equates extraordinary with rare.
3
Understand the Refutation
The author refutes by denial (man‘) and states that the utmost (ghayah) is the continuation of breaking custom (naghs al-‘adah). What does this mean?
Extraordinariness is defined by violation of natural law, not by scarcity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني - أنها لو ظهرت لكثرت كثرة الأولياء ، وخرجت عن كونها خارقة للعادة حقاً. ورد بالمنع ، بل عايته استمرار نقص العادة.
The second - that if they appeared, due to the multitude of the awliya', they would cease to be truly extraordinary. It is refuted by denial; rather, the utmost is the continuation of the breaking of custom.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
The second objection argues that if karamat appeared frequently because of the large number of saints, they would no longer be truly extraordinary (khariqah li’l-‘adah). The refutation is a simple denial (man‘) of the premise: even if they are many, they still involve the breaking of natural custom, and that custom-breaking can persist. The point is that being extraordinary does not require rarity; it only requires that the event violates the usual course of nature.

Key Terms
خارقة للعادة
نقص العادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Frequent miracles are no longer special.
Correction: Miracles are defined by being a break from natural law, not by infrequency.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the second objection that many karamat would cease to be extraordinary?
A) By accepting that they would become ordinary.
B) By denying that they would lose their extraordinary nature.
C) By stating that miracles are limited to prophets.
D) By citing a Quranic verse.

Key Takeaway
The extraordinary nature of a miracle lies in its violation of natural custom, not in its rarity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

After learning that extraordinariness is defined by the violation of custom rather than rarity, will you now commit to seeing each blessing as a distinct, direct act of Allah's power?
?

What does your reaction say about your belief in Allah's unlimited ability to break His own customs at any moment, for anyone He wills? Will you ask Allah to grant you the certainty that nothing is ordinary when it comes from Him?

Third Objection: Karamat and the Closure of Prophethood Proof
Third objection and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection against karamat as proof of prophethood and its refutation based on the context of a prophetic claim.

Core Idea
Context of claim transforms miracle into definitive proof.

The third objection raises a critical concern: if miracles can occur without confirming a prophetic claim, then how can we ever be sure that a prophet's miracle is truly a sign of prophethood? This passage presents the objection and its decisive refutation, reinforcing the key principle that the timing and context of a miracle matter.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through, then the translation. Note the logical structure: 'if... then...'.
The objection is concise but carries a powerful implication.
2
Identify the logical concern
The objection argues that if karamat can occur without the purpose of confirming a claim (tasdiq), then any miracle of a prophet could also be for another purpose, thus making it impossible to prove prophethood through miracles.
This is a potential collapse of the entire proof system.
3
Understand the refutation
The refutation states that when a miracle accompanies a claim of prophethood (muqāranat al-da‘wā), it gives definitive confirmation. This is because the context of the claim removes ambiguity.
The refutation relies on a principle already established earlier in the discussion.
4
Connect to the broader argument
Realize that this refutation preserves the distinction between karamat (which lack a prophetic claim) and mu‘jizat (which accompany a claim). The objection fails because it ignores the crucial role of context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث - لو ظهرت لا لغرض التصديق لانسد باب إثبات النبوة بالمعجزة لجواز أن يكون ما يظهر من النبي لغرض آخر غير التصديق . ورد بما مر من أنها عند مقارنة الدعوى تفيد التصديق قطعاً.
The third - if they appeared without the purpose of confirmation, the door of proving prophethood by miracle would be closed, because it would be possible that what appears from the prophet is for some other purpose than confirmation. It is refuted by what has passed, that when (the miracle) accompanies the claim, it definitively gives confirmation.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
The third objection argues that if karamat can occur without the purpose of confirming a claim (tasdiq), then miracles of prophets could also be for some other purpose, thus closing the door to proving prophethood. The refutation references an earlier point: when a miracle (mu'jiza) is performed in conjunction with a claim to prophethood, it definitively (qat‘an) serves as confirmation, regardless of other possible purposes. Thus the possibility of other purposes does not undermine the evidentiary value of prophetic miracles.

Key Terms
التصديق
إثبات النبوة
مقارنة الدعوى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that any miraculous act, whether from a saint or a prophet, could be for a non-prophetic purpose, making it impossible to prove prophethood.
Correction: The refutation shows that when a miracle accompanies a prophetic claim, its purpose is definitively confirmation, because the claim provides the context.

Checkpoint
What is the refutation of the third objection?
A) It is impossible for a prophet to perform a miracle for another purpose.
B) When a miracle accompanies the claim of prophethood, it gives definitive confirmation.
C) Karamat are never for confirmation.
D) Prophethood can be proven by other means.

Key Takeaway
The value of a miracle as proof of prophethood depends on its conjunction with a prophetic claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness something extraordinary in your life, do you reflexively assume it confirms your own spiritual status, or do you pause to ask: what claim does this event accompany? This lesson teaches that context transforms an event into proof. Where have you misread a blessing as a sign of your own favor, without linking it to the prophetic message? Today, commit to examining every blessing as a reminder of Allah's lordship and your servitude, not as evidence of your personal virtue.
?
The refutation hinges on the conjunction of miracle and claim. In your own life, what personal 'claims' have you made—about your identity, your knowledge, or your piety? Have you sought extraordinary signs to back them up, or have you anchored your certainty in the prophetic miracle of the Qur'an? This lesson reminds you that only prophetic claims are verified by miracles. Your status is not confirmed by personal wonders, but by following the one who brought the final claim. Will you let go of seeking validation through experiences and instead submit to the definitive proof of prophethood? Make a concrete plan today to study the prophetic proof so that your faith rests on firm ground.
?
The objection in this lesson suggests that if miracles can happen without a purpose, then any miracle could be an imposter. This doubt applies to your heart: are you treating Allah's favors (karamat) as if they confirm your own correctness without tying them to a prophetic claim? Have you ever felt that a answered prayer proved you were 'on the right path' in a way that bypassed the guidance of the prophets? Ask yourself: Am I using divine gifts as an excuse to follow my own desires, or as an invitation to deepen my commitment to the message? Resolve today to always return to the prophetic teaching as the measure of truth, not your personal experiences.

Does Sharing Miracles Diminish Prophets?
Al-Sanusi presents the fourth objection and its refutation regarding karamat of awliya'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth objection against the possibility of karamat for awliya' and its refutation, which argues that such phenomena actually elevate the prophets' status.

Core Idea
When followers gain miracles through prophets, it magnifies the prophets' honor, not diminishes it.

The fourth objection to the permissibility of karamat for awliya' is that it would lower the prophets' standing in people's hearts. If anyone can perform extraordinary acts, what makes prophets special? The refutation turns this on its head: the prophets' followers attaining such ranks through their guidance only increases respect for the prophets. Let's examine this exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part of the passage presenting the claim that sharing khawariq diminishes prophets' status. Understand the concern.
Note the wording: 'تخل بعظم قدر الأنبياء'.
2
Read the refutation
Read the refutation beginning with 'ورد بالمنع'. Notice the shift from denial to positive reasoning.
The refutation uses 'بل' to indicate a stronger claim.
3
Reflect on the logic
Ask yourself: How does followers attaining karamat increase the prophets' majesty? The reasoning hinges on the causal link: followers achieve this through the prophets' law and path.
This ties into the concept of barakah (blessing).
4
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to see if you grasped the refutation's stance.
The answer should align with 'increase' not 'diminish'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع - أن مشاركة الأولياء للأنبياء في ظهور الخوارق تخل بعظم قدر الأنبياء ووقوعهم في النفوس ( ورد بالمنع ، بل يزيد في جلالة أقدارهم والرغبة في اتباعهم، حيث نالت أممهم وأتباعهم مثل هذه الدرجة ببركة الاقتداء بشريعتهم، والاستقامة على طريقتهم .
The fourth - that the sharing of the awliya' with the prophets in the appearance of extraordinary events detracts from the great status of the prophets and their standing in souls. (It is refuted by denial; rather, it increases the majesty of their ranks and the desire to follow them, since their nations and followers attained such a degree through the blessing of following their law (shari‘ah) and being upright upon their path.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
The fourth objection claims that if saints share in performing extraordinary events, that would diminish the greatness of prophets in people's hearts. The refutation denies this (man‘) and instead asserts that it increases the prophets' majesty and people's desire to follow them. The reason is that when followers attain such high status through obedience to the prophets’ law and path, it reflects the prophets’ own greatness. Thus the karamat of followers actually honor the prophets.

Key Terms
مشاركة الأولياء
عظم قدر الأنبياء
الرغبة في اتباعهم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that allowing saints to have miraculous acts lowers the prophets' status.
Correction: The refutation shows it elevates their status because followers attain these acts through the prophets' guidance.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation of the fourth objection, how do the karamat of awliya' affect the status of prophets?
A) They diminish it.
B) They have no effect.
C) They increase the prophets' majesty and desire to follow them.
D) They confuse people about who is a prophet.

Key Takeaway
The fourth objection fails because the karamat of awliya' actually honor the prophets by showing the power of their guidance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly honor the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) by actively following his sunnah in your daily life, or do you merely claim love for him while neglecting his teachings? What one aspect of his guidance are you most lax about, and how can you commit to change starting today?
?
The more closely you follow the prophetic path, the more you become a vessel for divine blessing. Are you satisfied with being a casual follower, or do you yearn to attain the rank of a true wali by intensifying your adherence to the shari'ah? What concrete step will you take this week to be more 'upright upon his path'?
?
If the karamat of the awliya' are a direct result of their following the Prophet, then your spiritual progress is directly tied to your connection with him. How strong is that connection right now? If you were to examine your heart, do you feel a greater attachment to the Prophet or to worldly distractions? What must change to realign your priorities?

Fifth Objection: Knowledge of the Unseen Reserved for Prophets
Refutation of the objection that only prophets know the unseen.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quranic verse about the unseen is interpreted to allow saints knowledge of hidden things through karamat.

Core Idea
The verse's 'unseen' is specific to the Hour, not all hidden knowledge.

The fifth objection claims that only prophets can know the unseen, based on a Quranic verse. The author refutes this by contextualizing the verse and distinguishing divine disclosure from conjecture. This lesson unpacks that refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style.
2
Identify the objection and its basis
The objector uses a Quranic verse to argue that only prophets have knowledge of the unseen. Find the verse and the objector's reasoning.
3
Understand the author's answer
The author responds by limiting the scope of 'unseen' to the Hour and clarifying that the verse does not deny other hidden knowledge to saints. Note the distinction from soothsayers and dream interpreters.
The context of the verse (Surat al-Jinn) is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس - وهو في الإخبار عن المغيبات قوله تعالى عالم الغيب فلا يظهر على غيبه أحداً إلا من ارتضى من رسول (۲) . خص الرسل من بين المرتضين بالاطلاع على الغيب ، فلا يطلع غيرهم، وإن كانوا أولياء مرتضين . فما يشاهد من الكهنة إلقاء الجن والشياطين، ومن أصحاب التعبير والنجوم ظنون واستدلالات ربما تقع وربما لا تقع، ليس من اطلاع الله تعالى في شيء. والجواب أن الغيب ههنا ليس للعموم، بل مطلق أو معين هو وقت وقوع القيمة بقرينة السياق ولا يبعد ان يطلع عليه بعض الرسل من الملائكة أو البشر، فيصح الاستثناء، وإن جعل منقطعاً فلا خفاء، بل لا امتناع حينئذ في جعل الغيب للعموم
(۱) النفس هي الجوهر البخاري اللطيف، الحامل لقوة الحياة والحس والحركة الإرادية، وسماها الحكيم: الروح الحيوانية : فهي جوهر مشرق للبدن وعند الموت ينقطع ضوؤه عن ظاهر البدن وباطنه، وأما في وقت النوم فينقطع عن ظاهر البدن دون باطنه فثبت أن النوم والموت من جنس واحد لأن الموت هو الانقطاع الكلي والنوم هو الانقطاع الناقص فثبت أن القادر الحكيم دبر تعلق جوهر النفس بالبدن على ثلاثة أضرب الأول : إذا بلغ ضوء النفس إلى جميع أجزاء البدن ظاهره وباطنه فهو اليقظة ، وإن انقطع ضوؤها عن ظاهره دون باطنه فهو النوم، أو بالكلية فهو الموت .
(۲) سورة الجن آية رقم ٢٦ ، ٢٧ .
The fifth - and it concerns informing of hidden things: Allah's saying: 'Knower of the unseen, and He does not disclose His unseen to anyone, except to whom He has approved of messengers' (Quran 72:26-27). He singled out messengers from among the approved ones for access to the unseen, so no one else is informed, even if they are approved awliya'. What is observed from soothsayers (by) the casting of jinn and devils, and from interpreters of dreams and astrologers (are) conjectures and inferences that sometimes occur and sometimes do not, are not from the disclosure of Allah Almighty. The answer: that the unseen here is not universal, but absolute or specific—it is the time of the occurrence of the Hour by context of the passage, and it is not unlikely that some messengers, whether angels or humans, may be informed of it; thus the exception is valid. And if it is considered disjoined (munqati‘), there is no concealment; rather, there is no impossibility then in making the unseen universal.
(1) The soul (nafs) is the subtle vaporous substance, the carrier of the power of life, sensation, and voluntary movement; the philosopher (hakim) calls it the animal spirit. It is a substance that illumines the body, and at death its light cuts off from the apparent and inner parts of the body. During sleep it cuts off from the apparent but not the inner, so it is established that sleep and death are of one kind, because death is complete cut-off and sleep is incomplete cut-off. Thus it is established that the Powerful Wise One arranged the attachment of the soul's substance to the body in three ways: first, if the light of the soul reaches all parts of the body, apparent and inner, it is wakefulness; if its light cuts off from the apparent but not the inner, it is sleep; if completely, it is death.
(2) Surah al-Jinn, verses 26-27.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth objection, which is based on the Quranic verse from Surah al-Jinn that Allah does not disclose the unseen except to whomever He approves of messengers. The objector argues that this limits knowledge of the unseen exclusively to prophets, so saints cannot have such knowledge. The author then distinguishes between divine disclosure and the conjectures of soothsayers, dream interpreters, and astrologers, which are not from God. The answer explains that the word 'unseen' (ghayb) in this verse is not general; it is either absolute or specific to the time of the Hour, as indicated by the context. It is not impossible that some messengers (angels or humans) are informed of that particular unseen. The exception is valid; even if the exception is considered disjoined (munqati‘), there is no problem in making 'unseen' universal, because the verse does not preclude saints from knowing other hidden things through karamah. The passage then includes a long footnote defining the soul (nafs) in philosophical terms, explaining its nature and its connection to the body in wakefulness, sleep, and death. The footnote seems to be an editorial addition explaining the term 'النفوس' in the fourth objection, but it is placed here. The author likely added this footnote to clarify the nature of the soul in relation to sleep and karamat.

Key Terms
الغيب
الرسل
المرتضين
الكهنة
أصحاب التعبير
النجوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse forbids any knowledge of the unseen to non-prophets.
Correction: The verse refers specifically to the Hour, so it does not deny saints knowledge of other hidden things through karamah.

Checkpoint
How does the author answer the fifth objection based on the verse 'He does not disclose His unseen except to messengers'?
A) By arguing that saints are also messengers.
B) By saying that the verse is abrogated.
C) By stating that the word 'unseen' here refers specifically to the Hour, not all hidden things.
D) By denying the verse's authenticity.

Key Takeaway
The word 'unseen' in the verse is contextually restricted to the Hour, allowing saints to have knowledge of other hidden matters through divine favor.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the verse 'Knower of the unseen... except to messengers,' did your heart immediately assume that saints cannot know any hidden matter? What in your past education or culture led you to that interpretation, and are you willing to revise it based on this lesson?
?
The author distinguishes true divine disclosure from the conjectures of soothsayers and astrologers. Are there any practices in your life—like relying on horoscopes or ‘gut feelings’—that you treat as if they are from Allah, but which may actually be mere conjectures? Will you examine and abandon them today?
?
If Allah can grant knowledge of the unseen to His saints as a favor, why have you not sought such closeness to Him through sincere obedience and love? What is holding you back from being among the 'approved ones' (al-murtadīn) who might be honored with such divine disclosure?

The Soul: Substance and Three States
Definition of the soul and its three states of connection to the body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the soul as a subtle substance and its three states in relation to the body.

Core Idea
The soul's light connects to the body in wakefulness, sleep, and death in distinct ways.

This passage provides a philosophical definition of the soul (nafs) as a subtle vaporous substance that carries life, sensation, and movement. It explains how the soul's attachment to the body changes during wakefulness, sleep, and death. This lesson unpacks that explanation.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of the soul
The passage defines the soul as a subtle vaporous substance (جوهر بخاري لطيف) that is the carrier of life, sensation, and voluntary movement. Note the alternative term 'animal spirit' used by philosophers.
2
Understand the analogy of light
The soul's connection to the body is likened to light illuminating the body. Three states are distinguished based on how much of the body the light reaches.
Wakefulness: full light; Sleep: light cut off from outer parts; Death: light cut off completely.
3
Reflect on the purpose
The author uses this explanation to show that sleep and death are of one kind, differing in degree, which relates to the earlier discussion of karamat and the soul's experiences.
This connects to the broader theme of the unseen and miracles.

Second Objection: Frequency of Saints' Miracles and Its Refutation
Objection and refutation regarding frequency of saints' miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the second objection against saints' miracles and its refutation, and why frequency does not undermine miraculous nature.

Core Idea
A miracle's wonder lies in the breach of custom, not its rarity.

Sanusi presents the second challenge to the possibility of saints' miracles: if they were real, they'd be too common to be extraordinary. The refutation is subtle and hinges on what 'extraordinary' really means. Let's unpack this concise argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the brevity: objection and refutation are stated in a few lines.
2
Identify the objection and its premise
The objection argues: if saints' miracles appeared, they would be frequent due to many saints, so they wouldn't be truly extraordinary. Notice the implicit premise that 'extraordinary' means rare.
3
Understand the refutation
The refutation simply denies (al-man‘) and then clarifies: the point is not frequency but the continued breach of custom in each instance. Even if common, each event still breaks the natural pattern.
The key term is 'naks al-‘āda' (breach of custom).
4
Reflect on the theological significance
Why does this matter? It protects the category of saints' miracles from being dismissed as non-miraculous. The miracle's authenticity is tied to the event itself, not its statistical rarity.
This connects to the broader debate on karāmāt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني - أنها لو ظهرت لكثرت كثرة الأولياء ، وخرجت عن كونها خارقة للعادة حقاً. ورد بالمنع ، بل غايته استمرار نقص العادة.
Second: That if they appeared, they would be numerous due to the abundance of saints, and would cease to be truly miracles breaking the normal course of events. It is refuted by denial; rather, its ultimate outcome is the continuation of the breach of custom.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection against the possibility of saints' miracles (karāmāt) and its refutation. The objection argues that if such miracles were to appear, they would become numerous due to the large number of saints, and thus would no longer be truly extraordinary (khāriq li-l-‘āda). The refutation denies this, stating that the point is not the frequency of occurrence but rather the continued breach of the normal course of events in each instance. Even if they occur often, each individual event still breaks custom, so the miraculous nature is preserved.

Key Terms
كرامة
خارقة للعادة
أولياء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A frequent miracle cannot be a true miracle because it loses its extraordinary nature.
Correction: The miraculous quality lies in breaking the natural custom each time, not in rarity. Frequency does not negate the breach.

Checkpoint
What is the second objection against saints' miracles according to this passage?
A) Saints cannot perform miracles because they are not prophets.
B) Miracles of saints would be so numerous that they would no longer be extraordinary.
C) Saints' miracles are merely illusions.
D) Saints' miracles are not supported by revelation.

Key Takeaway
The second objection fails because miracles are defined by breaking custom, not by being rare.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a repeated blessing in your life—provision, health, safety. Have you come to see it as ordinary? If each instance is still a breach of custom from Allah, what would it mean to treat tonight's meal as a fresh miracle?
?
The objection assumed that frequency diminishes wonder. Where in your own heart have you become numb to Allah's repeated acts of mercy? What one step can you take tonight to see them as fresh breaks in the pattern of what you deserve?
?
If the value of a miracle lies in its breaking of custom, not its rarity, then even your most common blessings are extraordinary. How does this change the way you will thank Allah tonight for something you usually take for granted?
